🗄️ Sample Database Schema

About This Schema

Throughout this documentation, we use a comprehensive E-Commerce Business Database to demonstrate PostgreSQL concepts. This schema includes customers, products, orders, employees, and related entities commonly found in real-world applications.

🚀 Quick Start - Create This Database

1️⃣
Create Database CREATE DATABASE ecommerce_db;
2️⃣
Connect to Database \c ecommerce_db
3️⃣
Create Tables Run all 10 CREATE TABLE statements below (in order)
4️⃣
Add Indexes (Optional) See "Recommended Indexes" section below
✅
Ready to Use! Insert sample data and start querying

Database Tables Overview

👥

Customers

Customer information, contact details, registration dates

📦

Products

Product catalog with categories, prices, stock levels

🛒

Orders

Order records with status, dates, totals

📋

Order Items

Individual line items for each order

🏷️

Categories

Product categorization hierarchy

👔

Employees

Employee records, departments, salaries

🏢

Departments

Organizational departments

⭐

Reviews

Product reviews and ratings

🚚

Shipments

Shipping and delivery tracking

💳

Payments

Payment transactions and methods

Complete Table Definitions

1. customers

CREATE TABLE customers (
    customer_id SERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    first_name VARCHAR(50) NOT NULL,
    last_name VARCHAR(50) NOT NULL,
    email VARCHAR(100) UNIQUE NOT NULL,
    phone VARCHAR(20),
    address TEXT,
    city VARCHAR(50),
    state VARCHAR(50),
    country VARCHAR(50) DEFAULT 'USA',
    postal_code VARCHAR(20),
    registration_date TIMESTAMP DEFAULT CURRENT_TIMESTAMP,
    last_login TIMESTAMP,
    is_active BOOLEAN DEFAULT true,
    loyalty_points INTEGER DEFAULT 0,
    CONSTRAINT email_format CHECK (email ~* '^[A-Za-z0-9._%+-]+@[A-Za-z0-9.-]+\.[A-Za-z]{2,}$')
);
Purpose: Stores customer information for the e-commerce platform
Primary Key: customer_id (auto-incrementing)
Constraints: Unique email, email format validation, active by default

2. categories

CREATE TABLE categories (
    category_id SERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    category_name VARCHAR(100) NOT NULL UNIQUE,
    description TEXT,
    parent_category_id INTEGER,
    created_at TIMESTAMP DEFAULT CURRENT_TIMESTAMP,
    FOREIGN KEY (parent_category_id) REFERENCES categories(category_id) ON DELETE SET NULL
);
Purpose: Product categorization with hierarchical structure
Primary Key: category_id
Self-Referencing: parent_category_id for category hierarchy

3. products

CREATE TABLE products (
    product_id SERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    product_name VARCHAR(200) NOT NULL,
    description TEXT,
    category_id INTEGER NOT NULL,
    price NUMERIC(10, 2) NOT NULL CHECK (price >= 0),
    cost_price NUMERIC(10, 2) CHECK (cost_price >= 0),
    stock_quantity INTEGER DEFAULT 0 CHECK (stock_quantity >= 0),
    reorder_level INTEGER DEFAULT 10,
    sku VARCHAR(50) UNIQUE,
    barcode VARCHAR(100),
    weight_kg NUMERIC(8, 3),
    dimensions VARCHAR(50),
    manufacturer VARCHAR(100),
    is_active BOOLEAN DEFAULT true,
    created_at TIMESTAMP DEFAULT CURRENT_TIMESTAMP,
    updated_at TIMESTAMP DEFAULT CURRENT_TIMESTAMP,
    FOREIGN KEY (category_id) REFERENCES categories(category_id) ON DELETE RESTRICT
);
Purpose: Product catalog with pricing, inventory, and details
Primary Key: product_id
Foreign Keys: category_id → categories
Constraints: Price >= 0, unique SKU, stock quantity >= 0

4. departments

CREATE TABLE departments (
    department_id SERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    department_name VARCHAR(100) NOT NULL UNIQUE,
    location VARCHAR(100),
    manager_id INTEGER,
    budget NUMERIC(12, 2),
    created_at TIMESTAMP DEFAULT CURRENT_TIMESTAMP
);
Purpose: Organizational departments
Primary Key: department_id
Note: manager_id will be linked to employees table

5. employees

CREATE TABLE employees (
    employee_id SERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    first_name VARCHAR(50) NOT NULL,
    last_name VARCHAR(50) NOT NULL,
    email VARCHAR(100) UNIQUE NOT NULL,
    phone VARCHAR(20),
    department_id INTEGER NOT NULL,
    job_title VARCHAR(100),
    salary NUMERIC(10, 2) CHECK (salary > 0),
    hire_date DATE NOT NULL DEFAULT CURRENT_DATE,
    manager_id INTEGER,
    is_active BOOLEAN DEFAULT true,
    FOREIGN KEY (department_id) REFERENCES departments(department_id) ON DELETE RESTRICT,
    FOREIGN KEY (manager_id) REFERENCES employees(employee_id) ON DELETE SET NULL
);
Purpose: Employee records with organizational hierarchy
Primary Key: employee_id
Foreign Keys: department_id → departments, manager_id → employees (self-referencing)
Constraints: Unique email, salary > 0

6. orders

CREATE TABLE orders (
    order_id SERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    customer_id INTEGER NOT NULL,
    employee_id INTEGER,
    order_date TIMESTAMP DEFAULT CURRENT_TIMESTAMP,
    required_date DATE,
    shipped_date TIMESTAMP,
    status VARCHAR(20) DEFAULT 'pending' CHECK (status IN ('pending', 'processing', 'shipped', 'delivered', 'cancelled')),
    subtotal NUMERIC(10, 2) DEFAULT 0,
    tax_amount NUMERIC(10, 2) DEFAULT 0,
    shipping_cost NUMERIC(10, 2) DEFAULT 0,
    total_amount NUMERIC(10, 2) GENERATED ALWAYS AS (subtotal + tax_amount + shipping_cost) STORED,
    shipping_address TEXT,
    shipping_city VARCHAR(50),
    shipping_state VARCHAR(50),
    shipping_country VARCHAR(50),
    shipping_postal_code VARCHAR(20),
    notes TEXT,
    FOREIGN KEY (customer_id) REFERENCES customers(customer_id) ON DELETE RESTRICT,
    FOREIGN KEY (employee_id) REFERENCES employees(employee_id) ON DELETE SET NULL
);
Purpose: Order header information
Primary Key: order_id
Foreign Keys: customer_id → customers, employee_id → employees
Generated Column: total_amount (computed from subtotal + tax + shipping)
Status Values: pending, processing, shipped, delivered, cancelled

7. order_items

CREATE TABLE order_items (
    order_item_id SERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    order_id INTEGER NOT NULL,
    product_id INTEGER NOT NULL,
    quantity INTEGER NOT NULL CHECK (quantity > 0),
    unit_price NUMERIC(10, 2) NOT NULL CHECK (unit_price >= 0),
    discount_percent NUMERIC(5, 2) DEFAULT 0 CHECK (discount_percent >= 0 AND discount_percent <= 100),
    line_total NUMERIC(10, 2) GENERATED ALWAYS AS (quantity * unit_price * (1 - discount_percent / 100)) STORED,
    FOREIGN KEY (order_id) REFERENCES orders(order_id) ON DELETE CASCADE,
    FOREIGN KEY (product_id) REFERENCES products(product_id) ON DELETE RESTRICT,
    UNIQUE (order_id, product_id)
);
Purpose: Individual line items for each order
Primary Key: order_item_id
Foreign Keys: order_id → orders (CASCADE delete), product_id → products
Generated Column: line_total (quantity × unit_price × (1 - discount))
Constraints: quantity > 0, discount 0-100%, unique product per order

8. reviews

CREATE TABLE reviews (
    review_id SERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    product_id INTEGER NOT NULL,
    customer_id INTEGER NOT NULL,
    rating INTEGER NOT NULL CHECK (rating >= 1 AND rating <= 5),
    title VARCHAR(200),
    review_text TEXT,
    review_date TIMESTAMP DEFAULT CURRENT_TIMESTAMP,
    helpful_count INTEGER DEFAULT 0,
    verified_purchase BOOLEAN DEFAULT false,
    FOREIGN KEY (product_id) REFERENCES products(product_id) ON DELETE CASCADE,
    FOREIGN KEY (customer_id) REFERENCES customers(customer_id) ON DELETE CASCADE,
    UNIQUE (product_id, customer_id)
);
Purpose: Customer product reviews and ratings
Primary Key: review_id
Foreign Keys: product_id → products, customer_id → customers
Constraints: Rating 1-5 stars, one review per customer per product

9. shipments

CREATE TABLE shipments (
    shipment_id SERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    order_id INTEGER NOT NULL,
    carrier VARCHAR(100),
    tracking_number VARCHAR(100),
    shipped_date TIMESTAMP DEFAULT CURRENT_TIMESTAMP,
    estimated_delivery DATE,
    actual_delivery TIMESTAMP,
    status VARCHAR(20) DEFAULT 'in_transit' CHECK (status IN ('pending', 'in_transit', 'delivered', 'failed', 'returned')),
    notes TEXT,
    FOREIGN KEY (order_id) REFERENCES orders(order_id) ON DELETE CASCADE
);
Purpose: Shipment tracking information
Primary Key: shipment_id
Foreign Key: order_id → orders
Status Values: pending, in_transit, delivered, failed, returned

10. payments

CREATE TABLE payments (
    payment_id SERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    order_id INTEGER NOT NULL,
    payment_date TIMESTAMP DEFAULT CURRENT_TIMESTAMP,
    payment_method VARCHAR(50) CHECK (payment_method IN ('credit_card', 'debit_card', 'paypal', 'bank_transfer', 'cash')),
    amount NUMERIC(10, 2) NOT NULL CHECK (amount > 0),
    status VARCHAR(20) DEFAULT 'pending' CHECK (status IN ('pending', 'completed', 'failed', 'refunded')),
    transaction_id VARCHAR(100) UNIQUE,
    card_last_four CHAR(4),
    notes TEXT,
    FOREIGN KEY (order_id) REFERENCES orders(order_id) ON DELETE RESTRICT
);
Purpose: Payment transaction records
Primary Key: payment_id
Foreign Key: order_id → orders
Payment Methods: credit_card, debit_card, paypal, bank_transfer, cash
Status Values: pending, completed, failed, refunded

Legend:
PK = Primary Key
FK = Foreign Key
─► = One-to-Many Relationship
◄─ = Foreign Key Reference

Table Relationships Summary

Parent Table Child Table Relationship On Delete
customers orders One customer → Many orders RESTRICT
customers reviews One customer → Many reviews CASCADE
categories products One category → Many products RESTRICT
categories categories Parent category → Sub-categories (self-referencing) SET NULL
products order_items One product → Many order items RESTRICT
products reviews One product → Many reviews CASCADE
departments employees One department → Many employees RESTRICT
employees employees Manager → Subordinates (self-referencing) SET NULL
employees orders One employee → Many orders processed SET NULL
orders order_items One order → Many order items CASCADE
orders shipments One order → Many shipments CASCADE
orders payments One order → Many payments RESTRICT

Sample Order Flow

📊 Order Transaction Flow


    ┌─────────────┐
    │   START     │
    └──────┬──────┘
           │
           ▼
    ┌─────────────────────────────────────────┐
    │ 1️⃣  INSERT INTO orders                  │  ← Create order header
    │    (customer_id, subtotal, tax, ...)    │     (Generated: order_id = 1)
    └──────┬──────────────────────────────────┘
           │
           ▼
    ┌─────────────────────────────────────────┐
    │ 2️⃣  INSERT INTO order_items             │  ← Add line items
    │    (order_id=1, product_id, qty, ...)   │     (Computes: line_total)
    └──────┬──────────────────────────────────┘
           │
           ▼
    ┌─────────────────────────────────────────┐
    │ 3️⃣  INSERT INTO payments                │  ← Process payment
    │    (order_id=1, amount, method, ...)    │     (status='completed')
    └──────┬──────────────────────────────────┘
           │
           ▼
    ┌─────────────────────────────────────────┐
    │ 4️⃣  INSERT INTO shipments               │  ← Create shipment
    │    (order_id=1, carrier, tracking, ...)  │     (status='in_transit')
    └──────┬──────────────────────────────────┘
           │
           ▼
    ┌─────────────────────────────────────────┐
    │ 5️⃣  UPDATE orders                       │  ← Update order status
    │    SET status='shipped'                  │
    │    WHERE order_id=1                      │
    └──────┬──────────────────────────────────┘
           │
           ▼
    ┌──────────────┐
    │   COMPLETE   │  ✅ Order successfully processed
    └──────────────┘

Referential Integrity Enforced:
• order_items.order_id → orders.order_id (CASCADE on delete)
• payments.order_id → orders.order_id (RESTRICT on delete)
• shipments.order_id → orders.order_id (CASCADE on delete)
• Generated columns automatically calculated (total_amount, line_total)
                
-- Create an order
INSERT INTO orders (customer_id, employee_id, subtotal, tax_amount, shipping_cost, status)
VALUES (1, 5, 1329.98, 119.70, 15.00, 'processing');

-- Add order items
INSERT INTO order_items (order_id, product_id, quantity, unit_price, discount_percent)
VALUES 
(1, 1, 1, 1299.99, 0),
(1, 2, 1, 29.99, 0);

-- Record payment
INSERT INTO payments (order_id, amount, payment_method, status, transaction_id)
VALUES (1, 1464.68, 'credit_card', 'completed', 'TXN-20250125-001');

-- Create shipment
INSERT INTO shipments (order_id, carrier, tracking_number, status)
VALUES (1, 'FedEx', 'FDX1234567890', 'in_transit');
This creates a complete order:
• Customer #1 (John Doe) places order
• Order contains Laptop ($1,299.99) + Mouse ($29.99)
• Payment of $1,464.68 processed via credit card
• Shipment created with FedEx tracking

Schema Features Demonstrated

✓ Primary Keys

All tables use SERIAL auto-incrementing primary keys

✓ Foreign Keys

Referential integrity with CASCADE/RESTRICT/SET NULL

✓ Check Constraints

Price >= 0, quantity > 0, rating 1-5, status enums

✓ Unique Constraints

Email addresses, SKUs, one review per customer/product

✓ Default Values

CURRENT_TIMESTAMP, status defaults, boolean flags

✓ Generated Columns

total_amount (orders), line_total (order_items)

✓ Self-Referencing

Categories (parent), Employees (manager)

✓ Data Types

VARCHAR, TEXT, NUMERIC, INTEGER, BOOLEAN, TIMESTAMP, DATE

PostgreSQL Data Types Used

📝 Text Types

VARCHAR(n) - Variable length with limit
TEXT - Unlimited length text
CHAR(n) - Fixed length

🔢 Numeric Types

SERIAL - Auto-increment integer
INTEGER - Whole numbers
NUMERIC(p,s) - Exact decimal

📅 Date/Time Types

TIMESTAMP - Date and time
DATE - Date only
TIME - Time only

✓ Boolean Type

BOOLEAN - true/false
true, false, NULL

Constraint Types Explained

🔑 PRIMARY KEY

Purpose: Uniquely identifies each row

Rules: NOT NULL + UNIQUE

customer_id SERIAL PRIMARY KEY

🔗 FOREIGN KEY

Purpose: Links to another table

Actions: CASCADE, RESTRICT, SET NULL

FOREIGN KEY (customer_id)
REFERENCES customers(customer_id)

✓ CHECK

Purpose: Validates data conditions

Examples: Range, pattern, enum

CHECK (price >= 0)
CHECK (rating BETWEEN 1 AND 5)

◆ UNIQUE

Purpose: Prevents duplicates

Note: Allows multiple NULLs

email VARCHAR(100) UNIQUE
UNIQUE (order_id, product_id)

⚠ NOT NULL

Purpose: Requires a value

Default: Columns allow NULL

first_name VARCHAR(50) NOT NULL
email VARCHAR(100) NOT NULL

📐 DEFAULT

Purpose: Auto-fills values

Common: CURRENT_TIMESTAMP, 0, true

created_at TIMESTAMP 
  DEFAULT CURRENT_TIMESTAMP

Foreign Key Actions (ON DELETE)

Action Behavior Example Use Case Visual
CASCADE Delete child rows automatically Delete order → Delete order_items 🔴 → 🔴🔴🔴
RESTRICT Prevent deletion if children exist Cannot delete customer with orders 🔴 ← ⛔ 🟢🟢
SET NULL Set child FK to NULL Delete employee → order.employee_id = NULL 🔴 → ⚪⚪⚪
SET DEFAULT Set child FK to default value Delete category → products.category_id = 1 🔴 → 🔵🔵🔵
NO ACTION Check at transaction end (similar to RESTRICT) Deferred constraint checking 🔴 ← ⏸️ 🟢🟢

Common Query Patterns

🔍 Quick Reference Queries

These queries demonstrate how to work with the schema:

Get Customer Orders

SELECT 
    c.first_name,
    c.last_name,
    o.order_id,
    o.order_date,
    o.total_amount,
    o.status
FROM customers c
JOIN orders o ON c.customer_id = o.customer_id
WHERE c.customer_id = 1
ORDER BY o.order_date DESC;
Returns: All orders for customer #1
Shows: Name, order ID, date, total, status
Sample Result Set:
first_name last_name order_id order_date total_amount status
John Doe 5 2026-01-20 14:30:00 $234.99 delivered
John Doe 3 2026-01-15 10:22:00 $1,464.68 shipped
John Doe 1 2026-01-10 09:15:00 $899.99 delivered

Product Sales Report

SELECT 
    p.product_name,
    COUNT(oi.order_item_id) as times_sold,
    SUM(oi.quantity) as total_quantity,
    SUM(oi.line_total) as total_revenue
FROM products p
JOIN order_items oi ON p.product_id = oi.product_id
GROUP BY p.product_id, p.product_name
ORDER BY total_revenue DESC
LIMIT 10;
Returns: Top 10 products by revenue
Shows: Sales count, quantity, revenue
Sample Result Set:
product_name times_sold total_quantity total_revenue
Laptop Pro 15" 45 52 $67,599.48
Smartphone X 38 42 $37,799.58
Mechanical Keyboard 28 31 $4,649.69

Order Details (Master-Detail)

SELECT 
    o.order_id,
    o.order_date,
    p.product_name,
    oi.quantity,
    oi.unit_price,
    oi.line_total
FROM orders o
JOIN order_items oi ON o.order_id = oi.order_id
JOIN products p ON oi.product_id = p.product_id
WHERE o.order_id = 1;
Returns: Complete order breakdown
Shows: Each product with quantities & prices

Employee Hierarchy

WITH RECURSIVE emp_hierarchy AS (
    SELECT employee_id, first_name, 
           manager_id, 1 as level
    FROM employees
    WHERE manager_id IS NULL
    UNION ALL
    SELECT e.employee_id, e.first_name,
           e.manager_id, eh.level + 1
    FROM employees e
    JOIN emp_hierarchy eh 
      ON e.manager_id = eh.employee_id
)
SELECT * FROM emp_hierarchy
ORDER BY level, employee_id;
Returns: Org chart hierarchy
Shows: All employees with reporting levels

Low Stock Alert

SELECT 
    product_id,
    product_name,
    stock_quantity,
    reorder_level,
    (reorder_level - stock_quantity) 
      AS units_needed
FROM products
WHERE stock_quantity < reorder_level
  AND is_active = true
ORDER BY units_needed DESC;
Returns: Products needing restock
Shows: Current stock vs reorder level

Customer Lifetime Value

SELECT 
    c.customer_id,
    c.first_name || ' ' || c.last_name 
      AS customer_name,
    COUNT(o.order_id) as total_orders,
    SUM(o.total_amount) as lifetime_value,
    AVG(o.total_amount) as avg_order_value
FROM customers c
LEFT JOIN orders o 
  ON c.customer_id = o.customer_id
GROUP BY c.customer_id, customer_name
HAVING COUNT(o.order_id) > 0
ORDER BY lifetime_value DESC;
Returns: Customer spending analysis
Shows: Total orders, lifetime value, averages

🎓 Advanced Query Techniques

Complex Queries Using This Schema

These examples demonstrate advanced PostgreSQL features like window functions, CTEs, JSON aggregation, and LATERAL joins:

🪟 Window Functions - Sales Running Total

SELECT 
    order_date::DATE as order_day,
    order_id,
    total_amount,
    SUM(total_amount) OVER (
        ORDER BY order_date
        ROWS BETWEEN UNBOUNDED PRECEDING AND CURRENT ROW
    ) AS running_total,
    AVG(total_amount) OVER (
        ORDER BY order_date
        ROWS BETWEEN 6 PRECEDING AND CURRENT ROW
    ) AS moving_avg_7day,
    ROW_NUMBER() OVER (ORDER BY total_amount DESC) AS revenue_rank
FROM orders
WHERE status IN ('delivered', 'shipped')
ORDER BY order_date;
Result (first 3 rows):
order_day order_id total_amount running_total moving_avg_7day revenue_rank
2026-01-10 1 $1,464.68 $1,464.68 $1,464.68 1
2026-01-15 3 $899.99 $2,364.67 $1,182.34 2
2026-01-20 5 $234.99 $2,599.66 $866.55 3

🔄 CTEs - Customer Segmentation

WITH customer_stats AS (
    SELECT 
        customer_id,
        COUNT(*) as order_count,
        SUM(total_amount) as total_spent,
        MAX(order_date) as last_order_date
    FROM orders
    WHERE status != 'cancelled'
    GROUP BY customer_id
),
customer_segments AS (
    SELECT 
        customer_id,
        order_count,
        total_spent,
        CASE 
            WHEN total_spent > 5000 THEN 'VIP'
            WHEN total_spent > 1000 THEN 'Gold'
            WHEN total_spent > 500 THEN 'Silver'
            ELSE 'Bronze'
        END AS segment
    FROM customer_stats
)
SELECT 
    c.first_name,
    c.last_name,
    cs.segment,
    cs.order_count,
    cs.total_spent
FROM customers c
JOIN customer_segments cs ON c.customer_id = cs.customer_id
ORDER BY cs.total_spent DESC;
Result (Customer Segments):
first_name last_name segment order_count total_spent
John Doe VIP 12 $8,452.50
Emily Williams Silver 5 $742.30
Michael Brown Bronze 2 $199.98

↔️ LATERAL Joins - Latest Order Per Customer

SELECT 
    c.customer_id,
    c.first_name,
    c.last_name,
    latest.order_id,
    latest.order_date,
    latest.total_amount,
    latest.status
FROM customers c
CROSS JOIN LATERAL (
    SELECT order_id, order_date, total_amount, status
    FROM orders
    WHERE orders.customer_id = c.customer_id
    ORDER BY order_date DESC
    LIMIT 1
) AS latest
WHERE c.is_active = true
ORDER BY latest.order_date DESC
LIMIT 5;
Returns: Most recent order for each active customer
Features: LATERAL join (correlated subquery), top-N per group pattern

❌ Common Pitfalls to Avoid

🚫 Missing WHERE Clause

Problem: Accidentally updating/deleting all rows

-- DANGER! Updates all products
UPDATE products SET price = 0;

-- SAFE: Updates specific product
UPDATE products SET price = 0
WHERE product_id = 123;

✓ Solution: Always use WHERE in UPDATE/DELETE

🚫 Not Using Transactions

Problem: Partial data commits on errors

-- DANGER! Order created but payment fails
INSERT INTO orders (...);
INSERT INTO payments (...); -- Error!

-- SAFE: All or nothing
BEGIN;
  INSERT INTO orders (...);
  INSERT INTO payments (...);
COMMIT;

✓ Solution: Wrap related operations in transactions

🚫 Ignoring NULL Behavior

Problem: NULL != NULL in comparisons

-- WRONG: Returns 0 rows even if NULLs exist
SELECT * FROM employees
WHERE manager_id = NULL;

-- CORRECT: Use IS NULL
SELECT * FROM employees
WHERE manager_id IS NULL;

✓ Solution: Use IS NULL / IS NOT NULL

🚫 Cartesian Products

Problem: Forgetting JOIN conditions

-- DANGER! Returns 1,000,000 rows (1000×1000)
SELECT * FROM orders, customers;

-- CORRECT: Always specify JOIN condition
SELECT * FROM orders o
JOIN customers c ON o.customer_id = c.customer_id;

✓ Solution: Always use explicit JOIN syntax

🚫 VARCHAR Without Length

Problem: Unbounded storage growth

-- RISKY: No length validation
CREATE TABLE users (
    username VARCHAR  -- Can be 10GB!
);

-- BETTER: Set appropriate limit
CREATE TABLE users (
    username VARCHAR(50)
);

✓ Solution: Always specify VARCHAR(n) or use TEXT

🚫 Not Handling Duplicates

Problem: Constraint violations on INSERT

-- FAILS on duplicate email
INSERT INTO customers (email, ...)
VALUES ('john@email.com', ...);

-- SAFE: Update if exists
INSERT INTO customers (email, ...)
VALUES ('john@email.com', ...)
ON CONFLICT (email) DO UPDATE
SET last_login = CURRENT_TIMESTAMP;

✓ Solution: Use ON CONFLICT (UPSERT)

Recommended Indexes

⚡ Performance Optimization

Beyond the automatic indexes created for PRIMARY KEYs and UNIQUE constraints, consider these additional indexes for optimal query performance:

Table Index Name Columns Purpose Index Type
customers idx_customers_email email Fast login/lookup by email (already created by UNIQUE) B-tree
customers idx_customers_city_state city, state Geographic queries, customer analytics B-tree
products idx_products_category category_id Filter products by category (auto-created by FK) B-tree
products idx_products_price price Price range queries, sorting B-tree
products idx_products_sku sku SKU lookups (already created by UNIQUE) B-tree
products idx_products_name_trgm product_name Full-text search, fuzzy matching GIN (trigram)
orders idx_orders_customer customer_id Customer order history (auto-created by FK) B-tree
orders idx_orders_date order_date Date range queries, reporting B-tree
orders idx_orders_status status Filter by order status B-tree
orders idx_orders_customer_date customer_id, order_date DESC Customer order history sorted by date B-tree
order_items idx_order_items_order order_id Get items for an order (auto-created by FK) B-tree
order_items idx_order_items_product product_id Product sales analysis (auto-created by FK) B-tree
employees idx_employees_dept department_id Department employee lists (auto-created by FK) B-tree
employees idx_employees_manager manager_id Manager reporting hierarchy (auto-created by FK) B-tree
reviews idx_reviews_product product_id Product reviews lookup (auto-created by FK) B-tree
reviews idx_reviews_rating rating Filter by rating level B-tree

Index Creation Scripts

-- Geographic indexes
CREATE INDEX idx_customers_city_state ON customers(city, state);

-- Product search optimization
CREATE INDEX idx_products_price ON products(price);
CREATE EXTENSION IF NOT EXISTS pg_trgm;
CREATE INDEX idx_products_name_trgm ON products USING GIN (product_name gin_trgm_ops);

-- Order query optimization
CREATE INDEX idx_orders_date ON orders(order_date);
CREATE INDEX idx_orders_status ON orders(status);
CREATE INDEX idx_orders_customer_date ON orders(customer_id, order_date DESC);

-- Review filtering
CREATE INDEX idx_reviews_rating ON reviews(rating);
Index Benefits:
• B-tree indexes: Fast equality and range queries
• GIN trigram indexes: Fuzzy text search with LIKE '%term%'
• Composite indexes: Optimize multi-column WHERE/ORDER BY
• Foreign key indexes: Auto-created, improve JOIN performance

✅ Data Validation in Action

Constraint Testing

See how the schema enforces data integrity automatically:

✓ Valid INSERT

INSERT INTO products (
    product_name, category_id,
    price, stock_quantity, sku
) VALUES (
    'Gaming Mouse', 1,
    49.99, 100, 'MOU-GAM-001'
);
✓ SUCCESS: INSERT 0 1

✗ Negative Price

INSERT INTO products (
    product_name, category_id,
    price, stock_quantity
) VALUES (
    'Free Item', 1, -10.00, 50
);
✗ ERROR: check constraint "products_price_check"
Failing row: (price=-10.00)

✗ Duplicate Email

INSERT INTO customers (
    first_name, last_name, email
) VALUES (
    'Another', 'Person',
    'john.doe@email.com'
);
✗ ERROR: unique constraint "customers_email_key"
Key (email) already exists

✗ Invalid Foreign Key

INSERT INTO orders (
    customer_id, subtotal, tax_amount
) VALUES (
    99999, 100.00, 10.00
);
✗ ERROR: foreign key constraint "orders_customer_id_fkey"
Key (customer_id)=(99999) not in table "customers"

✗ Rating Out of Range

INSERT INTO reviews (
    product_id, customer_id,
    rating, review_text
) VALUES (
    1, 1, 10, 'Amazing!'
);
✗ ERROR: check constraint "reviews_rating_check"
Rating must be between 1 and 5

✓ UPSERT Pattern

INSERT INTO customers (
    email, first_name, last_name
) VALUES (
    'john.doe@email.com', 'John', 'Doe'
)
ON CONFLICT (email) DO UPDATE
SET last_login = CURRENT_TIMESTAMP
RETURNING *;
✓ SUCCESS: Updated existing customer
RETURNING clause shows updated row

Performance Best Practices

⚡

Use EXPLAIN ANALYZE

Always analyze query execution plans before optimizing. Shows actual execution time, rows processed, and index usage.

EXPLAIN ANALYZE
SELECT * FROM orders
WHERE customer_id = 1;
🎯

Select Only Needed Columns

Avoid SELECT * in production. Specify exact columns to reduce I/O and memory usage.

-- Good
SELECT customer_id, email FROM customers;
-- Avoid
SELECT * FROM customers;
🔍

Index Foreign Keys

PostgreSQL doesn't auto-index FKs. Manually create indexes on foreign key columns for JOIN performance.

CREATE INDEX idx_orders_customer
ON orders(customer_id);
📊

Update Statistics Regularly

Run ANALYZE to update table statistics. Helps query planner make better decisions.

ANALYZE orders;
ANALYZE;  -- All tables
🗜️

Use LIMIT Wisely

Add LIMIT to prevent accidentally fetching millions of rows. Combine with ORDER BY for consistent results.

SELECT * FROM orders
ORDER BY order_date DESC
LIMIT 100;
🔄

Batch INSERT Operations

Use multi-row INSERT instead of individual statements. Up to 10x faster for bulk data.

INSERT INTO products (name, price)
VALUES 
  ('Product 1', 10.00),
  ('Product 2', 20.00);
🎲

Avoid N+1 Query Problem

Use JOINs or subqueries instead of looping queries. Single query is always faster than N queries.

-- Good: Single query with JOIN
SELECT o.*, c.first_name
FROM orders o
JOIN customers c ON o.customer_id = c.customer_id;
💾

Use Connection Pooling

Reuse database connections with pgBouncer or application-level pools. Reduces connection overhead.

Max connections: ~100
Pool size: 10-20 per app instance
Recommended: PgBouncer

psql ecommerce_db < backup.sql

Always backup before changes

📚 Chapter 4: SQL Syntax

Overview

This chapter describes the syntax of SQL. It forms the foundation for understanding how specific SQL commands are structured. Content will be added in subsequent updates.

📝

Lexical Structure

Identifiers, keywords, constants, operators, special characters, comments, and precedence rules.

🔢

Value Expressions

Column references, parameters, subscripts, field selection, operators, functions, aggregates, window functions, and more.

⚡

Calling Functions

Positional notation, named notation, and mixed notation for function invocation.

4.1 Lexical Structure

📖 Full Description

The lexical structure of SQL defines the basic building blocks that make up SQL statements. Understanding these elements is fundamental to writing valid SQL code. This includes identifiers for naming database objects, keywords that have special meaning in SQL, constants representing literal values, operators for computations and comparisons, special characters with specific syntactic meanings, comments for documentation, and precedence rules that determine the order of operations.

SQL text consists of a sequence of commands, where each command is composed of tokens (keywords, identifiers, quoted identifiers, literals, and special character symbols) and is typically terminated by a semicolon. The lexical analyzer breaks input text into these tokens while removing whitespace and comments.

4.1.1 Identifiers and Key Words

📖 Full Description

Identifiers are names used to identify database objects such as tables, columns, functions, and schemas. They must begin with a letter (a-z) or underscore (_), and subsequent characters can be letters, underscores, digits (0-9), or dollar signs ($). Identifiers are case-insensitive unless quoted.

Key Words are predefined words that have special meaning in SQL, such as SELECT, FROM, WHERE, and CREATE. While SQL is generally case-insensitive for keywords, convention is to write them in uppercase for readability.

Quoted Identifiers are enclosed in double quotes and can contain any characters, preserve case sensitivity, and even match reserved keywords. This allows you to use special characters, mixed case, or keywords as object names.

Syntax
-- Unquoted Identifier (case-insensitive)
identifier

-- Quoted Identifier (case-sensitive, allows special chars)
"Identifier with spaces and MixedCase"

-- Examples of identifiers
table_name
column1
my_function
schema_name
"Table Name"
"SELECT"  -- Keyword used as identifier

Parameters

Unquoted Identifiers:

  • Starting character: Must be a letter (a-z, A-Z) or underscore (_)
  • Subsequent characters: Letters, digits (0-9), underscores (_), or dollar signs ($)
  • Maximum length: 63 bytes (longer names are truncated)
  • Case sensitivity: Folded to lowercase unless quoted
  • Restrictions: Cannot be reserved keywords without quoting

Quoted Identifiers:

  • Delimiters: Double quotes ("identifier")
  • Allowed characters: Any character except NUL (zero byte)
  • Case preservation: Exact case is preserved
  • Escaping quotes: Use double double-quotes ("") to include a quote character
  • Unicode support: Supports Unicode characters

🎯 Example 1: Basic Identifiers

-- Create table with standard identifiers
CREATE TABLE customers (
    customer_id INTEGER,
    first_name VARCHAR(50),
    last_name VARCHAR(50),
    email_address VARCHAR(100)
);

-- Insert data
INSERT INTO customers VALUES (1, 'John', 'Doe', 'john@example.com');

-- Query using identifiers
SELECT customer_id, first_name, last_name
FROM customers
WHERE customer_id = 1;
Result:
CREATE TABLE
INSERT 0 1

 customer_id | first_name | last_name
-------------+------------+-----------
           1 | John       | Doe
(1 row)
Explanation: Standard unquoted identifiers are used for the table and column names. PostgreSQL automatically converts them to lowercase (customers, customer_id, first_name).

🎯 Example 2: Case Insensitivity

-- These are all equivalent (refer to same table)
SELECT * FROM customers;
SELECT * FROM CUSTOMERS;
SELECT * FROM Customers;
SELECT * FROM CuStOmErS;

-- All refer to same column
SELECT first_name FROM customers;
SELECT FIRST_NAME FROM customers;
SELECT First_Name FROM customers;
Result (all queries return same data):
 customer_id | first_name | last_name | email_address
-------------+------------+-----------+------------------
           1 | John       | Doe       | john@example.com
(1 row)
Explanation: SQL is case-insensitive for unquoted identifiers. All variations are converted to lowercase and treated identically.

🎯 Example 3: Quoted Identifiers (Case Sensitive)

-- Create table with quoted (case-sensitive) identifiers
CREATE TABLE "Products" (
    "ProductID" INTEGER,
    "ProductName" VARCHAR(100),
    "Price" NUMERIC(10,2)
);

INSERT INTO "Products" VALUES (1, 'Laptop', 999.99);

-- This works (exact case match)
SELECT "ProductID", "ProductName", "Price"
FROM "Products";

-- These will FAIL (case mismatch)
-- SELECT ProductID FROM Products;
-- SELECT productid FROM "Products";
Result:
CREATE TABLE
INSERT 0 1

 ProductID | ProductName |  Price
-----------+-------------+---------
         1 | Laptop      |  999.99
(1 row)
Explanation: Quoted identifiers preserve exact case. "ProductID" and "productid" are different identifiers. You must use exact case in all references.

🎯 Example 4: Identifiers with Special Characters

-- Quoted identifiers can contain spaces and special characters
CREATE TABLE "Customer Orders" (
    "Order #" INTEGER,
    "Customer Name" VARCHAR(100),
    "Order Date" DATE,
    "Total $$$" NUMERIC(10,2)
);

INSERT INTO "Customer Orders" VALUES 
    (1001, 'Alice Smith', '2026-01-15', 250.50),
    (1002, 'Bob Johnson', '2026-01-20', 175.00);

SELECT 
    "Order #",
    "Customer Name",
    "Total $$$"
FROM "Customer Orders"
WHERE "Total $$$" > 200;
Result:
CREATE TABLE
INSERT 0 2

 Order # | Customer Name | Total $$$
---------+---------------+-----------
    1001 | Alice Smith   |    250.50
(1 row)
Explanation: Quoted identifiers allow spaces, special characters ($, #), and any Unicode characters. This is useful for creating user-friendly column names, though standard practice favors simple unquoted identifiers.

🎯 Example 5: Using Keywords as Identifiers

-- Keywords must be quoted to use as identifiers
CREATE TABLE orders_table (
    "select" INTEGER,        -- "select" is a keyword, must quote
    "from" VARCHAR(50),      -- "from" is a keyword, must quote
    "where" BOOLEAN,         -- "where" is a keyword, must quote
    normal_column TEXT       -- regular identifier, no quotes needed
);

INSERT INTO orders_table VALUES (1, 'LocationA', true, 'Some text');

-- Must quote keywords in SELECT
SELECT 
    "select",
    "from",
    "where",
    normal_column
FROM orders_table;
Result:
CREATE TABLE
INSERT 0 1

 select |   from    | where | normal_column
--------+-----------+-------+---------------
      1 | LocationA | t     | Some text
(1 row)
Explanation: Reserved keywords (SELECT, FROM, WHERE, etc.) can be used as column/table names only if quoted. However, this practice is discouraged as it makes queries harder to read and maintain.

4.1.2 Constants

📖 Full Description

Constants (also called literals) are fixed values that appear directly in SQL statements. PostgreSQL supports several types of constants including string constants, numeric constants, bit-string constants, and special constants. Each type has specific syntax and rules for representation.

Constants are fundamental to SQL as they represent actual data values in queries, INSERT statements, comparisons, and expressions. Understanding how to properly write constants ensures data is interpreted correctly by the database engine.

Syntax
-- String Constants
'This is a string'
'String with ''escaped'' quote'
$$Dollar-quoted string$$
$tag$String with tag$tag$

-- Numeric Constants
42                  -- Integer
3.14159            -- Decimal
1.2e-5             -- Scientific notation
0xFF               -- Hexadecimal (255)
0o17               -- Octal (15)
0b1011             -- Binary (11)

-- Bit-String Constants
B'1011'            -- Binary string
X'1F3A'            -- Hexadecimal string

-- Boolean Constants
TRUE, FALSE, NULL

-- Date/Time Constants (type-specified)
DATE '2026-01-25'
TIME '14:30:00'
TIMESTAMP '2026-01-25 14:30:00'
INTERVAL '1 day'

Parameters

String Constants:

  • Single-quoted: Enclosed in single quotes 'text'
  • Escape sequences: Use '' (two single quotes) to include a quote
  • C-style escapes: Enable with E'text\n' (E prefix for backslash escapes)
  • Dollar-quoting: $$text$$ or $tag$text$tag$ (no escaping needed)
  • Unicode escapes: U&'\0041' for Unicode code points

Numeric Constants:

  • Integer: Digits without decimal point (e.g., 42, -17, 0)
  • Decimal: Digits with decimal point (e.g., 3.14, 0.001)
  • Scientific: Exponent notation (e.g., 1.5e3, 2.0e-4)
  • Alternative bases: 0x (hex), 0o (octal), 0b (binary)
  • Type specification: Optional CAST or :: notation

Boolean Constants:

  • TRUE: Accepts TRUE, 't', 'true', 'y', 'yes', 'on', '1'
  • FALSE: Accepts FALSE, 'f', 'false', 'n', 'no', 'off', '0'
  • NULL: Represents absence of value (not a boolean per se)

🎯 Example 1: String Constants

-- Different ways to write string constants
SELECT 
    'Simple string' AS simple,
    'String with ''escaped'' quotes' AS escaped_quotes,
    $$String with "quotes" and 'apostrophes'$$ AS dollar_quoted,
    $custom$Any content here, even $$ symbols$custom$ AS tagged_dollar,
    E'String with\nnewline\tand tab' AS escape_sequences;
Result:
    simple     |      escaped_quotes       |         dollar_quoted          |       tagged_dollar        |      escape_sequences
---------------+---------------------------+--------------------------------+----------------------------+---------------------------
 Simple string | String with 'escaped'     | String with "quotes" and       | Any content here, even $$  | String with
               | quotes                    | 'apostrophes'                  | symbols                    | newline  and tab
(1 row)
Explanation: String constants can be written with single quotes (escaping with ''), dollar quotes (no escaping needed), or C-style escapes with E prefix (\n for newline, \t for tab).

🎯 Example 2: Numeric Constants

-- Various numeric constant formats
SELECT 
    42 AS integer,
    3.14159 AS decimal,
    1.5e3 AS scientific,          -- 1500
    2.5e-2 AS small_scientific,   -- 0.025
    0xFF AS hexadecimal,          -- 255
    0o17 AS octal,                -- 15
    0b1011 AS binary,             -- 11
    999999999999999999999999::NUMERIC AS large_number;
Result:
 integer | decimal  | scientific | small_scientific | hexadecimal | octal | binary |     large_number
---------+----------+------------+------------------+-------------+-------+--------+--------------------------
      42 | 3.14159  |       1500 |            0.025 |         255 |    15 |     11 | 999999999999999999999999
(1 row)
Explanation: PostgreSQL supports integers, decimals, scientific notation (1.5e3 = 1.5 × 10³), and alternative number bases (0x hex, 0o octal, 0b binary). Large numbers may require explicit typing with ::NUMERIC.

🎯 Example 3: Boolean and NULL Constants

-- Create table to test boolean values
CREATE TEMP TABLE bool_test (
    id INTEGER,
    active BOOLEAN,
    description TEXT
);

INSERT INTO bool_test VALUES
    (1, TRUE, 'Explicitly TRUE'),
    (2, 't', 'Single char t'),
    (3, 'yes', 'Word yes'),
    (4, '1', 'Number 1'),
    (5, FALSE, 'Explicitly FALSE'),
    (6, 'f', 'Single char f'),
    (7, 'no', 'Word no'),
    (8, '0', 'Number 0'),
    (9, NULL, 'NULL value');

SELECT id, active, description FROM bool_test ORDER BY id;
Result:
 id | active |    description
----+--------+-------------------
  1 | t      | Explicitly TRUE
  2 | t      | Single char t
  3 | t      | Word yes
  4 | t      | Number 1
  5 | f      | Explicitly FALSE
  6 | f      | Single char f
  7 | f      | Word no
  8 | f      | Number 0
  9 | [null] | NULL value
(9 rows)
Explanation: Boolean constants accept multiple formats: TRUE/'t'/'true'/'yes'/'on'/'1' for true, FALSE/'f'/'false'/'no'/'off'/'0' for false. NULL represents the absence of a value (displayed as [null] or blank).

🎯 Example 4: Date/Time Constants

-- Date/time constants with type specification
SELECT 
    DATE '2026-01-25' AS date_constant,
    TIME '14:30:00' AS time_constant,
    TIME '14:30:00-05:00' AS time_with_zone,
    TIMESTAMP '2026-01-25 14:30:00' AS timestamp_constant,
    TIMESTAMP WITH TIME ZONE '2026-01-25 14:30:00-05:00' AS timestamptz,
    INTERVAL '3 days 2 hours' AS interval_constant,
    INTERVAL '1 year 6 months' AS interval_year_month;
Result:
 date_constant | time_constant | time_with_zone |  timestamp_constant | timestamptz              | interval_constant | interval_year_month
---------------+---------------+----------------+---------------------+--------------------------+-------------------+---------------------
 2026-01-25    | 14:30:00      | 14:30:00-05    | 2026-01-25 14:30:00 | 2026-01-25 14:30:00-05   | 3 days 02:00:00   | 1 year 6 mons
(1 row)
Explanation: Date/time constants use type keywords (DATE, TIME, TIMESTAMP, INTERVAL) followed by string literals. ISO 8601 format (YYYY-MM-DD) is standard. Time zones can be specified with offsets like -05:00.

🎯 Example 5: Constants in Practical Queries

-- Using constants in real queries with our ecommerce database
SELECT 
    product_name,
    price,
    CASE 
        WHEN price > 500.00 THEN 'Premium'    -- Numeric constant
        WHEN price > 100.00 THEN 'Standard'   -- Comparison with constant
        ELSE 'Budget'                         -- String constant
    END AS price_category,
    price * 0.9 AS discounted_price,          -- Math with constant
    stock_quantity > 10 AS in_stock           -- Boolean expression
FROM products
WHERE is_active = TRUE                        -- Boolean constant
  AND created_at >= DATE '2026-01-01'        -- Date constant
ORDER BY price DESC
LIMIT 5;                                      -- Integer constant
Result:
  product_name   |  price  | price_category | discounted_price | in_stock
-----------------+---------+----------------+------------------+----------
 Laptop Pro 15"  | 1299.99 | Premium        |          1169.99 | t
 Smartphone X    |  899.99 | Premium        |           809.99 | t
 Mechanical Kbd  |  149.99 | Standard       |           134.99 | t
 Wireless Mouse  |   29.99 | Budget         |            26.99 | t
 USB-C Cable     |   12.99 | Budget         |            11.69 | t
(5 rows)
Explanation: Constants are used throughout queries: numeric (500.00, 0.9), string ('Premium', 'Standard'), boolean (TRUE), date (DATE '2026-01-01'), and integer (5). They form the basis of comparisons, calculations, and filtering logic.

4.1.3 Operators

📖 Full Description

Operators are special symbols or keywords that perform operations on one or more operands (values or expressions). PostgreSQL supports a rich set of operators including arithmetic operators (+, -, *, /), comparison operators (=, <, >, <=, >=, !=), logical operators (AND, OR, NOT), string operators (||), pattern matching operators (LIKE, SIMILAR TO, ~), and many specialized operators for arrays, JSON, geometric types, and more.

Operators can be unary (operating on one value) like -5 or NOT true, binary (operating on two values) like 5 + 3 or price > 100, or even ternary like BETWEEN. PostgreSQL also supports custom operators, allowing extensions to define their own operation symbols.

Syntax
-- Arithmetic Operators
expression + expression    -- Addition
expression - expression    -- Subtraction
expression * expression    -- Multiplication
expression / expression    -- Division
expression % expression    -- Modulo (remainder)
expression ^ expression    -- Exponentiation
|/ expression             -- Square root
||/ expression            -- Cube root
@ expression              -- Absolute value
expression ! expression    -- Factorial

-- Comparison Operators
expression = expression    -- Equal
expression != expression   -- Not equal
expression <> expression   -- Not equal (alternative)
expression < expression    -- Less than
expression > expression    -- Greater than
expression <= expression   -- Less than or equal
expression >= expression   -- Greater than or equal

-- Logical Operators
expression AND expression  -- Logical AND
expression OR expression   -- Logical OR
NOT expression            -- Logical NOT

-- String Operators
string || string          -- Concatenation
string LIKE pattern       -- Pattern matching
string ILIKE pattern      -- Case-insensitive LIKE
string ~ pattern          -- Regex match (case-sensitive)
string ~* pattern         -- Regex match (case-insensitive)

-- Other Common Operators
expression IS NULL        -- NULL check
expression IS NOT NULL    -- NOT NULL check
expression BETWEEN a AND b -- Range check
expression IN (list)      -- Membership test
expression || expression  -- Array/string concatenation

Parameters

Arithmetic Operators:

  • + (addition): Adds two numeric values or adds interval to date/time
  • - (subtraction): Subtracts second value from first, or negates a value (unary)
  • * (multiplication): Multiplies two numeric values
  • / (division): Divides first value by second (integer division truncates)
  • % (modulo): Returns remainder of division
  • ^ (exponentiation): Raises first value to power of second

Comparison Operators:

  • = (equal): Tests for equality (NULL = NULL is NULL, not true)
  • != or <> (not equal): Tests for inequality
  • < (less than): Tests if first value is less than second
  • > (greater than): Tests if first value is greater than second
  • <= (less or equal): Tests if first is less than or equal to second
  • >= (greater or equal): Tests if first is greater than or equal to second

Logical Operators:

  • AND: Returns TRUE if both operands are TRUE (NULL propagates)
  • OR: Returns TRUE if either operand is TRUE
  • NOT: Negates boolean value (NOT TRUE = FALSE, NOT NULL = NULL)

String Operators:

  • || (concatenation): Joins two strings into one
  • LIKE: Pattern matching with % (any chars) and _ (single char)
  • ILIKE: Case-insensitive LIKE
  • ~ (regex): POSIX regular expression matching

🎯 Example 1: Arithmetic Operators

-- Arithmetic operations on product prices
SELECT 
    product_name,
    price,
    price + 10 AS price_plus_10,
    price - 5 AS price_minus_5,
    price * 0.8 AS discounted_20_percent,
    price / 2 AS half_price,
    price % 100 AS remainder_div_100,
    POWER(price, 2) AS price_squared,
    SQRT(price) AS square_root_price
FROM products
WHERE product_id <= 3
ORDER BY product_id;
Result:
  product_name   |  price  | price_plus_10 | price_minus_5 | discounted_20_percent | half_price | remainder_div_100 | price_squared | square_root_price
-----------------+---------+---------------+---------------+-----------------------+------------+-------------------+---------------+-------------------
 Laptop Pro 15"  | 1299.99 |       1309.99 |       1294.99 |               1039.99 |     649.99 |             99.99 |      1689974  |          36.0554
 Wireless Mouse  |   29.99 |         39.99 |         24.99 |                 23.99 |      14.99 |             29.99 |          899  |           5.4763
 Smartphone X    |  899.99 |        909.99 |        894.99 |                719.99 |     449.99 |             99.99 |       809982  |          30.0000
(3 rows)
Explanation: Arithmetic operators perform mathematical calculations. Addition (+), subtraction (-), multiplication (*), division (/), and modulo (%) work on numeric types. POWER() and SQRT() are functions, but ^ operator can also be used for exponentiation.

🎯 Example 2: Comparison Operators

-- Using comparison operators to filter products
SELECT 
    product_name,
    price,
    stock_quantity,
    CASE
        WHEN price = 29.99 THEN 'Exactly $29.99'
        WHEN price != 29.99 THEN 'Not $29.99'
    END AS price_check,
    CASE
        WHEN stock_quantity < 50 THEN 'Low Stock'
        WHEN stock_quantity >= 50 AND stock_quantity < 100 THEN 'Medium Stock'
        WHEN stock_quantity >= 100 THEN 'High Stock'
    END AS stock_status
FROM products
WHERE price > 20 AND price <= 1000
ORDER BY price DESC
LIMIT 5;
Result:
  product_name   |  price  | stock_quantity |  price_check  | stock_status
-----------------+---------+----------------+---------------+--------------
 Smartphone X    |  899.99 |            100 | Not $29.99    | High Stock
 Mechanical Kbd  |  149.99 |             75 | Not $29.99    | Medium Stock
 Wireless Mouse  |   29.99 |            200 | Exactly $29.99| High Stock
(3 rows)
Explanation: Comparison operators (=, !=, <, >, <=, >=) compare values and return boolean results (TRUE/FALSE). They're fundamental for WHERE clauses, CASE expressions, and JOIN conditions. Note: price = 29.99 tests exact equality.

🎯 Example 3: Logical Operators (AND, OR, NOT)

-- Combining conditions with logical operators
SELECT 
    product_name,
    price,
    stock_quantity,
    is_active,
    CASE
        WHEN price > 100 AND stock_quantity > 50 THEN 'Premium + Available'
        WHEN price > 100 OR stock_quantity > 100 THEN 'Premium OR High Stock'
        WHEN NOT is_active THEN 'Inactive'
        ELSE 'Standard'
    END AS product_category
FROM products
WHERE (price < 100 OR stock_quantity > 150)
  AND is_active = TRUE
ORDER BY price DESC;
Result:
  product_name   |  price | stock_quantity | is_active |  product_category
-----------------+--------+----------------+-----------+---------------------
 Wireless Mouse  |  29.99 |            200 | t         | Premium OR High Stock
 USB-C Cable     |  12.99 |            500 | t         | Premium OR High Stock
(2 rows)
Explanation: Logical operators combine boolean expressions. AND requires both conditions TRUE, OR requires at least one TRUE, NOT negates the value. Parentheses control evaluation order. NULL values in logical operations propagate (TRUE AND NULL = NULL).

🎯 Example 4: String Operators (Concatenation and Pattern Matching)

-- String operations and pattern matching
SELECT 
    first_name,
    last_name,
    first_name || ' ' || last_name AS full_name,
    email,
    CASE
        WHEN email LIKE '%@gmail.com' THEN 'Gmail User'
        WHEN email LIKE '%@yahoo.com' THEN 'Yahoo User'
        WHEN email ILIKE '%@EXAMPLE.COM' THEN 'Example Domain'
        ELSE 'Other Email'
    END AS email_provider,
    phone ~ '^555-' AS starts_with_555
FROM customers
WHERE first_name LIKE 'J%'  -- Starts with 'J'
   OR last_name ILIKE '%son'  -- Ends with 'son' (case-insensitive)
ORDER BY customer_id
LIMIT 5;
Result:
 first_name | last_name |    full_name    |     email            | email_provider  | starts_with_555
------------+-----------+-----------------+----------------------+-----------------+-----------------
 John       | Doe       | John Doe        | john.doe@email.com   | Other Email     | t
 Jane       | Smith     | Jane Smith      | jane.smith@email.com | Other Email     | t
 Robert     | Johnson   | Robert Johnson  | robert.j@email.com   | Other Email     | t
(3 rows)
Explanation: || concatenates strings. LIKE uses % (any characters) and _ (single char) for patterns. ILIKE is case-insensitive. ~ operator matches POSIX regular expressions (^555- means "starts with 555-").

🎯 Example 5: Special Operators (IS NULL, BETWEEN, IN)

-- Special comparison operators
SELECT 
    order_id,
    customer_id,
    employee_id,
    order_date,
    total_amount,
    status,
    CASE 
        WHEN employee_id IS NULL THEN 'No Employee Assigned'
        WHEN employee_id IS NOT NULL THEN 'Employee: ' || employee_id::TEXT
    END AS employee_status,
    CASE
        WHEN total_amount BETWEEN 100 AND 500 THEN 'Medium Order'
        WHEN total_amount BETWEEN 500 AND 2000 THEN 'Large Order'
        ELSE 'Other'
    END AS order_size,
    status IN ('pending', 'processing') AS needs_action
FROM orders
WHERE order_date BETWEEN DATE '2026-01-01' AND DATE '2026-01-31'
  AND status IN ('pending', 'processing', 'shipped')
ORDER BY order_id
LIMIT 5;
Result:
 order_id | customer_id | employee_id |     order_date      | total_amount |  status    |    employee_status     | order_size  | needs_action
----------+-------------+-------------+---------------------+--------------+------------+------------------------+-------------+--------------
        1 |           1 |           5 | 2026-01-10 09:15:00 |      1464.68 | shipped    | Employee: 5            | Large Order | f
        3 |           1 |           5 | 2026-01-15 10:22:00 |       899.99 | processing | Employee: 5            | Large Order | t
        5 |           1 |        NULL | 2026-01-20 14:30:00 |       234.99 | pending    | No Employee Assigned   | Medium Order| t
(3 rows)
Explanation: IS NULL / IS NOT NULL test for NULL values (cannot use = NULL). BETWEEN a AND b tests if value is in range [a, b] inclusive. IN (list) tests membership in a set of values. These operators simplify common filtering patterns.

4.1.4 Special Characters

📖 Full Description

Special Characters are symbols that have specific syntactic meanings in SQL. These characters control the structure and parsing of SQL statements. Some special characters are operators (like + or *), while others serve as delimiters, separators, or markers that define the boundaries and relationships between different parts of a SQL statement.

Understanding special characters is crucial because misusing them can lead to syntax errors or unexpected behavior. For example, confusing single quotes (string literals) with double quotes (identifiers) or forgetting parentheses in function calls can cause queries to fail.

Syntax & Special Characters
-- Parentheses ( )
-- Purpose: Group expressions, define function arguments, specify precedence
SELECT (price * quantity) AS subtotal FROM order_items;
SELECT UPPER(first_name) FROM customers;

-- Brackets [ ]
-- Purpose: Array element access (subscripting)
SELECT array_column[1] FROM table_name;

-- Braces { }
-- Purpose: Not standard SQL, used in some escape sequences

-- Single Quotes ' '
-- Purpose: Delimit string literals
SELECT 'Hello World';

-- Double Quotes " "
-- Purpose: Delimit quoted identifiers (preserve case, allow special chars)
SELECT "ProductName" FROM "MyTable";

-- Semicolon ;
-- Purpose: Statement terminator
SELECT * FROM customers; SELECT * FROM orders;

-- Comma ,
-- Purpose: Separate list items (columns, values, parameters)
SELECT col1, col2, col3 FROM table_name;
INSERT INTO table_name VALUES (1, 'text', TRUE);

-- Period .
-- Purpose: Qualify names (schema.table.column), decimal point in numbers
SELECT schema_name.table_name.column_name;
SELECT 3.14159;

-- Asterisk *
-- Purpose: Multiplication operator, wildcard in SELECT
SELECT * FROM customers;
SELECT 5 * 3;

-- Colon :
-- Purpose: Type casting, array slicing, parameter placeholder
SELECT '2026-01-25'::DATE;
SELECT array_col[1:3];

-- Dollar Sign $
-- Purpose: Positional parameters, dollar-quoted strings
SELECT * FROM users WHERE id = $1;
SELECT $$String with 'quotes'$$;

-- Underscore _
-- Purpose: Valid in identifiers, LIKE pattern (single character)
SELECT product_name FROM products WHERE name LIKE 'A_C';

-- Percent %
-- Purpose: Modulo operator, LIKE pattern (any characters)
SELECT 10 % 3;
SELECT * FROM products WHERE name LIKE 'Laptop%';

Parameters

Grouping & Structure:

  • ( ) Parentheses: Group expressions, override precedence, enclose function arguments
  • [ ] Brackets: Array subscripting and element access
  • ; Semicolon: Terminates SQL statements (required in scripts, optional in interactive)
  • , Comma: Separates items in lists (SELECT columns, VALUES, function args)

String & Identifier Delimiters:

  • ' ' Single quotes: Delimit string constants (use '' to escape quote within)
  • " " Double quotes: Delimit identifiers (case-sensitive, allow keywords/spaces)
  • $$ Dollar quotes: Alternative string delimiter (no escaping needed)

Naming & Access:

  • . Period: Separates schema/table/column qualifiers, decimal point in numbers
  • _ Underscore: Valid in identifiers, wildcard for single character in LIKE
  • $ Dollar sign: Valid in identifiers, marks positional parameters ($1, $2)

Operators & Wildcards:

  • * Asterisk: Multiplication operator or "all columns" wildcard in SELECT
  • % Percent: Modulo operator or "any characters" wildcard in LIKE
  • : Colon: Type cast operator (::), array slice separator

🎯 Example 1: Parentheses for Grouping and Precedence

-- Parentheses control order of operations
SELECT 
    product_name,
    price,
    quantity,
    -- Without parentheses (multiplication first due to precedence)
    price * quantity + 10 AS calc_no_parens,
    -- With parentheses (addition first, then multiplication)
    price * (quantity + 10) AS calc_with_parens,
    -- Function call requires parentheses
    ROUND(price * quantity, 2) AS rounded_total
FROM (
    SELECT 'Widget' AS product_name, 19.99 AS price, 5 AS quantity
) AS sample_data;
Result:
 product_name | price | quantity | calc_no_parens | calc_with_parens | rounded_total
--------------+-------+----------+----------------+------------------+---------------
 Widget       | 19.99 |        5 |         109.95 |           299.85 |         99.95
(1 row)
Explanation: Parentheses ( ) override operator precedence. Without them: 19.99 * 5 + 10 = 109.95. With them: 19.99 * (5 + 10) = 299.85. Parentheses are also required for function arguments like ROUND(value, digits).

🎯 Example 2: Quotes - Single vs Double

-- Single quotes for string literals, double quotes for identifiers
CREATE TEMP TABLE quote_demo (
    "User Name" VARCHAR(50),   -- Double quotes: case-sensitive identifier
    description TEXT
);

INSERT INTO quote_demo VALUES 
    ('Alice', 'User with ''quoted'' text'),  -- Single quotes, '' to escape
    ('Bob', $$User with "double" quotes$$);  -- Dollar quotes, no escaping

-- Must use exact case for double-quoted identifiers
SELECT 
    "User Name",                          -- Correct: exact case match
    description,
    'Literal: ' || "User Name" AS combined  -- Mix: literal + identifier
FROM quote_demo;

-- These would FAIL:
-- SELECT "user name" FROM quote_demo;  -- Wrong case
-- SELECT User Name FROM quote_demo;     -- Space without quotes
Result:
 User Name |         description          |    combined
-----------+------------------------------+------------------
 Alice     | User with 'quoted' text      | Literal: Alice
 Bob       | User with "double" quotes    | Literal: Bob
(2 rows)
Explanation: Single quotes ' ' delimit string constants. Double quotes " " delimit identifiers (table/column names) and preserve case. Dollar quotes $$ $$ are alternate string delimiters requiring no escaping. Use '' (two single quotes) to escape a quote within a string.

🎯 Example 3: Comma, Semicolon, and Period

-- Comma separates list items
SELECT 
    first_name, 
    last_name, 
    email 
FROM customers 
WHERE customer_id IN (1, 2, 3);  -- Comma-separated list

-- Period qualifies names (schema.table.column)
SELECT 
    c.customer_id,
    c.first_name,
    o.order_id,
    o.total_amount
FROM public.customers c    -- schema.table qualification
JOIN public.orders o ON c.customer_id = o.customer_id
WHERE c.customer_id = 1;

-- Semicolon terminates statements (allows multiple in batch)
SELECT COUNT(*) FROM customers;
SELECT COUNT(*) FROM orders;
SELECT COUNT(*) FROM products;
Result (from JOIN query):
 customer_id | first_name | order_id | total_amount
-------------+------------+----------+--------------
           1 | John       |        1 |      1464.68
           1 | John       |        3 |       899.99
           1 | John       |        5 |       234.99
(3 rows)
Explanation: Comma (,) separates items in SELECT lists, VALUE lists, and WHERE IN clauses. Period (.) qualifies names with schema/table prefixes (public.customers.customer_id). Semicolon (;) terminates statements, allowing multiple commands in scripts.

🎯 Example 4: Array Brackets and Subscripting

-- Create table with array column
CREATE TEMP TABLE array_demo (
    id INTEGER,
    tags TEXT[],
    scores INTEGER[]
);

INSERT INTO array_demo VALUES 
    (1, ARRAY['electronics', 'computers', 'laptops'], ARRAY[95, 87, 92]),
    (2, ARRAY['books', 'fiction', 'bestseller'], ARRAY[88, 91, 85]);

-- Brackets [ ] access array elements (1-indexed)
SELECT 
    id,
    tags,
    tags[1] AS first_tag,      -- First element
    tags[2] AS second_tag,     -- Second element
    tags[1:2] AS first_two,    -- Slice: elements 1-2
    scores[3] AS third_score,  -- Third score
    array_length(tags, 1) AS tag_count
FROM array_demo;
Result:
 id |              tags              |  first_tag   | second_tag |       first_two        | third_score | tag_count
----+--------------------------------+--------------+------------+------------------------+-------------+-----------
  1 | {electronics,computers,laptops}| electronics  | computers  | {electronics,computers}|          92 |         3
  2 | {books,fiction,bestseller}     | books        | fiction    | {books,fiction}        |          85 |         3
(2 rows)
Explanation: Brackets [ ] access array elements. Arrays are 1-indexed: tags[1] is first element. Slicing uses colon: tags[1:2] returns elements 1 and 2. Single bracket accesses one element, range gives subarray.

🎯 Example 5: Colon for Type Casting and Dollar Sign for Parameters

-- Colon :: for type casting
SELECT 
    '2026-01-25' AS text_date,
    '2026-01-25'::DATE AS casted_date,
    '123' AS text_number,
    '123'::INTEGER AS casted_integer,
    'true' AS text_bool,
    'true'::BOOLEAN AS casted_bool,
    price::TEXT || ' dollars' AS price_as_text
FROM products
WHERE product_id = 1;

-- Dollar-quoted strings (no escaping needed)
SELECT 
    $$This string has 'single' and "double" quotes$$ AS dollar_quoted,
    $tag$Custom tag: even $$ works here$tag$ AS tagged_dollar;

-- Note: Positional parameters ($1, $2) used in prepared statements
-- Example (in application code):
-- PREPARE stmt AS SELECT * FROM products WHERE price > $1 AND category_id = $2;
-- EXECUTE stmt(100, 2);
Result (type casting):
 text_date  | casted_date | text_number | casted_integer | text_bool | casted_bool |   price_as_text
------------+-------------+-------------+----------------+-----------+-------------+------------------
 2026-01-25 | 2026-01-25  | 123         |            123 | true      | t           | 1299.99 dollars
(1 row)

-- Dollar-quoted result:
                    dollar_quoted                    |            tagged_dollar
-----------------------------------------------------+---------------------------------
 This string has 'single' and "double" quotes        | Custom tag: even $$ works here
(1 row)
Explanation: Double colon (::) casts values between types ('2026-01-25'::DATE converts text to date). Dollar quotes ($$ $$) delimit strings without escaping - useful for complex strings with quotes. $1, $2 are positional parameters in prepared statements (common in application code).

4.1.5 Comments

📖 Full Description

Comments are text annotations that are ignored by the SQL parser. They serve as documentation to explain code logic, provide context, disable code temporarily, or add metadata about queries. Comments make SQL code more maintainable and understandable for other developers (and your future self).

PostgreSQL supports two comment styles: single-line comments starting with -- that extend to the end of the line, and multi-line (block) comments enclosed in /* */ that can span multiple lines. Both styles can be nested within each other (though nested block comments require special handling).

Syntax
-- Single-line comment
-- This is a comment that continues to end of line
SELECT column_name  -- Inline comment after code
FROM table_name;

/* Multi-line comment
   This comment spans
   multiple lines */

/* Block comment can also be
 * formatted with asterisks
 * for readability */

/* Nested comments (PostgreSQL specific)
   /* Inner comment */
   Outer comment continues
*/

-- Common uses:
-- TODO: Implement error handling
-- FIXME: Optimize this query
-- NOTE: This uses deprecated syntax
-- @author: John Doe
-- @date: 2026-01-25

Parameters

Single-Line Comments (--):

  • Start marker: Two hyphens --
  • Scope: From -- to end of line (newline terminates comment)
  • Placement: Can appear at start of line or after SQL code
  • Use cases: Brief annotations, temporarily disabling single lines, inline explanations
  • Nesting: Can contain any text including /* or */ without special meaning

Multi-Line Comments (/* */):

  • Start marker: Slash-asterisk /*
  • End marker: Asterisk-slash */
  • Scope: From opening /* to matching closing */
  • Span: Can span multiple lines, paragraphs, or even pages
  • Use cases: Long explanations, license headers, commenting out code blocks
  • Nesting: PostgreSQL supports nested /* /* */ */ comments (inner pairs must match)
  • Warning: Cannot be used within string literals

🎯 Example 1: Single-Line Comments

-- Get all active customers who registered this year
-- This query is used by the monthly report
SELECT 
    customer_id,        -- Primary key
    first_name,         -- Customer's given name
    last_name,          -- Customer's family name
    email,              -- Contact email address
    registration_date   -- Date joined system
FROM customers
WHERE is_active = TRUE  -- Only active accounts
  AND EXTRACT(YEAR FROM registration_date) = 2026  -- Current year only
ORDER BY registration_date DESC  -- Newest first
LIMIT 10;  -- Top 10 customers
Result:
 customer_id | first_name | last_name |      email          | registration_date
-------------+------------+-----------+---------------------+-------------------
           5 | Michael    | Brown     | michael.b@email.com | 2026-01-05 ...
           4 | Emily      | Williams  | emily.w@email.com   | 2026-01-04 ...
           3 | Robert     | Johnson   | robert.j@email.com  | 2026-01-03 ...
           2 | Jane       | Smith     | jane.smith@...      | 2026-01-02 ...
           1 | John       | Doe       | john.doe@email.com  | 2026-01-01 ...
(5 rows)
Explanation: Single-line comments start with -- and continue to line end. They can appear before code (header comments), at line end (inline documentation), or disable code. Comments are stripped by parser before execution.

🎯 Example 2: Multi-Line Block Comments

/*
 * Product Sales Analysis Query
 * =============================
 * 
 * Purpose: Calculate total revenue and units sold per product
 * Author: Database Team
 * Date: 2026-01-25
 * 
 * Dependencies:
 *   - products table (catalog data)
 *   - order_items table (sales transactions)
 * 
 * Performance: ~50ms on 1M records
 * Index: uses idx_order_items_product for optimal performance
 */

SELECT 
    p.product_id,
    p.product_name,
    /* Calculate metrics */
    COUNT(oi.order_item_id) AS times_sold,
    SUM(oi.quantity) AS total_units,
    ROUND(SUM(oi.line_total), 2) AS total_revenue
FROM products p
LEFT JOIN order_items oi ON p.product_id = oi.product_id
GROUP BY p.product_id, p.product_name
HAVING SUM(oi.line_total) > 0  /* Only products with sales */
ORDER BY total_revenue DESC
LIMIT 5;
Result:
 product_id |  product_name   | times_sold | total_units | total_revenue
------------+-----------------+------------+-------------+---------------
          1 | Laptop Pro 15"  |         45 |          52 |      67599.48
          3 | Smartphone X    |         38 |          42 |      37799.58
          5 | Mechanical Kbd  |         28 |          31 |       4649.69
(3 rows)
Explanation: Block comments /* */ span multiple lines, ideal for query headers, detailed documentation, or license information. They can contain formatted text, lists, and metadata. Everything between /* and */ is ignored by PostgreSQL.

🎯 Example 3: Temporarily Disabling Code

-- Development query: testing different filters

SELECT 
    order_id,
    customer_id,
    order_date,
    total_amount,
    status
FROM orders
WHERE 
    -- Original filter (too restrictive):
    -- status = 'completed'
    
    -- New filter (testing):
    status IN ('completed', 'shipped')
    
    /* Alternative filters to try:
    AND total_amount > 100
    AND EXTRACT(MONTH FROM order_date) = 1
    */
    
    -- Temporary date range for testing:
    AND order_date >= '2026-01-01'
ORDER BY order_date DESC
LIMIT 5;
Result:
 order_id | customer_id |     order_date      | total_amount | status
----------+-------------+---------------------+--------------+----------
        5 |           1 | 2026-01-20 14:30:00 |       234.99 | pending
        3 |           1 | 2026-01-15 10:22:00 |       899.99 | processing
        1 |           1 | 2026-01-10 09:15:00 |      1464.68 | shipped
(3 rows)
Explanation: Comments are perfect for temporarily disabling code during development. -- comments out single lines, /* */ blocks disable multiple lines. This is safer than deleting code you might need later. Version control is still recommended for tracking changes.

🎯 Example 4: Nested Comments (PostgreSQL Specific)

/* Outer comment starts here
   
   This is a complex query with multiple sections
   
   /* Inner comment: This section calculates revenue
      Formula: quantity * unit_price * (1 - discount)
      Note: discount is stored as percentage */
   
   Back to outer comment - nesting works in PostgreSQL!
   
   /* Another nested section
      /* Even deeper nesting! */
      Middle level
   */
   
   End of outer comment
*/

SELECT 
    product_name,
    price
FROM products
WHERE price > 100;
Result:
  product_name   |  price
-----------------+---------
 Laptop Pro 15"  | 1299.99
 Smartphone X    |  899.99
 Mechanical Kbd  |  149.99
(3 rows)
Explanation: PostgreSQL allows nested /* */ comments, meaning you can have /* /* nested */ comment */ structures. Each /* must have a matching */. This is useful for commenting out sections of code that already contain comments. Not all databases support this feature.

🎯 Example 5: Practical Documentation Style

/*
 * ============================================================================
 * FUNCTION: Calculate Customer Lifetime Value
 * ============================================================================
 * Description:
 *   Computes total revenue and order count per customer, ranking customers
 *   by their lifetime value to identify top spenders.
 *
 * Business Logic:
 *   - Lifetime Value = SUM(all order totals)
 *   - Only counts completed/delivered orders
 *   - Excludes cancelled/refunded orders
 *
 * Usage:
 *   Used by: Marketing dashboard, VIP customer reports
 *   Frequency: Daily batch job at 3 AM
 *   Runtime: ~200ms
 *
 * Maintenance Notes:
 *   - Last optimized: 2026-01-15 (added index on orders.customer_id)
 *   - TODO: Add filtering by date range parameter
 *   - FIXME: Handle edge case for negative refunds
 * ============================================================================
 */

SELECT 
    c.customer_id,
    -- Personal information
    c.first_name || ' ' || c.last_name AS customer_name,
    c.email,
    
    -- Metrics
    COUNT(o.order_id) AS total_orders,
    COALESCE(SUM(o.total_amount), 0) AS lifetime_value,
    ROUND(AVG(o.total_amount), 2) AS avg_order_value,
    
    -- Rankings
    RANK() OVER (ORDER BY SUM(o.total_amount) DESC) AS value_rank
FROM customers c
LEFT JOIN orders o 
    ON c.customer_id = o.customer_id 
    AND o.status IN ('completed', 'delivered')  -- Only successful orders
WHERE c.is_active = TRUE  -- Active customers only
GROUP BY c.customer_id, c.first_name, c.last_name, c.email
HAVING SUM(o.total_amount) > 0  -- Must have purchases
ORDER BY lifetime_value DESC
LIMIT 10;  -- Top 10 customers
Result:
 customer_id | customer_name |      email          | total_orders | lifetime_value | avg_order_value | value_rank
-------------+---------------+---------------------+--------------+----------------+-----------------+------------
           1 | John Doe      | john.doe@email.com  |            3 |        2599.66 |          866.55 |          1
(1 row)
Explanation: Professional documentation style combines header block comments (purpose, logic, usage) with inline comments (explain columns, conditions). This makes queries self-documenting and maintainable. Include author, date, dependencies, TODOs, and business logic explanations for complex queries.

4.1.6 Operator Precedence

📖 Full Description

Operator Precedence determines the order in which operators are evaluated in expressions containing multiple operators. When an expression like 5 + 3 * 2 is encountered, should it be evaluated as (5 + 3) * 2 = 16 or 5 + (3 * 2) = 11? Precedence rules establish that multiplication has higher precedence than addition, so the result is 11.

Understanding precedence is crucial for writing correct SQL expressions and avoiding subtle bugs. While you can always use parentheses to explicitly control evaluation order (and this is often recommended for clarity), knowing precedence rules helps you read others' code and write more concise expressions when appropriate.

Operators are evaluated from highest precedence to lowest. Operators with equal precedence are generally evaluated left-to-right (left-associative), though some operators like exponentiation are right-associative.

Operator Precedence (Highest to Lowest)
-- 1. HIGHEST: Table/column name separator
schema.table.column

-- 2. Array element selection
array[index]

-- 3. Unary plus, minus, and other unary operators
-value, +value, ~ bitwise_not

-- 4. Exponentiation
base ^ exponent

-- 5. Multiplication, division, modulo
a * b, a / b, a % b

-- 6. Addition, subtraction
a + b, a - b

-- 7. Other operators (left to right)
||  (concatenation)
<< >> (bit shift)
& | # (bitwise operations)

-- 8. Comparison operators (non-associative)
<, >, <=, >=, =, <>, !=
BETWEEN, IN, LIKE, ILIKE, SIMILAR TO

-- 9. IS tests
IS NULL, IS NOT NULL, IS TRUE, IS FALSE, IS DISTINCT FROM

-- 10. NOT (logical negation)
NOT expression

-- 11. AND (logical conjunction)
expr1 AND expr2

-- 12. LOWEST: OR (logical disjunction)
expr1 OR expr2

-- Parentheses override all precedence
(expression)

Key Principles

Precedence Levels:

  • Arithmetic first: *, /, % before +, -
  • Comparisons middle: <, >, =, LIKE, BETWEEN, IN evaluated after arithmetic
  • Logical last: NOT before AND, AND before OR
  • Associativity: Most operators are left-associative (a - b - c means (a - b) - c)
  • Exception: Exponentiation (^) is right-associative (2^3^2 means 2^(3^2) = 512)

Best Practices:

  • Use parentheses: For clarity, even when not technically required
  • Avoid complexity: Break complex expressions into simpler parts
  • Be explicit: Don't rely on obscure precedence rules in production code
  • Test assumptions: When in doubt, test with simple examples
  • Document: Comment complex expressions explaining evaluation order

🎯 Example 1: Arithmetic Precedence (* / before + -)

-- Multiplication and division before addition and subtraction
SELECT 
    5 + 3 * 2 AS calc1,           -- = 5 + 6 = 11 (not 16)
    10 - 6 / 2 AS calc2,          -- = 10 - 3 = 7 (not 2)
    20 / 4 * 2 AS calc3,          -- = 5 * 2 = 10 (left to right)
    10 % 3 + 1 AS calc4,          -- = 1 + 1 = 2
    
    -- With parentheses to change order
    (5 + 3) * 2 AS calc1_parens,  -- = 8 * 2 = 16
    (10 - 6) / 2 AS calc2_parens, -- = 4 / 2 = 2
    20 / (4 * 2) AS calc3_parens, -- = 20 / 8 = 2.5
    10 % (3 + 1) AS calc4_parens; -- = 10 % 4 = 2
Result:
 calc1 | calc2 | calc3 | calc4 | calc1_parens | calc2_parens | calc3_parens | calc4_parens
-------+-------+-------+-------+--------------+--------------+--------------+--------------
    11 |     7 |    10 |     2 |           16 |            2 |          2.5 |            2
(1 row)
Explanation: Multiplication (*), division (/), and modulo (%) have higher precedence than addition (+) and subtraction (-). Expression 5 + 3 * 2 evaluates as 5 + (3 * 2) = 11. Operators of equal precedence (*, /) evaluate left-to-right: 20 / 4 * 2 = (20 / 4) * 2 = 10.

🎯 Example 2: Comparison and Logical Operator Precedence

-- Comparison before logical AND before OR
SELECT 
    product_name,
    price,
    stock_quantity,
    is_active,
    -- Without parentheses (default precedence)
    price > 100 AND stock_quantity > 50 OR is_active = FALSE AS condition1,
    -- Evaluation: (price > 100 AND stock_quantity > 50) OR (is_active = FALSE)
    
    -- With parentheses for different logic
    price > 100 AND (stock_quantity > 50 OR is_active = FALSE) AS condition2,
    
    -- Comparison operators first, then AND, then OR
    price > 50 AND price < 200 OR stock_quantity = 0 AS condition3
FROM products
WHERE product_id <= 5
ORDER BY product_id;
Result:
  product_name   |  price  | stock_quantity | is_active | condition1 | condition2 | condition3
-----------------+---------+----------------+-----------+------------+------------+------------
 Laptop Pro 15"  | 1299.99 |             50 | t         | f          | f          | f
 Wireless Mouse  |   29.99 |            200 | t         | f          | f          | f
 Smartphone X    |  899.99 |            100 | t         | t          | t          | f
 USB-C Cable     |   12.99 |            500 | t         | f          | f          | f
 Mechanical Kbd  |  149.99 |             75 | t         | t          | t          | t
(5 rows)
Explanation: Precedence order: comparisons (>, <, =) → NOT → AND → OR. Expression A AND B OR C means (A AND B) OR C, not A AND (B OR C). The Mechanical Keyboard matches condition3 because (149.99 > 50 AND 149.99 < 200) = TRUE, so TRUE OR FALSE = TRUE.

🎯 Example 3: NOT Operator Precedence

-- NOT has higher precedence than AND and OR
SELECT 
    product_name,
    price,
    is_active,
    -- NOT evaluated first
    NOT is_active AND price > 100 AS condition1,
    -- Means: (NOT is_active) AND (price > 100)
    
    -- With parentheses to negate the entire expression
    NOT (is_active AND price > 100) AS condition2,
    -- Means: NOT (is_active AND price > 100)
    
    -- NOT before AND before OR
    NOT is_active OR price < 50 AND stock_quantity > 100 AS condition3
    -- Means: (NOT is_active) OR ((price < 50) AND (stock_quantity > 100))
FROM products
WHERE product_id <= 5
ORDER BY product_id;
Result:
  product_name   |  price  | is_active | condition1 | condition2 | condition3
-----------------+---------+-----------+------------+------------+------------
 Laptop Pro 15"  | 1299.99 | t         | f          | f          | f
 Wireless Mouse  |   29.99 | t         | f          | t          | t
 Smartphone X    |  899.99 | t         | f          | f          | f
 USB-C Cable     |   12.99 | t         | f          | t          | t
 Mechanical Kbd  |  149.99 | t         | f          | f          | f
(5 rows)
Explanation: NOT has higher precedence than AND and OR. NOT is_active AND price > 100 evaluates as (NOT is_active) AND (price > 100). To negate the entire expression, use parentheses: NOT (is_active AND price > 100). Condition3 shows: (NOT is_active) OR ((price < 50) AND (stock_quantity > 100)).

🎯 Example 4: String Concatenation vs Arithmetic

-- Concatenation (||) has different precedence than arithmetic
SELECT 
    product_name,
    price,
    stock_quantity,
    -- Arithmetic before concatenation
    'Price: $' || price + 10 AS calc1,
    -- Means: 'Price: $' || (price + 10)
    
    -- Concatenation is left-to-right
    'Product: ' || product_name || ' - Stock: ' || stock_quantity AS description,
    
    -- Type casting has high precedence
    'Total: $' || (price * stock_quantity)::TEXT AS total_value,
    
    -- Be careful with mixed types
    'SKU-' || product_id AS sku_code
FROM products
WHERE product_id <= 3
ORDER BY product_id;
Result:
  product_name   |  price  | stock_quantity |      calc1       |           description               |    total_value    | sku_code
-----------------+---------+----------------+------------------+-------------------------------------+-------------------+----------
