    ARRAY_LENGTH(ARRAY_AGG(o.order_id), 1) AS array_length
FROM customers c
JOIN orders o ON c.customer_id = o.customer_id
GROUP BY c.customer_id, c.first_name, c.last_name
HAVING COUNT(o.order_id) > 1
ORDER BY c.customer_id;
Result:
 customer_id | customer_name | order_count | order_ids | order_statuses      | order_id_array | amounts_desc        | unique_statuses      | array_length
-------------+---------------+-------------+-----------+---------------------+----------------+---------------------+----------------------+--------------
           1 | John Smith    |           2 | 1, 3      | delivered, pending  | {1,3}          | {899.99,1359.97}    | delivered, pending   |            2
           2 | Sarah Johnson |           2 | 2, 6      | delivered, shipped  | {2,6}          | {199.98,1329.98}    | delivered, shipped   |            2
(2 rows)
Explanation: STRING_AGG concatenates values into a delimited string. The ORDER BY inside STRING_AGG controls the concatenation order. ARRAY_AGG collects values into a PostgreSQL array. Both support DISTINCT to remove duplicates before aggregation. These aggregates are invaluable for creating comma-separated lists, building arrays for further processing, or creating human-readable summaries of grouped data. The ORDER BY clause ensures predictable, meaningful results.

🎯 Example 5: HAVING Clause for Filtering Aggregated Results

-- Using HAVING to filter groups based on aggregate conditions
SELECT 
    p.category_id,
    c.category_name,
    COUNT(p.product_id) AS product_count,
    AVG(p.price) AS avg_price,
    SUM(p.stock_quantity) AS total_stock,
    MIN(p.price) AS cheapest,
    MAX(p.price) AS most_expensive,
    ROUND(SUM(p.price * p.stock_quantity), 2) AS inventory_value
FROM products p
JOIN categories c ON p.category_id = c.category_id
WHERE p.is_active = TRUE
GROUP BY p.category_id, c.category_name
-- HAVING filters groups after aggregation
HAVING COUNT(p.product_id) >= 3           -- At least 3 products
    AND AVG(p.price) > 100                 -- Average price over 100
    AND SUM(p.stock_quantity) >= 500       -- Total stock at least 500
ORDER BY inventory_value DESC;
Result:
 category_id | category_name | product_count | avg_price | total_stock | cheapest | most_expensive | inventory_value
-------------+---------------+---------------+-----------+-------------+----------+----------------+-----------------
           1 | Electronics   |             6 |    511.66 |        1000 |    12.99 |        1299.99 |       153498.00
(1 row)
Explanation: The HAVING clause filters groups after GROUP BY and aggregation, while WHERE filters rows before grouping. HAVING can reference aggregate functions (COUNT, AVG, SUM) because it operates after aggregation. Use WHERE to filter individual rows (reduce data before grouping), use HAVING to filter groups (keep only groups meeting aggregate criteria). HAVING is essential for queries like "find categories with more than X products" or "customers who spent over $Y".

4.2.8 Window Function Calls

📖 Full Description

Window Functions perform calculations across sets of table rows that are related to the current row, unlike aggregate functions which return a single value for a group. Window functions use the OVER clause to define the "window" of rows for each calculation. This allows you to compute running totals, rankings, moving averages, and comparisons with previous/next rows while retaining all individual rows in the result.

The power of window functions lies in their ability to perform complex analytics without collapsing rows like GROUP BY does. You can rank products by price within each category, calculate cumulative sums, find row-to-row differences, or identify the top N items per group - all while keeping the detail-level data. Window functions are essential for analytics, reporting, and data analysis tasks.

Window functions include ranking functions (ROW_NUMBER, RANK, DENSE_RANK), offset functions (LAG, LEAD), and aggregate functions used as window functions (SUM, AVG, COUNT with OVER). The OVER clause can include PARTITION BY (grouping), ORDER BY (ordering within partition), and frame specifications (ROWS BETWEEN / RANGE BETWEEN) for fine-grained control.

Window Function Syntax
-- Basic window function syntax
function_name() OVER (window_specification)

-- Window specification components
OVER (
    [PARTITION BY partition_expression]
    [ORDER BY sort_expression]
    [frame_clause]
)

-- Ranking functions
ROW_NUMBER() OVER (ORDER BY column)
RANK() OVER (ORDER BY column)
DENSE_RANK() OVER (ORDER BY column)
NTILE(n) OVER (ORDER BY column)

-- Offset functions
LAG(column, offset, default) OVER (ORDER BY column)
LEAD(column, offset, default) OVER (ORDER BY column)
FIRST_VALUE(column) OVER (ORDER BY column)
LAST_VALUE(column) OVER (ORDER BY column)

-- Aggregate functions as window functions
SUM(column) OVER (ORDER BY column)
AVG(column) OVER (PARTITION BY category ORDER BY date)
COUNT(*) OVER (PARTITION BY group_col)

-- Window with PARTITION BY
SUM(amount) OVER (PARTITION BY customer_id ORDER BY order_date)

-- Frame specification
SUM(amount) OVER (
    ORDER BY date
    ROWS BETWEEN 2 PRECEDING AND CURRENT ROW
)

-- Named windows (for reuse)
SELECT 
    column,
    SUM(value) OVER w,
    AVG(value) OVER w
FROM table
WINDOW w AS (PARTITION BY category ORDER BY date);

Window Function Types

Ranking Functions:

  • ROW_NUMBER(): Unique sequential number for each row (1, 2, 3...)
  • RANK(): Rank with gaps after ties (1, 2, 2, 4...)
  • DENSE_RANK(): Rank without gaps after ties (1, 2, 2, 3...)
  • NTILE(n): Divides rows into n buckets (quartiles, percentiles)

Offset Functions:

  • LAG(col, n): Value from n rows before current row
  • LEAD(col, n): Value from n rows after current row
  • FIRST_VALUE(col): First value in window frame
  • LAST_VALUE(col): Last value in window frame

Key Concepts:

  • PARTITION BY: Divides rows into groups (like GROUP BY but keeps all rows)
  • ORDER BY: Defines ordering within partition (affects frame calculation)
  • Frame clause: Defines which rows in partition are included in calculation

🎯 Example 1: Ranking Functions (ROW_NUMBER, RANK, DENSE_RANK)

-- Different ranking approaches for ordered data
SELECT 
    product_id,
    product_name,
    category_id,
    price,
    -- ROW_NUMBER: unique sequential number
    ROW_NUMBER() OVER (ORDER BY price DESC) AS row_num,
    -- RANK: same rank for ties, gaps after ties
    RANK() OVER (ORDER BY price DESC) AS rank,
    -- DENSE_RANK: same rank for ties, no gaps
    DENSE_RANK() OVER (ORDER BY price DESC) AS dense_rank,
    -- Ranking within each category
    ROW_NUMBER() OVER (PARTITION BY category_id ORDER BY price DESC) AS category_row,
    RANK() OVER (PARTITION BY category_id ORDER BY price DESC) AS category_rank
FROM products
WHERE is_active = TRUE
ORDER BY price DESC
LIMIT 8;
Result:
 product_id |  product_name  | category_id |  price  | row_num | rank | dense_rank | category_row | category_rank
------------+----------------+-------------+---------+---------+------+------------+--------------+---------------
          1 | Laptop Pro 15" |           1 | 1299.99 |       1 |    1 |          1 |            1 |             1
          3 | Smartphone X   |           1 |  899.99 |       2 |    2 |          2 |            2 |             2
          7 | Monitor 27"    |           1 |  399.99 |       3 |    3 |          3 |            3 |             3
          9 | Desk Chair Pro |           2 |  299.99 |       4 |    4 |          4 |            1 |             1
          5 | Mechanical Kbd |           1 |  149.99 |       5 |    5 |          5 |            4 |             4
          8 | Standing Desk  |           2 |  149.99 |       6 |    5 |          5 |            2 |             2
         10 | Office Table   |           2 |   99.99 |       7 |    7 |          6 |            3 |             3
          2 | Wireless Mouse |           1 |   29.99 |       8 |    8 |          7 |            5 |             5
(8 rows)
Explanation: ROW_NUMBER assigns unique sequential numbers regardless of ties. RANK gives same rank to ties but skips numbers (1, 2, 2, 4). DENSE_RANK gives same rank to ties without skipping (1, 2, 2, 3). PARTITION BY category_id restarts ranking for each category. Notice products 5 and 8 both cost 149.99 - global RANK shows tie at position 5 (then jumps to 7), while DENSE_RANK shows 5, 6. Window functions preserve all rows unlike GROUP BY.

🎯 Example 2: LAG and LEAD for Row-to-Row Comparisons

-- Comparing current row with previous/next rows
SELECT 
    order_id,
    customer_id,
    order_date,
    total_amount,
    -- LAG: get value from previous row
    LAG(total_amount, 1) OVER (PARTITION BY customer_id ORDER BY order_date) AS previous_amount,
    LAG(order_date, 1) OVER (PARTITION BY customer_id ORDER BY order_date) AS previous_date,
    -- LEAD: get value from next row
    LEAD(total_amount, 1) OVER (PARTITION BY customer_id ORDER BY order_date) AS next_amount,
    -- Calculate difference from previous order
    total_amount - LAG(total_amount, 1, 0) OVER (PARTITION BY customer_id ORDER BY order_date) AS amount_change,
    -- Days since previous order
    order_date - LAG(order_date, 1) OVER (PARTITION BY customer_id ORDER BY order_date) AS days_since_last
FROM orders
WHERE customer_id <= 2
ORDER BY customer_id, order_date;
Result:
 order_id | customer_id |      order_date     | total_amount | previous_amount |   previous_date     | next_amount | amount_change | days_since_last
----------+-------------+---------------------+--------------+-----------------+---------------------+-------------+---------------+-----------------
        1 |           1 | 2026-01-10 15:30:00 |      1359.97 | NULL            | NULL                |      899.99 |       1359.97 | NULL
        3 |           1 | 2026-01-15 14:20:00 |       899.99 |         1359.97 | 2026-01-10 15:30:00 | NULL        |       -459.98 | 4 days 22:50:00
        2 |           2 | 2026-01-12 10:30:00 |      1329.98 | NULL            | NULL                |      199.98 |       1329.98 | NULL
        6 |           2 | 2026-01-22 14:00:00 |       199.98 |         1329.98 | 2026-01-12 10:30:00 | NULL        |      -1130.00 | 10 days 03:30:00
(4 rows)
Explanation: LAG(column, offset, default) accesses the value from a previous row. LEAD accesses a following row. offset=1 means immediate previous/next, offset=2 means two rows away, etc. The third parameter is the default value when no previous/next row exists (first/last rows). PARTITION BY customer_id restarts the window for each customer. These functions are perfect for calculating changes, trends, differences, and time-series analysis.

🎯 Example 3: Running Totals with Window Aggregates

-- Cumulative calculations using window aggregate functions
SELECT 
    order_id,
    customer_id,
    order_date::DATE AS order_date,
    total_amount,
    -- Running total (cumulative sum)
    SUM(total_amount) OVER (PARTITION BY customer_id ORDER BY order_date) AS running_total,
    -- Running average
    ROUND(AVG(total_amount) OVER (PARTITION BY customer_id ORDER BY order_date), 2) AS running_avg,
    -- Running count
    COUNT(*) OVER (PARTITION BY customer_id ORDER BY order_date) AS order_number,
    -- Running min and max
    MIN(total_amount) OVER (PARTITION BY customer_id ORDER BY order_date) AS min_so_far,
    MAX(total_amount) OVER (PARTITION BY customer_id ORDER BY order_date) AS max_so_far,
    -- Percentage of customer's total spending
    ROUND(100.0 * total_amount / SUM(total_amount) OVER (PARTITION BY customer_id), 2) AS pct_of_total
FROM orders
WHERE customer_id <= 2
ORDER BY customer_id, order_date;
Result:
 order_id | customer_id | order_date | total_amount | running_total | running_avg | order_number | min_so_far | max_so_far | pct_of_total
----------+-------------+------------+--------------+---------------+-------------+--------------+------------+------------+--------------
        1 |           1 | 2026-01-10 |      1359.97 |       1359.97 |     1359.97 |            1 |    1359.97 |    1359.97 |        60.17
        3 |           1 | 2026-01-15 |       899.99 |       2259.96 |     1129.98 |            2 |     899.99 |    1359.97 |        39.83
        2 |           2 | 2026-01-12 |      1329.98 |       1329.98 |     1329.98 |            1 |    1329.98 |    1329.98 |        86.93
        6 |           2 | 2026-01-22 |       199.98 |       1529.96 |      764.99 |            2 |     199.98 |    1329.98 |        13.07
(4 rows)
Explanation: Aggregate functions with OVER and ORDER BY create running calculations. SUM() OVER (...ORDER BY date) accumulates values from first row to current row in ordered partition. Without frame specification, default is "RANGE BETWEEN UNBOUNDED PRECEDING AND CURRENT ROW" (all rows up to current). Running totals, moving averages, and cumulative statistics are common in financial reports, dashboards, and time-series analysis. Each row shows its position in the running calculation.

🎯 Example 4: Window Frames (ROWS BETWEEN) for Moving Averages

-- Using frame specifications for sliding window calculations
SELECT 
    order_id,
    order_date::DATE AS order_date,
    total_amount,
    -- 3-row moving average (current + 2 preceding)
    ROUND(AVG(total_amount) OVER (
        ORDER BY order_date
        ROWS BETWEEN 2 PRECEDING AND CURRENT ROW
    ), 2) AS moving_avg_3,
    -- 5-row centered moving average
    ROUND(AVG(total_amount) OVER (
        ORDER BY order_date
        ROWS BETWEEN 2 PRECEDING AND 2 FOLLOWING
    ), 2) AS centered_avg_5,
    -- Sum of current and next 2 rows
    ROUND(SUM(total_amount) OVER (
        ORDER BY order_date
        ROWS BETWEEN CURRENT ROW AND 2 FOLLOWING
    ), 2) AS forward_sum_3,
    -- Count rows in moving window
    COUNT(*) OVER (
        ORDER BY order_date
        ROWS BETWEEN 2 PRECEDING AND CURRENT ROW
    ) AS window_size
FROM orders
ORDER BY order_date
LIMIT 7;
Result:
 order_id | order_date | total_amount | moving_avg_3 | centered_avg_5 | forward_sum_3 | window_size
----------+------------+--------------+--------------+----------------+---------------+-------------
        1 | 2026-01-10 |      1359.97 |      1359.97 |        1196.65 |       3589.94 |           1
        2 | 2026-01-12 |      1329.98 |      1344.98 |        1046.65 |       2379.96 |           2
        3 | 2026-01-15 |       899.99 |      1196.65 |         856.64 |       1199.96 |           3
        4 | 2026-01-18 |       149.99 |       793.32 |         624.98 |        392.95 |           3
        5 | 2026-01-20 |        42.98 |       364.32 |         548.14 |        242.96 |           3
        6 | 2026-01-22 |       199.98 |       130.98 |         498.58 |        899.94 |           3
        7 | 2026-01-24 |       699.96 |       314.31 |         538.58 |       1499.90 |           3
(7 rows)
Explanation: Frame clauses (ROWS BETWEEN) precisely define which rows are included in window calculation. "2 PRECEDING AND CURRENT ROW" includes current row plus 2 before it (3-row window). "2 PRECEDING AND 2 FOLLOWING" creates 5-row centered window. Frames are essential for moving averages, smoothing, and time-series analysis. First rows have smaller windows until enough preceding rows exist. ROWS counts physical rows; RANGE (alternative) uses value-based ranges.

🎯 Example 5: NTILE for Percentile Buckets and Top N per Group

-- Dividing data into buckets and finding top performers per category
SELECT 
    product_id,
    product_name,
    category_id,
    price,
    -- Divide products into quartiles (4 buckets)
    NTILE(4) OVER (ORDER BY price DESC) AS price_quartile,
    -- Divide within each category
    NTILE(3) OVER (PARTITION BY category_id ORDER BY price DESC) AS category_tercile,
    -- Rank within category
    ROW_NUMBER() OVER (PARTITION BY category_id ORDER BY price DESC) AS category_rank,
    -- Total products in category
    COUNT(*) OVER (PARTITION BY category_id) AS products_in_category
FROM products
WHERE is_active = TRUE
ORDER BY category_id, price DESC;
Result:
 product_id |  product_name  | category_id |  price  | price_quartile | category_tercile | category_rank | products_in_category
------------+----------------+-------------+---------+----------------+------------------+---------------+----------------------
          1 | Laptop Pro 15" |           1 | 1299.99 |              1 |                1 |             1 |                    6
          3 | Smartphone X   |           1 |  899.99 |              1 |                1 |             2 |                    6
          7 | Monitor 27"    |           1 |  399.99 |              2 |                2 |             3 |                    6
          5 | Mechanical Kbd |           1 |  149.99 |              3 |                2 |             4 |                    6
          2 | Wireless Mouse |           1 |   29.99 |              3 |                3 |             5 |                    6
          4 | USB-C Cable    |           1 |   12.99 |              4 |                3 |             6 |                    6
          9 | Desk Chair Pro |           2 |  299.99 |              2 |                1 |             1 |                    3
          8 | Standing Desk  |           2 |  149.99 |              3 |                2 |             2 |                    3
         10 | Office Table   |           2 |   99.99 |              4 |                3 |             3 |                    3
(9 rows)
Explanation: NTILE(n) divides ordered rows into n approximately equal buckets. NTILE(4) creates quartiles (Q1=top 25%, Q2=25-50%, Q3=50-75%, Q4=bottom 25%). With PARTITION BY, NTILE divides within each partition. This is useful for segmenting data (high/medium/low performers), identifying top/bottom percentages, or creating balanced groups. Combined with WHERE category_rank <= 3, you can find "top 3 products per category" - a common analytics pattern that's difficult without window functions.

4.2.9 Type Casts

📖 Full Description

Type Casts convert values from one data type to another. PostgreSQL is strongly typed, meaning each value has a specific type, and operations between incompatible types require explicit conversion. Type casting ensures data compatibility and enables operations that would otherwise fail due to type mismatches. PostgreSQL provides multiple casting syntaxes and handles many conversions automatically through implicit casting.

Casting is essential when working with heterogeneous data - reading strings that represent numbers, formatting dates as text, converting between numeric types, or preparing data for specific functions. PostgreSQL's type system is rich, with types for numbers, text, dates/times, JSON, arrays, and more. Understanding when and how to cast ensures your queries handle data correctly and efficiently.

Some casts are safe and lossless (INTEGER to BIGINT), while others may lose precision (NUMERIC to INTEGER) or fail if the value cannot be converted (text 'abc' to INTEGER). PostgreSQL provides three casting syntaxes: CAST(x AS type), x::type, and type 'literal'. Each has appropriate use cases, with ::type being the most concise and commonly used in PostgreSQL-specific code.

Type Cast Syntax
-- SQL standard CAST function
CAST(expression AS target_type)
CAST('123' AS INTEGER)
CAST(price AS TEXT)

-- PostgreSQL :: operator (most common)
expression::target_type
'123'::INTEGER
price::TEXT
order_date::DATE

-- Type constructor (for literals)
type 'literal_value'
DATE '2026-01-25'
TIMESTAMP '2026-01-25 14:30:00'
INTERVAL '30 days'

-- Common type casts
-- Numeric conversions
INTEGER_value::NUMERIC
NUMERIC_value::INTEGER
'123'::INTEGER
'123.45'::NUMERIC

-- String conversions
number_column::TEXT
date_column::TEXT
TEXT_column::INTEGER

-- Date/time conversions
'2026-01-25'::DATE
'14:30:00'::TIME
'2026-01-25 14:30:00'::TIMESTAMP
TIMESTAMP_column::DATE
DATE_column::TIMESTAMP

-- JSON conversions
json_column::JSONB
'{"key": "value"}'::JSON
text_column::JSON

-- Array conversions
'{1,2,3}'::INTEGER[]
text_array::TEXT[]

Casting Principles

Cast Safety:

  • Widening casts: Safe, no data loss (INT to BIGINT, DATE to TIMESTAMP)
  • Narrowing casts: May lose precision (NUMERIC to INT, TIMESTAMP to DATE)
  • Parsing casts: Can fail at runtime (TEXT to INTEGER with non-numeric text)
  • Formatting casts: Usually safe (any type to TEXT)

Implicit vs Explicit:

  • Implicit casting: PostgreSQL automatically converts compatible types
  • Explicit casting: You specify the conversion with CAST or ::
  • When required: Type ambiguity, incompatible types, or clarity
  • Best practice: Be explicit in complex queries for clarity

Common Patterns:

  • String to number: '123'::INTEGER, '45.67'::NUMERIC
  • Number to string: price::TEXT, customer_id::VARCHAR
  • Date formatting: date_column::TEXT, TO_CHAR(date, format)
  • Timestamp to date: timestamp_column::DATE (drops time)

🎯 Example 1: Basic Type Casting Between Common Types

-- Converting between text, numbers, and dates
SELECT 
    '123'::INTEGER AS text_to_int,
    '456.78'::NUMERIC AS text_to_numeric,
    '456.78'::DECIMAL(10,2) AS text_to_decimal,
    123::TEXT AS int_to_text,
    456.78::TEXT AS numeric_to_text,
    '2026-01-25'::DATE AS text_to_date,
    '14:30:00'::TIME AS text_to_time,
    '2026-01-25 14:30:00'::TIMESTAMP AS text_to_timestamp,
    CAST('true' AS BOOLEAN) AS text_to_bool,
    CAST(1 AS BOOLEAN) AS int_to_bool;
Result:
 text_to_int | text_to_numeric | text_to_decimal | int_to_text | numeric_to_text | text_to_date |text_to_time| text_to_timestamp   | text_to_bool | int_to_bool
-------------+-----------------+-----------------+-------------+-----------------+--------------+------------+---------------------+--------------+-------------
         123 |          456.78 |          456.78 | 123         | 456.78          | 2026-01-25   | 14:30:00   | 2026-01-25 14:30:00 | t            | t
(1 row)
Explanation: Type casting converts between data types. The :: operator is PostgreSQL's shorthand for CAST(). String literals must be in correct format for target type ('123' for INTEGER, '2026-01-25' for DATE). Any type can be cast to TEXT for display. Boolean casts accept 'true'/'false', 't'/'f', 'yes'/'no', '1'/'0', or TRUE/FALSE keywords. These basic casts are fundamental for data type compatibility and manipulation.

🎯 Example 2: Numeric Type Conversions and Precision

-- Converting between numeric types with different precisions
SELECT 
    product_name,
    price,
    price::INTEGER AS price_as_int,
    price::NUMERIC(10,0) AS price_rounded,
    price::NUMERIC(10,1) AS price_one_decimal,
    stock_quantity::NUMERIC AS stock_as_numeric,
    stock_quantity::BIGINT AS stock_as_bigint,
    (price * stock_quantity)::INTEGER AS inventory_value_int,
    ROUND(price * stock_quantity, 2)::NUMERIC(12,2) AS inventory_value_precise
FROM products
WHERE product_id <= 4
ORDER BY product_id;
Result:
  product_name  |  price  | price_as_int | price_rounded | price_one_decimal | stock_as_numeric | stock_as_bigint | inventory_value_int | inventory_value_precise
----------------+---------+--------------+---------------+-------------------+------------------+-----------------+---------------------+-------------------------
 Laptop Pro 15" | 1299.99 |         1300 |          1300 |            1300.0 |               50 |              50 |               64999 |                64999.50
 Wireless Mouse |   29.99 |           30 |            30 |              30.0 |              200 |             200 |                5998 |                 5998.00
 Smartphone X   |  899.99 |          900 |           900 |             900.0 |              100 |             100 |               89999 |                89999.00
 USB-C Cable    |   12.99 |           13 |            13 |              13.0 |              500 |             500 |                6495 |                 6495.00
(4 rows)
Explanation: Numeric type casts handle precision differently. Casting NUMERIC to INTEGER truncates decimals (1299.99 becomes 1300 after rounding, not 1299). NUMERIC(10,1) specifies precision (10 total digits, 1 after decimal). INTEGER to NUMERIC is safe (no data loss), but NUMERIC to INTEGER loses decimal precision. For financial calculations, use ROUND() before casting to INTEGER to control rounding behavior. Always consider precision requirements when casting numeric types.

🎯 Example 3: Date and Timestamp Conversions

-- Converting between date/time types and extracting components
SELECT 
    order_id,
    order_date,
    -- Timestamp to Date (drops time)
    order_date::DATE AS just_date,
    -- Timestamp to Time (drops date)
    order_date::TIME AS just_time,
    -- Date to Timestamp (adds midnight time)
    order_date::DATE::TIMESTAMP AS date_to_timestamp,
    -- Formatting as text
    order_date::TEXT AS timestamp_as_text,
    order_date::DATE::TEXT AS date_as_text,
    -- Text to date parsing
    '2026-12-25'::DATE AS christmas,
    -- Interval casting
    '30 days'::INTERVAL AS thirty_days,
    order_date + '30 days'::INTERVAL AS due_date
FROM orders
WHERE order_id <= 3
ORDER BY order_id;
Result:
 order_id |      order_date     | just_date  | just_time |  date_to_timestamp  | timestamp_as_text   | date_as_text | christmas  | thirty_days | due_date
----------+---------------------+------------+-----------+---------------------+---------------------+--------------+------------+-------------+---------------------
        1 | 2026-01-10 15:30:00 | 2026-01-10 | 15:30:00  | 2026-01-10 00:00:00 | 2026-01-10 15:30:00 | 2026-01-10   | 2026-12-25 | 30 days     | 2026-02-09 15:30:00
        2 | 2026-01-12 10:30:00 | 2026-01-12 | 10:30:00  | 2026-01-12 00:00:00 | 2026-01-12 10:30:00 | 2026-01-12   | 2026-12-25 | 30 days     | 2026-02-11 10:30:00
        3 | 2026-01-15 14:20:00 | 2026-01-15 | 14:20:00  | 2026-01-15 00:00:00 | 2026-01-15 14:20:00 | 2026-01-15   | 2026-12-25 | 30 days     | 2026-02-14 14:20:00
(3 rows)
Explanation: Date/time casting allows conversion between temporal types. TIMESTAMP::DATE extracts just the date portion (loses time information). TIMESTAMP::TIME extracts just the time. DATE::TIMESTAMP adds midnight (00:00:00) as the time. Casting to TEXT formats values for display. Text strings in correct format ('YYYY-MM-DD') can be cast to DATE. INTERVAL type represents time durations and can be cast from text like '30 days', '2 hours', '1 year'. Date arithmetic uses INTERVAL casts.

🎯 Example 4: Conditional Casting with Error Handling

-- Safe casting with error handling using CASE and validation
WITH data AS (
    SELECT * FROM (VALUES
        ('123', 'valid_number'),
        ('456.78', 'valid_decimal'),
        ('abc', 'invalid_number'),
        ('2026-01-25', 'valid_date'),
        ('not-a-date', 'invalid_date')
    ) AS t(value, description)
)
SELECT 
    value,
    description,
    -- Safe numeric cast with CASE
    CASE 
        WHEN value ~ '^[0-9]+$' THEN value::INTEGER
        ELSE NULL
    END AS safe_int_cast,
    -- Safe numeric cast with decimal
    CASE 
        WHEN value ~ '^[0-9]+\.?[0-9]*$' THEN value::NUMERIC
        ELSE NULL
    END AS safe_numeric_cast,
    -- Safe date cast
    CASE 
        WHEN value ~ '^\d{4}-\d{2}-\d{2}$' 
        THEN value::DATE
        ELSE NULL
    END AS safe_date_cast,
    -- Alternative: try casting in subquery with NULL on error
    (SELECT value::INTEGER FROM (VALUES(1)) x WHERE value ~ '^[0-9]+$') AS try_int
FROM data;
Result:
   value    |   description   | safe_int_cast | safe_numeric_cast | safe_date_cast | try_int
------------+-----------------+---------------+-------------------+----------------+---------
 123        | valid_number    |           123 |               123 | NULL           |     123
 456.78     | valid_decimal   |          NULL |            456.78 | NULL           |    NULL
 abc        | invalid_number  |          NULL |              NULL | NULL           |    NULL
 2026-01-25 | valid_date      |          NULL |              NULL | 2026-01-25     |    NULL
 not-a-date | invalid_date    |          NULL |              NULL | NULL           |    NULL
(5 rows)
Explanation: Casting text to numbers or dates can fail if the text is invalid. Use CASE with pattern validation (regex ~) to check format before casting, returning NULL for invalid values instead of causing errors. The regex '^[0-9]+$' matches integers only, '^[0-9]+\.?[0-9]*$' matches decimals, '^\d{4}-\d{2}-\d{2}$' matches date format. This defensive programming prevents query failures with untrusted or user-input data. PostgreSQL 13+ has support for TRY_CAST but CASE remains widely compatible.

🎯 Example 5: Array and JSON Type Casting

-- Converting between arrays, JSON, and other types
SELECT 
    -- Text to array
    '{1,2,3,4,5}'::INTEGER[] AS text_to_int_array,
    '{"apple","banana","cherry"}'::TEXT[] AS text_to_text_array,
    -- Array to text
    ARRAY[1,2,3]::TEXT AS array_to_text,
    -- JSON casting
    '{"name": "John", "age": 30}'::JSON AS text_to_json,
    '{"name": "John", "age": 30}'::JSONB AS text_to_jsonb,
    -- JSON to text
    ('{"key": "value"}'::JSON)::TEXT AS json_to_text,
    -- Extracting from JSON with casting
    ('{"age": 30}'::JSON->>'age')::INTEGER AS json_extracted_int,
    -- Array operations with casting
    ARRAY_LENGTH('{10,20,30}'::INTEGER[], 1) AS array_length,
    -- Complex: CSV string to array to individual rows
    UNNEST(STRING_TO_ARRAY('a,b,c', ',')) AS unnested_value;
Result:
 text_to_int_array | text_to_text_array | array_to_text |         text_to_json          |        text_to_jsonb          | json_to_text        | json_extracted_int | array_length | unnested_value
-------------------+--------------------+---------------+-------------------------------+-------------------------------+---------------------+--------------------+--------------+----------------
 {1,2,3,4,5}       | {apple,banana,...} | {1,2,3}       | {"name":"John","age":30}      | {"age":30,"name":"John"}      | {"key":"value"}     |                 30 |            3 | a
 {1,2,3,4,5}       | {apple,banana,...} | {1,2,3}       | {"name":"John","age":30}      | {"age":30,"name":"John"}      | {"key":"value"}     |                 30 |            3 | b
 {1,2,3,4,5}       | {apple,banana,...} | {1,2,3}       | {"name":"John","age":30}      | {"age":30,"name":"John"}      | {"key":"value"}     |                 30 |            3 | c
(3 rows)
Explanation: PostgreSQL supports casting to/from complex types. Text in array literal format ('{1,2,3}') casts to array types. JSON text casts to JSON/JSONB types. The ->> operator extracts JSON values as text, which can then be cast to appropriate types. Arrays cast to text showing their literal representation. JSONB is binary JSON (faster, supports indexing) while JSON stores exact text. These casts enable working with structured data, API responses, and complex data transformations in SQL.

4.2.10 Collation Expressions

📖 Full Description

Collation defines the sorting order and comparison rules for text data. Different languages and locales have different sorting conventions - German sorts 'ä' differently than English, Spanish treats 'ñ' as distinct, and case sensitivity varies by context. PostgreSQL allows you to specify collation per-column, per-query, or per-expression using the COLLATE clause, enabling precise control over string comparison and sorting behavior.

By default, PostgreSQL uses the database's default collation (often based on system locale). When you need different behavior - case-insensitive sorting, locale-specific ordering, or deterministic comparisons for indexing - you can override the collation. This is essential for internationalized applications, multi-language systems, and cases where default sorting doesn't match business requirements.

Collation expressions use the syntax expression COLLATE collation_name. Common collations include "C" (byte-order, fastest), "POSIX" (similar to C), "en_US.utf8" (US English), and special collations like "und-x-icu" (Unicode ICU). Understanding collation helps ensure correct text handling across languages and cultural contexts.

Collation Expression Syntax
-- Basic collation syntax
expression COLLATE collation_name

-- In WHERE clauses
WHERE column_name COLLATE "C" = 'value'
WHERE last_name COLLATE "en_US" > 'Smith'

-- In ORDER BY
ORDER BY column_name COLLATE "C" ASC
ORDER BY last_name COLLATE "en_US.utf8" DESC

-- Case-insensitive comparisons (if available)
WHERE email COLLATE "en_US.utf8" ILIKE pattern
WHERE name COLLATE "und-x-icu" = value

-- In SELECT expressions
SELECT 
    name,
    name COLLATE "C" AS name_c_order,
    name COLLATE "en_US.utf8" AS name_locale_order
FROM table_name;

-- Common collations
"C"             -- Byte order (fastest, deterministic)
"POSIX"         -- POSIX locale
"en_US.utf8"    -- US English UTF-8
"en_GB.utf8"    -- British English
"de_DE.utf8"    -- German
"es_ES.utf8"    -- Spanish
"und-x-icu"     -- Unicode ICU

-- Viewing available collations
SELECT * FROM pg_collation;

-- Default collation per column
CREATE TABLE products (
    name TEXT COLLATE "en_US.utf8",
    description TEXT
);

Collation Concepts

Why Collation Matters:

  • Sorting order: Different languages sort letters differently
  • Case sensitivity: Controls whether 'A' equals 'a'
  • Accent sensitivity: Whether 'e' equals 'é' or 'ë'
  • Performance: "C" collation is fastest for simple byte comparison
  • Indexing: Deterministic collations required for some index types

Common Collations:

  • "C": Simple byte-order comparison, fastest, deterministic
  • "POSIX": Similar to C, portable across systems
  • Locale-specific: "en_US.utf8", "de_DE.utf8" for language rules
  • ICU: "und-x-icu" Unicode standard, most comprehensive

When to Override:

  • Case-insensitive sorting: Natural sort order for user-facing data
  • Multi-language apps: Different sorting per language
  • Performance tuning: Use "C" for faster comparisons
  • Consistent behavior: Override unpredictable locale defaults

🎯 Example 1: Basic Collation Comparison (C vs Locale)

-- Comparing different collation behaviors
WITH names AS (
    SELECT name FROM (VALUES
        ('Apple'),
        ('apple'),
        ('Banana'),
        ('banana'),
        ('Äpfel'),  -- German for apples
        ('Zebra')
    ) AS t(name)
)
SELECT 
    name,
    -- Default collation (locale-dependent)
    ROW_NUMBER() OVER (ORDER BY name) AS default_order,
    -- C collation (byte order: uppercase before lowercase)
    ROW_NUMBER() OVER (ORDER BY name COLLATE "C") AS c_order,
    -- Explicit locale collation (case-insensitive grouping)
    ROW_NUMBER() OVER (ORDER BY name COLLATE "en_US.utf8") AS locale_order
FROM names
ORDER BY name COLLATE "C";
Result:
  name   | default_order | c_order | locale_order
---------+---------------+---------+--------------
 Apple   |             2 |       1 |            2
 Banana  |             4 |       2 |            4
 Zebra   |             6 |       3 |            6
 apple   |             3 |       4 |            1
 Äpfel   |             1 |       5 |            3
 banana  |             5 |       6 |            5
(6 rows)
Explanation: Collation dramatically affects sort order. "C" collation sorts by byte value (ASCII/UTF-8 order): uppercase letters (65-90) before lowercase (97-122), so 'Apple' < 'Banana' < 'Zebra' < 'apple'. Locale collations like "en_US.utf8" often treat case variations together and handle accented characters contextually. The default collation depends on database settings. Notice Äpfel's position varies - "C" sorts by UTF-8 byte value (high), while locale-aware sorts place it near other 'A' words.

🎯 Example 2: Case-Insensitive Sorting and Comparison

-- Using collation for case-insensitive operations
SELECT 
    first_name,
    last_name,
    email,
    -- Case-sensitive comparison (default for many collations)
    first_name = 'john' AS exact_match,
    -- Case-insensitive using COLLATE with case-folding
    LOWER(first_name) = 'john' AS case_insensitive_manual,
    -- Using ILIKE (inherently case-insensitive)
    first_name ILIKE 'john' AS ilike_match,
    -- Collation affects equality too
    first_name COLLATE "en_US.utf8" AS name_with_collation
FROM customers
WHERE LOWER(first_name) IN ('john', 'sarah', 'michael')
ORDER BY first_name COLLATE "C";
Result:
 first_name | last_name |          email          | exact_match | case_insensitive_manual | ilike_match | name_with_collation
------------+-----------+-------------------------+-------------+-------------------------+-------------+---------------------
 John       | Smith     | john.smith@example.com  | f           | t                       | t           | John
 Michael    | Brown     | michael.b@example.com   | f           | f                       | f           | Michael
 Sarah      | Johnson   | sarah.j@example.com     | f           | f                       | f           | Sarah
(3 rows)
Explanation: Case sensitivity depends on both collation and operator choice. Regular = is case-sensitive for most collations. ILIKE is PostgreSQL's case-insensitive LIKE operator (regardless of collation). To achieve case-insensitive equality, use LOWER(col) = LOWER(value) or ILIKE for patterns. Some collations have built-in case-insensitivity. Collation affects both comparison (WHERE) and ordering (ORDER BY). For user-facing searches, case-insensitive comparison improves usability.

🎯 Example 3: Collation in ORDER BY for Natural Sorting

-- Different sort orders based on collation
WITH products AS (
    SELECT product_name FROM (VALUES
        ('USB Cable'),
        ('usb cable'),
        ('USB-C Adapter'),
        ('Monitor'),
        ('mouse'),
        ('Mouse Pad'),
        ('Keyboard')
    ) AS t(product_name)
)
SELECT 
    product_name,
    ROW_NUMBER() OVER (ORDER BY product_name COLLATE "C") AS c_sort_order,
    ROW_NUMBER() OVER (ORDER BY product_name COLLATE "en_US.utf8") AS locale_sort_order,
    ROW_NUMBER() OVER (ORDER BY LOWER(product_name) COLLATE "C") AS case_insensitive_sort
FROM products
ORDER BY product_name COLLATE "en_US.utf8";
Result:
 product_name   | c_sort_order | locale_sort_order | case_insensitive_sort
----------------+--------------+-------------------+-----------------------
 Keyboard       |            1 |                 1 |                     1
 Monitor        |            2 |                 2 |                     2
 Mouse Pad      |            3 |                 3 |                     3
 USB Cable      |            4 |                 4 |                     6
 USB-C Adapter  |            5 |                 5 |                     7
 mouse          |            7 |                 6 |                     4
 usb cable      |            6 |                 7 |                     5
(7 rows)
Explanation: Collation in ORDER BY controls sort behavior. "C" collation sorts uppercase before lowercase (Keyboard, Monitor, Mouse Pad, USB..., mouse, usb...). Locale collations often group case variants together (Mouse, mouse are near each other). Combining LOWER() with "C" collation creates deterministic case-insensitive sorting. Choose collation based on needs: "C" for performance and deterministic behavior, locale for user-facing natural order. Product listings, name directories, and reports benefit from appropriate collation choice.

🎯 Example 4: Multi-Language Collation Handling

-- Handling multiple languages with appropriate collations
WITH international_customers AS (
    SELECT customer_name, language FROM (VALUES
        ('Müller', 'German'),
        ('Møller', 'Danish'),
        ('Molina', 'Spanish'),
        ('Özdemir', 'Turkish'),
        ('Olsen', 'Norwegian'),
        ('Ñúñez', 'Spanish')
    ) AS t(customer_name, language)
)
SELECT 
    customer_name,
    language,
    -- Standard C collation (byte order)
    ROW_NUMBER() OVER (ORDER BY customer_name COLLATE "C") AS byte_order,
    -- German collation (ä, ö, ü handled specifically)
    ROW_NUMBER() OVER (ORDER BY customer_name COLLATE "de_DE.utf8") AS german_order,
    -- Spanish collation (ñ as separate letter)
    ROW_NUMBER() OVER (ORDER BY customer_name COLLATE "es_ES.utf8") AS spanish_order,
    -- Generic locale
    ROW_NUMBER() OVER (ORDER BY customer_name COLLATE "en_US.utf8") AS generic_order
FROM international_customers
ORDER BY customer_name COLLATE "en_US.utf8";
Result:
 customer_name | language  | byte_order | german_order | spanish_order | generic_order
---------------+-----------+------------+--------------+---------------+---------------
 Molina        | Spanish   |          2 |            1 |             1 |             1
 Møller        | Danish    |          3 |            2 |             2 |             2
 Müller        | German    |          4 |            3 |             3 |             3
 Olsen         | Norwegian |          1 |            4 |             4 |             4
 Özdemir       | Turkish   |          5 |            5 |             5 |             5
 Ñúñez         | Spanish   |          6 |            6 |             6 |             6
(6 rows)
Explanation: Different language collations handle accented and special characters according to linguistic rules. German "de_DE.utf8" sorts ü (umlaut) near 'u', Spanish "es_ES.utf8" treats ñ as distinct letter between 'n' and 'o', while "C" collation sorts by UTF-8 byte value. Multi-language applications should either use a per-language collation when language is known, or a generic Unicode collation (ICU) for consistent behavior. Storing language metadata enables dynamic collation selection.

🎯 Example 5: Performance and Deterministic Collation

-- Using C collation for performance and deterministic behavior
WITH performance_test AS (
    SELECT 
        product_name,
        category_name
    FROM products p
    JOIN categories c ON p.category_id = c.category_id
    WHERE p.is_active = TRUE
)
SELECT 
    category_name,
    product_name,
    -- C collation: fast, deterministic, indexable
    product_name COLLATE "C" AS deterministic_name,
    -- Can be used in unique indexes and constraints
    CASE 
        WHEN product_name COLLATE "C" < 'M' THEN 'A-L'
        WHEN product_name COLLATE "C" < 'Z' THEN 'M-Y'
        ELSE 'Z+'
    END AS alphabetic_bucket
FROM performance_test
ORDER BY category_name COLLATE "C", product_name COLLATE "C"
LIMIT 8;
Result:
 category_name |  product_name  | deterministic_name | alphabetic_bucket
---------------+----------------+--------------------+-------------------
 Electronics   | Laptop Pro 15" | Laptop Pro 15"     | A-L
 Electronics   | Mechanical Kbd | Mechanical Kbd     | M-Y
 Electronics   | Monitor 27"    | Monitor 27"        | M-Y
 Electronics   | Smartphone X   | Smartphone X       | M-Y
 Electronics   | USB-C Cable    | USB-C Cable        | M-Y
 Electronics   | Wireless Mouse | Wireless Mouse     | M-Y
 Furniture     | Desk Chair Pro | Desk Chair Pro     | A-L
 Furniture     | Office Table   | Office Table       | A-L
(8 rows)
Explanation: "C" collation provides deterministic, fast byte-order comparison. This is essential for: (1) Performance-critical queries where locale-aware comparison overhead matters, (2) Unique indexes and constraints requiring deterministic behavior, (3) Cross-platform consistency (locale availability varies), (4) Simple prefix/range queries. Use "C" for internal identifiers, codes, and machine-generated data. Use locale collations for human-readable, user-facing text. Many databases use "C" as default for these reasons.

4.2.11 Scalar Subqueries

📖 Full Description

Scalar Subqueries are SELECT statements enclosed in parentheses that return exactly one row with one column, usable anywhere a single value is expected. Unlike regular subqueries in FROM clauses or joins, scalar subqueries appear in expression contexts - SELECT lists, WHERE conditions, CASE statements - providing a value computed from another query. They enable embedding complex logic and correlated calculations directly in expressions.

Scalar subqueries are powerful for pulling related data without joins, computing aggregates per row, or performing existence checks. A common pattern is using correlated scalar subqueries that reference the outer query's columns, allowing row-specific calculations. For example, getting each product's category name, or calculating each customer's total orders inline without joining.

The critical requirement: scalar subqueries must return exactly one row and one column. Returning zero rows yields NULL. Returning multiple rows causes a runtime error. This constraint makes them suitable for lookups, aggregates, and cases where you expect a single result. For multiple values, use arrays, JSON aggregation, or traditional joins instead.

Scalar Subquery Syntax
-- Basic scalar subquery in SELECT
SELECT 
    column1,
    (SELECT column FROM table WHERE condition) AS computed_value
FROM table_name;

-- Scalar subquery in WHERE clause
WHERE column > (SELECT AVG(column) FROM other_table)

-- Scalar subquery with correlation (references outer query)
SELECT 
    product_name,
    (SELECT category_name 
     FROM categories c 
     WHERE c.category_id = p.category_id) AS category
FROM products p;

-- Scalar subquery with aggregate
SELECT 
    customer_id,
    (SELECT COUNT(*) 
     FROM orders o 
     WHERE o.customer_id = c.customer_id) AS order_count
FROM customers c;

-- Multiple scalar subqueries
SELECT 
    product_id,
    (SELECT MIN(price) FROM products) AS min_price,
    (SELECT MAX(price) FROM products) AS max_price,
    (SELECT AVG(price) FROM products) AS avg_price;

-- Scalar subquery in CASE
CASE
    WHEN price > (SELECT AVG(price) FROM products) THEN 'Above Average'
    ELSE 'Below Average'
END

-- Nested scalar subqueries
SELECT 
    (SELECT MAX(total) 
     FROM (SELECT SUM(amount) AS total FROM orders GROUP BY customer_id) sub
    ) AS max_customer_total;

Scalar Subquery Characteristics

Requirements:

  • Single row: Must return exactly one row (0 rows → NULL, >1 rows → error)
  • Single column: Must return exactly one column
  • Any type: Return value can be any data type
  • NULL handling: Zero rows returns NULL, not error

Correlated vs Non-Correlated:

  • Non-correlated: Independent subquery, executed once
  • Correlated: References outer query columns, executed per row
  • Performance: Non-correlated is faster (single execution)
  • Use case: Correlated for row-specific calculations

Common Patterns:

  • Lookup: Get related value without JOIN
  • Aggregate: Count, sum, average per row
  • Comparison: Compare to global average, min, max
  • Existence: Check if related records exist
  • Calculation: Complex computed values

🎯 Example 1: Basic Non-Correlated Scalar Subqueries

-- Using scalar subqueries for global comparisons
SELECT 
    product_id,
    product_name,
    price,
    -- Non-correlated: executed once, reused for all rows
    (SELECT AVG(price) FROM products) AS avg_price,
    (SELECT MIN(price) FROM products) AS min_price,
    (SELECT MAX(price) FROM products) AS max_price,
    -- Comparison with subquery result
    price - (SELECT AVG(price) FROM products) AS price_diff_from_avg,
    -- Conditional based on subquery
    CASE 
        WHEN price > (SELECT AVG(price) FROM products) THEN 'Above Average'
        WHEN price < (SELECT AVG(price) FROM products) THEN 'Below Average'
        ELSE 'Average'
    END AS price_category
FROM products
WHERE is_active = TRUE
ORDER BY price DESC
LIMIT 5;
Result:
 product_id |  product_name  |  price  | avg_price | min_price | max_price | price_diff_from_avg | price_category
------------+----------------+---------+-----------+-----------+-----------+---------------------+----------------
          1 | Laptop Pro 15" | 1299.99 |    338.49 |     12.99 |   1299.99 |              961.50 | Above Average
          3 | Smartphone X   |  899.99 |    338.49 |     12.99 |   1299.99 |              561.50 | Above Average
          7 | Monitor 27"    |  399.99 |    338.49 |     12.99 |   1299.99 |               61.50 | Above Average
          9 | Desk Chair Pro |  299.99 |    338.49 |     12.99 |   1299.99 |              -38.50 | Below Average
          5 | Mechanical Kbd |  149.99 |    338.49 |     12.99 |   1299.99 |             -188.50 | Below Average
(5 rows)
Explanation: Non-correlated scalar subqueries don't reference outer query columns, so they execute once and return the same value for all rows. (SELECT AVG(price) FROM products) calculates overall average once, then that value is reused for every product. This is efficient for global statistics. Each row compares its price to the global average. Non-correlated subqueries are like constants computed at query start.

🎯 Example 2: Correlated Scalar Subqueries for Row-Specific Data

-- Correlated subqueries reference outer query and execute per row
SELECT 
    c.customer_id,
    c.first_name,
    c.last_name,
    -- Correlated: executed once per customer
    (SELECT COUNT(*) 
     FROM orders o 
     WHERE o.customer_id = c.customer_id) AS order_count,
    (SELECT SUM(total_amount) 
     FROM orders o 
     WHERE o.customer_id = c.customer_id) AS total_spent,
    (SELECT MAX(order_date) 
     FROM orders o 
     WHERE o.customer_id = c.customer_id) AS last_order_date,
    -- Conditional based on correlated subquery
    CASE 
        WHEN (SELECT COUNT(*) FROM orders o WHERE o.customer_id = c.customer_id) > 2 
        THEN 'Frequent'
        WHEN (SELECT COUNT(*) FROM orders o WHERE o.customer_id = c.customer_id) > 0 
        THEN 'Occasional'
        ELSE 'Never Ordered'
    END AS customer_type
FROM customers c
WHERE c.customer_id <= 5
ORDER BY c.customer_id;
Result:
 customer_id | first_name | last_name | order_count | total_spent |     last_order_date  | customer_type
-------------+------------+-----------+-------------+-------------+----------------------+---------------
           1 | John       | Smith     |           2 |     2259.96 | 2026-01-15 14:20:00  | Occasional
           2 | Sarah      | Johnson   |           2 |     1529.96 | 2026-01-22 14:00:00  | Occasional
           3 | Michael    | Brown     |           1 |      149.99 | 2026-01-18 09:15:00  | Occasional
           4 | Emily      | Davis     |           1 |       42.98 | 2026-01-20 16:45:00  | Occasional
           5 | James      | Wilson    |           0 |        NULL | NULL                 | Never Ordered
(5 rows)
Explanation: Correlated scalar subqueries reference outer query (c.customer_id), executing once per outer row with that row's values. Each customer gets their own order count, total spent, and last order date. WHERE o.customer_id = c.customer_id creates the correlation. This is like a per-row function call. Performance note: correlated subqueries run many times (once per outer row), so they're slower than joins for large datasets but clearer for simple lookups.

🎯 Example 3: Scalar Subqueries for Lookups Without JOIN

-- Using scalar subqueries instead of joins for simple lookups
SELECT 
    p.product_id,
    p.product_name,
    p.price,
    -- Lookup category name without JOIN
    (SELECT c.category_name 
     FROM categories c 
     WHERE c.category_id = p.category_id) AS category_name,
    -- Lookup department through category
    (SELECT d.department_name 
     FROM categories c 
     JOIN departments d ON c.department_id = d.department_id
     WHERE c.category_id = p.category_id) AS department_name,
    -- Calculate relative price within category
    ROUND(100.0 * p.price / NULLIF(
        (SELECT AVG(price) FROM products p2 WHERE p2.category_id = p.category_id),
        0
    ), 2) AS pct_of_category_avg
FROM products p
WHERE p.is_active = TRUE
ORDER BY p.product_id
LIMIT 5;
Result:
 product_id |  product_name  |  price  | category_name | department_name | pct_of_category_avg
------------+----------------+---------+---------------+-----------------+---------------------
          1 | Laptop Pro 15" | 1299.99 | Electronics   | Technology      |              253.88
          2 | Wireless Mouse |   29.99 | Electronics   | Technology      |                5.86
          3 | Smartphone X   |  899.99 | Electronics   | Technology      |              175.78
          4 | USB-C Cable    |   12.99 | Electronics   | Technology      |                2.54
          5 | Mechanical Kbd |  149.99 | Electronics   | Technology      |               29.30
(5 rows)
Explanation: Scalar subqueries provide an alternative to JOIN for simple lookups. Instead of JOIN categories, use (SELECT category_name FROM categories WHERE...). This keeps the query structure simpler when you need just one or two related values. The tradeoff: joins may be faster for multiple lookups (optimizer can handle better), but scalar subqueries are clearer for occasional lookups. Note NULLIF prevents division by zero when calculating percentages.

🎯 Example 4: Scalar Subqueries in WHERE and HAVING Clauses

-- Using scalar subqueries for filtering based on aggregates
SELECT 
    p.category_id,
    (SELECT c.category_name FROM categories c WHERE c.category_id = p.category_id) AS category_name,
    COUNT(*) AS product_count,
    AVG(p.price) AS avg_price,
    SUM(p.stock_quantity) AS total_stock
FROM products p
WHERE p.is_active = TRUE
    -- Filter: price above global average
    AND p.price > (SELECT AVG(price) FROM products WHERE is_active = TRUE)
    -- Filter: category has more than 2 products
    AND (SELECT COUNT(*) FROM products p2 WHERE p2.category_id = p.category_id) > 2
GROUP BY p.category_id
HAVING 
    -- HAVING: group avg above global avg
    AVG(p.price) > (SELECT AVG(price) * 1.5 FROM products WHERE is_active = TRUE)
ORDER BY avg_price DESC;
Result:
 category_id | category_name | product_count | avg_price | total_stock
-------------+---------------+---------------+-----------+-------------
           1 | Electronics   |             3 |    749.99 |         250
(1 row)
Explanation: Scalar subqueries in WHERE/HAVING enable dynamic filtering based on computed values. WHERE filters individual rows: only products priced above average and in categories with >2 products. HAVING filters groups: only categories where group average exceeds 1.5× global average. Each scalar subquery provides a comparison value. This pattern is common for "above average", "top performers", or "outlier" queries. Alternative approaches use CTEs or window functions, but scalar subqueries are concise for simple cases.

🎯 Example 5: Handling NULL and Error Cases

-- Demonstrating NULL handling and preventing errors
SELECT 
    c.customer_id,
    c.first_name,
    c.last_name,
    -- Returns NULL if no orders (0 rows)
    (SELECT MAX(o.order_date) 
     FROM orders o 
     WHERE o.customer_id = c.customer_id) AS last_order_date,
    -- COALESCE handles NULL case
    COALESCE(
        (SELECT SUM(o.total_amount) FROM orders o WHERE o.customer_id = c.customer_id),
        0
    ) AS total_spent,
    -- Safe division with NULLIF
    COALESCE(
        (SELECT SUM(o.total_amount) FROM orders o WHERE o.customer_id = c.customer_id) / 
        NULLIF((SELECT COUNT(*) FROM orders o WHERE o.customer_id = c.customer_id), 0),
        0
    ) AS avg_order_value,
    -- Subquery with LIMIT 1 ensures single row (would error if multiple)
    (SELECT o.order_id 
     FROM orders o 
     WHERE o.customer_id = c.customer_id 
     ORDER BY o.order_date DESC 
     LIMIT 1) AS latest_order_id
FROM customers c
WHERE c.customer_id <= 5
ORDER BY c.customer_id;
Result:
 customer_id | first_name | last_name |     last_order_date  | total_spent | avg_order_value | latest_order_id
-------------+------------+-----------+----------------------+-------------+-----------------+-----------------
           1 | John       | Smith     | 2026-01-15 14:20:00  |     2259.96 |         1129.98 |               3
           2 | Sarah      | Johnson   | 2026-01-22 14:00:00  |     1529.96 |          764.98 |               6
           3 | Michael    | Brown     | 2026-01-18 09:15:00  |      149.99 |          149.99 |               4
           4 | Emily      | Davis     | 2026-01-20 16:45:00  |       42.98 |           42.98 |               5
           5 | James      | Wilson    | NULL                 |        0.00 |            0.00 |            NULL
(5 rows)
Explanation: Scalar subqueries return NULL when no rows match (customer with zero orders). Use COALESCE to provide defaults for NULL results. NULLIF prevents division-by-zero errors. LIMIT 1 in subqueries ensures single-row results even if multiple rows could match (takes first after ORDER BY). Without LIMIT 1, multiple matches cause "scalar subquery returned more than one row" error. Always design scalar subqueries to guarantee single-row results through WHERE conditions, aggregates (which always return one row), or LIMIT 1.

4.2.12 Array Constructors

📖 Full Description

Array Constructors create array values in PostgreSQL using various syntaxes. PostgreSQL supports multi-dimensional arrays of any data type, making them powerful for storing lists, matrices, tags, and sets of values. Array constructors include the ARRAY keyword with elements, array literal notation with curly braces, and the ARRAY subquery syntax that builds arrays from query results.

Arrays enable storing related values together without creating separate tables or rows. Common use cases include storing product tags, tracking user roles, maintaining lists of IDs, or representing mathematical vectors and matrices. PostgreSQL provides rich array operations - indexing, slicing, searching, aggregation - making arrays practical for real applications beyond simple storage.

Understanding array construction is essential for leveraging PostgreSQL's array capabilities. You can create arrays from literals (ARRAY[1,2,3]), from subqueries (ARRAY(SELECT...)), or using aggregate functions (ARRAY_AGG). Arrays can be single or multi-dimensional, contain any PostgreSQL type (integers, text, timestamps, even custom types), and support NULL elements.

Array Constructor Syntax
-- ARRAY constructor with explicit elements
ARRAY[element1, element2, element3, ...]
ARRAY[1, 2, 3, 4, 5]
ARRAY['apple', 'banana', 'cherry']

-- Array literal notation (PostgreSQL specific)
'{element1, element2, element3}'::type[]
'{1,2,3,4,5}'::INTEGER[]
'{"apple","banana","cherry"}'::TEXT[]

-- ARRAY from subquery (single column)
ARRAY(SELECT column FROM table WHERE condition)
ARRAY(SELECT product_id FROM products WHERE is_active = TRUE)

-- Multi-dimensional arrays
ARRAY[[1,2],[3,4]]              -- 2x2 matrix
ARRAY[[1,2,3],[4,5,6],[7,8,9]]  -- 3x3 matrix

-- Empty arrays
ARRAY[]::INTEGER[]              -- Empty integer array
'{}'::TEXT[]                    -- Empty text array

-- Arrays with NULL elements
ARRAY[1, NULL, 3, NULL, 5]
ARRAY['a', NULL, 'c']

-- Nested array construction
ARRAY[
    ARRAY[1, 2],
    ARRAY[3, 4]
]

-- Array concatenation
ARRAY[1,2] || ARRAY[3,4]        -- {1,2,3,4}
ARRAY[1,2] || 3                 -- {1,2,3}

-- Common array operations
array_length(array, dimension)  -- Get length
array_dims(array)               -- Get dimensions as text
unnest(array)                   -- Expand to rows

Array Constructor Types

Constructor Syntaxes:

  • ARRAY[...]: Explicit ARRAY keyword with elements in brackets
  • '{...}'::type[]: String literal cast to array type
  • ARRAY(SELECT...): Build array from subquery results
  • ARRAY_AGG(): Aggregate function that builds arrays

Array Features:

  • Any type: Arrays of integers, text, dates, JSON, custom types
  • Multi-dimensional: 1D, 2D, 3D, or higher
  • NULL support: Arrays can contain NULL elements
  • Variable length: Arrays grow/shrink dynamically
  • 1-indexed: First element is array[1], not array[0]

Common Uses:

  • Tags and labels: Product tags, user roles, categories
  • ID lists: Store related IDs without separate join table
  • Mathematical: Vectors, matrices for computations
  • Time series: Sequences of measurements or events
  • Configuration: Lists of settings, options, choices

🎯 Example 1: Basic Array Construction Methods

-- Different ways to construct arrays
SELECT 
    -- ARRAY constructor with explicit elements
    ARRAY[1, 2, 3, 4, 5] AS array_explicit,
    -- Array literal notation with cast
    '{10,20,30,40,50}'::INTEGER[] AS array_literal,
    -- Text array
    ARRAY['apple', 'banana', 'cherry', 'date'] AS text_array,
    -- Array with NULLs
    ARRAY[1, NULL, 3, NULL, 5] AS array_with_nulls,
    -- Empty array
    ARRAY[]::INTEGER[] AS empty_array,
    -- Array concatenation
    ARRAY[1,2,3] || ARRAY[4,5,6] AS concatenated,
    -- Append element to array
    ARRAY[1,2,3] || 4 AS array_append,
    -- Prepend element to array
    0 || ARRAY[1,2,3] AS array_prepend;
Result:
 array_explicit | array_literal |      text_array       | array_with_nulls | empty_array | concatenated | array_append | array_prepend
----------------+---------------+-----------------------+------------------+-------------+--------------+--------------+---------------
 {1,2,3,4,5}    | {10,20,30,... | {apple,banana,cherry..| {1,NULL,3,NULL,5}| {}          | {1,2,3,4,5,6}| {1,2,3,4}    | {0,1,2,3}
(1 row)
Explanation: PostgreSQL offers multiple array construction syntaxes. ARRAY[...] is explicit and type-safe. String literals like '{1,2,3}'::INTEGER[] are concise but require correct format and type casting. Arrays can contain NULLs (NULL elements, not NULL array). Empty arrays are valid with ARRAY[]::type[] syntax. The || operator concatenates arrays or appends/prepends single elements. Arrays are flexible data structures for grouping related values.

🎯 Example 2: Building Arrays from Subqueries

-- ARRAY(SELECT...) constructs arrays from query results
SELECT 
    c.customer_id,
    c.first_name || ' ' || c.last_name AS customer_name,
    -- Array of all order IDs for this customer
    ARRAY(
        SELECT o.order_id 
        FROM orders o 
        WHERE o.customer_id = c.customer_id 
        ORDER BY o.order_date
    ) AS order_ids,
    -- Array of order amounts
    ARRAY(
        SELECT o.total_amount 
        FROM orders o 
        WHERE o.customer_id = c.customer_id 
        ORDER BY o.order_date
    ) AS order_amounts,
    -- Array of order statuses
    ARRAY(
        SELECT o.status 
        FROM orders o 
        WHERE o.customer_id = c.customer_id 
        ORDER BY o.order_date
    ) AS order_statuses,
    -- Count using array_length
    array_length(ARRAY(SELECT o.order_id FROM orders o WHERE o.customer_id = c.customer_id), 1) AS order_count
FROM customers c
WHERE c.customer_id <= 3
ORDER BY c.customer_id;
Result:
 customer_id | customer_name | order_ids |   order_amounts    |    order_statuses      | order_count
-------------+---------------+-----------+--------------------+------------------------+-------------
           1 | John Smith    | {1,3}     | {1359.97,899.99}   | {delivered,pending}    |           2
           2 | Sarah Johnson | {2,6}     | {1329.98,199.98}   | {delivered,shipped}    |           2
           3 | Michael Brown | {4}       | {149.99}           | {shipped}              |           1
(3 rows)
Explanation: ARRAY(SELECT...) builds arrays from subquery results. Each customer gets an array of their order IDs, amounts, and statuses. The subquery must return single column; multiple rows become array elements. ORDER BY in subquery controls array element order. This pattern is powerful for collecting related data without separate queries or complex joins. array_length(array, 1) returns array size (first dimension). This approach is cleaner than string concatenation for lists.

🎯 Example 3: Multi-Dimensional Arrays

-- Creating and working with 2D arrays (matrices)
SELECT 
    -- 2x2 matrix
    ARRAY[[1,2],[3,4]] AS matrix_2x2,
    -- 3x3 identity matrix
    ARRAY[[1,0,0],[0,1,0],[0,0,1]] AS identity_3x3,
    -- 2x3 matrix (2 rows, 3 columns)
    ARRAY[[10,20,30],[40,50,60]] AS matrix_2x3,
    -- Accessing elements
    (ARRAY[[1,2],[3,4]])[1][1] AS top_left,
    (ARRAY[[1,2],[3,4]])[2][2] AS bottom_right,
    -- Array dimensions
    array_dims(ARRAY[[1,2,3],[4,5,6]]) AS dimensions_text,
    -- Length of each dimension
    array_length(ARRAY[[1,2,3],[4,5,6]], 1) AS rows,
    array_length(ARRAY[[1,2,3],[4,5,6]], 2) AS columns;
Result:
 matrix_2x2  | identity_3x3        |  matrix_2x3    | top_left | bottom_right | dimensions_text | rows | columns
-------------+---------------------+----------------+----------+--------------+-----------------+------+---------
 {{1,2},{3,4}| {{1,0,0},{0,1,0},...| {{10,20,30},...|        1 |            4 | [1:2][1:3]      |    2 |       3
(1 row)
Explanation: Multi-dimensional arrays use nested ARRAY[[...],[...]] syntax. ARRAY[[1,2],[3,4]] creates a 2x2 matrix. Access elements with multiple subscripts: array[row][column]. array_dims() returns dimension ranges as text (e.g., "[1:2][1:3]" means rows 1-2, columns 1-3). array_length(array, n) gets length of nth dimension. Multi-dimensional arrays are useful for matrices, grids, game boards, and tabular data structures. All sub-arrays must have same length.

🎯 Example 4: Arrays with Complex Types

-- Arrays of various PostgreSQL types
SELECT 
    -- Array of dates
    ARRAY['2026-01-01'::DATE, '2026-02-01'::DATE, '2026-03-01'::DATE] AS date_array,
    -- Array of timestamps
    ARRAY[
        '2026-01-01 10:00:00'::TIMESTAMP,
        '2026-01-01 14:30:00'::TIMESTAMP
    ] AS timestamp_array,
    -- Array of numeric/decimal
    ARRAY[10.5, 20.75, 30.125]::NUMERIC[] AS numeric_array,
    -- Array of JSON
    ARRAY['{"id": 1}'::JSON, '{"id": 2}'::JSON] AS json_array,
    -- Array of intervals
    ARRAY['1 day'::INTERVAL, '2 hours'::INTERVAL, '30 minutes'::INTERVAL] AS interval_array,
    -- Extract elements
    (ARRAY['2026-01-01'::DATE, '2026-02-01'::DATE])[1] AS first_date,
    -- Array operations
    array_length(ARRAY[10.5, 20.75, 30.125]::NUMERIC[], 1) AS count;
Result:
      date_array      |        timestamp_array         |  numeric_array  |       json_array       |       interval_array       | first_date | count
----------------------+--------------------------------+-----------------+------------------------+----------------------------+------------+-------
 {2026-01-01,2026-... | {"2026-01-01 10:00:00","2026...| {10.5,20.75,... | {"{\"id\": 1}","{\"id\"...| {"1 day","2:00:00","00:30...| 2026-01-01 |     3
(1 row)
Explanation: PostgreSQL arrays support any data type - dates, timestamps, numerics, JSON, intervals, even custom types. Each array is strongly typed (INTEGER[], TEXT[], DATE[], etc.). Elements must match the array's declared type. Type casting (::DATE, ::NUMERIC[]) ensures correct types. Arrays of complex types enable sophisticated data structures - time series of timestamps, lists of JSON documents, sequences of intervals. Array operations (subscripting, length, search) work consistently across all types.

🎯 Example 5: Practical Array Operations and Functions

-- Common array functions and operations
WITH product_data AS (
    SELECT 
        product_id,
        product_name,
        ARRAY['electronics', 'computers', 'laptops', 'portable'] AS tags
    FROM products
    WHERE product_id = 1
)
SELECT 
    product_name,
    tags,
    -- Array properties
    array_length(tags, 1) AS tag_count,
    array_dims(tags) AS dimensions,
    -- Element access
    tags[1] AS first_tag,
    tags[array_length(tags, 1)] AS last_tag,
    -- Slicing
    tags[2:3] AS middle_tags,
    -- Search operations
    'laptops' = ANY(tags) AS has_laptop_tag,
    'phones' = ANY(tags) AS has_phone_tag,
    -- Array contains
    tags @> ARRAY['computers'] AS contains_computers,
    -- Overlap check
    tags && ARRAY['tablets', 'phones', 'laptops'] AS overlaps_mobile,
    -- Converting array to rows
    unnest(tags) AS individual_tags
FROM product_data;
Result:
  product_name  |              tags               | tag_count | dimensions | first_tag  | last_tag | middle_tags        | has_laptop_tag | has_phone_tag | contains_computers | overlaps_mobile | individual_tags
----------------+---------------------------------+-----------+------------+------------+----------+--------------------+----------------+---------------+--------------------+-----------------+-----------------
 Laptop Pro 15" | {electronics,computers,laptops...|         4 | [1:4]      | electronic.| portable | {computers,laptops}|  t             | f             | t                  | t               | electronics
 Laptop Pro 15" | {electronics,computers,laptops...|         4 | [1:4]      | electronic.| portable | {computers,laptops}|  t             | f             | t                  | t               | computers
 Laptop Pro 15" | {electronics,computers,laptops...|         4 | [1:4]      | electronic.| portable | {computers,laptops}|  t             | f             | t                  | t               | laptops
 Laptop Pro 15" | {electronics,computers,laptops...|         4 | [1:4]      | electronic.| portable | {computers,laptops}|  t             | f             | t                  | t               | portable
(4 rows)
Explanation: PostgreSQL provides rich array operations. array_length() gets size, subscripting accesses elements, slicing extracts subarrays. The ANY operator checks if value exists in array. @> tests if left array contains right array elements. && tests overlap (common elements). unnest() expands array to multiple rows (useful for joining or searching). These operations make arrays practical for tags, categories, lists, and set operations without complex joins or string parsing.

4.2.13 Row Constructors

📖 Full Description

Row Constructors create composite values (row types) containing multiple fields of potentially different types. Using the ROW(...) keyword or implicit row notation, you can bundle related values into a single composite value. This is useful for comparing multiple columns simultaneously, passing structured data to functions, or temporarily grouping related values without creating tables or types.

Row constructors enable powerful comparison operations - comparing entire rows at once, using IN clauses with multiple columns, or performing lexicographic ordering. They're also essential when working with composite types, returning multiple values from functions, or building structured data dynamically. PostgreSQL's type system treats each table as defining an implicit composite type, making row constructors natural for working with table data.

Row constructor syntax includes explicit ROW(val1, val2, ...) and implicit (val1, val2, ...). Fields in constructed rows can be accessed using field selection syntax or positional notation (f1, f2, f3, ...). Understanding row constructors enhances your ability to work with structured data and perform sophisticated comparisons in SQL.

Row Constructor Syntax
-- Explicit ROW constructor
ROW(value1, value2, value3, ...)
ROW(1, 'John', 'Smith')
ROW(product_id, product_name, price)

-- Implicit row constructor (parentheses only)
(value1, value2, value3, ...)
(1, 'John', 'Smith')
(customer_id, last_name)

-- Row comparison in WHERE
WHERE (col1, col2) = (val1, val2)
WHERE (customer_id, status) IN ((1, 'active'), (2, 'active'))

-- Row ordering (lexicographic)
ORDER BY (col1, col2) DESC

-- Accessing row fields
(ROW(1, 'John', 'Smith')).f1  -- Returns 1
(ROW(1, 'John', 'Smith')).f2  -- Returns 'John'

-- Row constructor with table row type
(SELECT t FROM table_name t WHERE id = 1)

-- Multiple column UPDATE
UPDATE table SET (col1, col2) = (val1, val2) WHERE...

-- Row comparison operators
=, <>, <, >, <=, >=
(a, b) > (x, y)  -- Lexicographic: compares a to x, if equal compares b to y

-- IN clause with multiple columns
WHERE (col1, col2) IN (
    SELECT other_col1, other_col2 FROM other_table
)

-- Row constructor in VALUES
INSERT INTO table VALUES 
    ROW(1, 'a', 100),
    ROW(2, 'b', 200);

Row Constructor Features

Construction Methods:

  • Explicit ROW(): ROW(val1, val2, ...) - clear and explicit
  • Implicit (): (val1, val2, ...) - concise syntax
  • Table row type: (SELECT t FROM table t WHERE...) - complete row
  • Field access: (row_expression).f1, .f2, .f3 for anonymous rows

Common Operations:

  • Equality: (a,b) = (x,y) tests if all fields match
  • Inequality: (a,b) <> (x,y) tests if any field differs
  • Ordering: (a,b) < (x,y) lexicographic comparison
  • IN clause: Multi-column membership testing
  • Multi-column UPDATE: Update multiple columns at once

Use Cases:

  • Multi-column comparison: WHERE (col1, col2) = (val1, val2)
  • Composite keys: Comparing or searching by multiple fields
  • Batch updates: SET (col1, col2, col3) = (subquery...)
  • Function returns: Return multiple values as composite
  • Temporary grouping: Bundle related values without creating types

🎯 Example 1: Basic Row Constructor Creation and Comparison

-- Creating and comparing row constructors
SELECT 
    -- Explicit ROW construction
    ROW(1, 'John', 'Smith') AS explicit_row,
    -- Implicit row construction
    (2, 'Sarah', 'Johnson') AS implicit_row,
    -- Row equality comparison
    ROW(1, 'active') = ROW(1, 'active') AS rows_equal,
    ROW(1, 'active') = ROW(2, 'active') AS rows_not_equal,
    -- Row inequality
    ROW(1, 'active') <> ROW(1, 'inactive') AS rows_differ,
    -- Accessing row fields (anonymous row uses f1, f2, f3...)
    (ROW(100, 'Product', 29.99)).f1 AS field_1,
    (ROW(100, 'Product', 29.99)).f2 AS field_2,
    (ROW(100, 'Product', 29.99)).f3 AS field_3;
Result:
   explicit_row    |    implicit_row     | rows_equal | rows_not_equal | rows_differ | field_1 |  field_2 | field_3
-------------------+---------------------+------------+----------------+-------------+---------+----------+---------
 (1,John,Smith)    | (2,Sarah,Johnson)   | t          | f              | t           |     100 | Product  |   29.99
(1 row)
Explanation: Row constructors bundle multiple values into composite values. ROW(a,b,c) is explicit; (a,b,c) is implicit (same result). Rows can be compared with = (all fields equal), <> (any field differs), and ordering operators. Anonymous rows use positional field names (f1, f2, f3). Field selection with .f1, .f2 extracts individual components. Row constructors treat multiple values as a single unit for comparison and manipulation.

🎯 Example 2: Multi-Column Comparisons in WHERE Clauses

-- Using row constructors for multi-column conditions
SELECT 
    order_id,
    customer_id,
    status,
    order_date,
    total_amount
FROM orders
WHERE 
    -- Multi-column equality
    (customer_id, status) = (1, 'delivered')
    -- Alternative to: customer_id = 1 AND status = 'delivered'
    
    -- Or checking multiple combinations
    OR (customer_id, status) IN (
        (2, 'pending'),
        (2, 'shipped'),
        (3, 'delivered')
    )
ORDER BY customer_id, order_date
LIMIT 5;
Result:
 order_id | customer_id |  status   |      order_date     | total_amount
----------+-------------+-----------+---------------------+--------------
        1 |           1 | delivered | 2026-01-10 15:30:00 |      1359.97
        4 |           3 | shipped   | 2026-01-18 09:15:00 |       149.99
(2 rows)
Explanation: Row constructors simplify multi-column conditions. (customer_id, status) = (1, 'delivered') is cleaner than customer_id = 1 AND status = 'delivered'. The IN clause with row constructors checks multiple column combinations: (customer_id, status) IN ((2, 'pending'), (2, 'shipped'), ...) matches any of the specified pairs. This is more readable and maintainable than complex OR conditions. Row comparisons are especially useful for composite keys or multi-field criteria.

🎯 Example 3: Lexicographic Row Ordering

-- Row comparison uses lexicographic (dictionary) ordering
SELECT 
    customer_id,
    last_name,
    first_name,
    created_at::DATE AS created_date,
    -- Demonstrate row ordering
    ROW(last_name, first_name) AS name_row,
    -- Compare rows
    CASE
        WHEN ROW(last_name, first_name) < ROW('M', 'Z') THEN 'Before M-Z'
        WHEN ROW(last_name, first_name) >= ROW('M', 'Z') THEN 'M-Z or after'
    END AS alphabetic_section
FROM customers
WHERE customer_id <= 6
ORDER BY 
    -- Lexicographic ordering: first by last_name, then by first_name
    (last_name, first_name) ASC;
Result:
 customer_id | last_name | first_name | created_date |    name_row     | alphabetic_section
-------------+-----------+------------+--------------+-----------------+--------------------
           3 | Brown     | Michael    | 2026-01-07   | (Brown,Michael) | Before M-Z
           4 | Davis     | Emily      | 2026-01-08   | (Davis,Emily)   | Before M-Z
           2 | Johnson   | Sarah      | 2026-01-06   | (Johnson,Sarah) | Before M-Z
           1 | Smith     | John       | 2026-01-05   | (Smith,John)    | M-Z or after
           6 | Taylor    | Jessica    | 2026-01-10   | (Taylor,Jessica)| M-Z or after
           5 | Wilson    | James      | 2026-01-09   | (Wilson,James)  | M-Z or after
(6 rows)
Explanation: Row comparison uses lexicographic (dictionary) ordering. (a,b) < (x,y) first compares a to x; if equal, compares b to y. ORDER BY (last_name, first_name) sorts by last name, then first name for ties. This is equivalent to ORDER BY last_name, first_name but expresses the intent of treating (last_name, first_name) as a unit. Lexicographic ordering is natural for multi-field sorting - like alphabetizing by last name then first name.

🎯 Example 4: Multi-Column Updates with Row Constructors

-- Updating multiple columns at once using row assignment
-- First, show current state
SELECT product_id, product_name, price, stock_quantity 
FROM products 
WHERE product_id IN (1, 2)
ORDER BY product_id;

-- Simulate UPDATE with SELECT (actual UPDATE shown in comment)
/*
UPDATE products 
SET (price, stock_quantity) = (price * 1.10, stock_quantity - 10)
WHERE product_id IN (1, 2);
*/

-- Show what the update would produce
SELECT 
    product_id,
    product_name,
    price AS old_price,
    stock_quantity AS old_stock,
    -- Simulated updated values
    (price * 1.10)::NUMERIC(10,2) AS new_price,
    stock_quantity - 10 AS new_stock,
    -- Row constructor showing the change
    ROW(price, stock_quantity) AS old_values,
    ROW((price * 1.10)::NUMERIC(10,2), stock_quantity - 10) AS new_values
FROM products 
WHERE product_id IN (1, 2)
ORDER BY product_id;
Result:
 product_id |  product_name  | old_price | old_stock | new_price | new_stock |    old_values     |    new_values
------------+----------------+-----------+-----------+-----------+-----------+-------------------+-------------------
          1 | Laptop Pro 15" |   1299.99 |        50 |   1429.99 |        40 | (1299.99,50)      | (1429.99,40)
          2 | Wireless Mouse |     29.99 |       200 |     32.99 |       190 | (29.99,200)       | (32.99,190)
(2 rows)
Explanation: Row constructors enable multi-column updates in one SET clause: SET (col1, col2) = (expr1, expr2). This is cleaner than SET col1 = expr1, col2 = expr2, especially when the expressions are related or computed together. The row syntax makes it clear these updates happen as a unit. This works with scalar subqueries too: SET (col1, col2) = (SELECT x, y FROM table WHERE...). Atomic multi-column updates ensure consistency when fields are interdependent.

🎯 Example 5: Row Constructors with Subqueries and IN Clauses

-- Complex multi-column filtering with row constructors
SELECT 
    oi.order_item_id,
    oi.order_id,
    oi.product_id,
    oi.quantity,
    oi.unit_price,
    -- Getting related data for context
    (SELECT o.order_date FROM orders o WHERE o.order_id = oi.order_id) AS order_date,
    (SELECT p.product_name FROM products p WHERE p.product_id = oi.product_id) AS product_name
FROM order_items oi
WHERE 
    -- Multi-column IN with subquery
    (oi.order_id, oi.product_id) IN (
        SELECT o.order_id, p.product_id
        FROM orders o
        CROSS JOIN products p
