        WHERE o.customer_id = 1
          AND p.price > 100
          AND o.status = 'delivered'
    )
    -- Alternative: row comparison with ANY
    OR (oi.order_id, oi.quantity) = ANY(
        ARRAY[ROW(1, 1), ROW(1, 2)]::RECORD[]
    )
ORDER BY oi.order_item_id
LIMIT 5;
Result:
 order_item_id | order_id | product_id | quantity | unit_price |      order_date     |  product_name
---------------+----------+------------+----------+------------+---------------------+----------------
             1 |        1 |          1 |        1 |    1299.99 | 2026-01-10 15:30:00 | Laptop Pro 15"
             2 |        1 |          2 |        2 |      29.99 | 2026-01-10 15:30:00 | Wireless Mouse
(2 rows)
Explanation: Row constructors in IN clauses enable multi-column membership testing. (order_id, product_id) IN (SELECT ...) checks if the row (order_id, product_id) matches any row from the subquery. This is more elegant than OR-ing multiple AND conditions. The = ANY operator with array of rows provides another syntax for similar logic. Row constructors make multi-column lookups, joins-without-joining, and complex filtering patterns cleaner and more maintainable. They're essential for composite key lookups.

4.2.14 Expression Evaluation Rules

📖 Full Description

Expression Evaluation Rules govern how PostgreSQL processes and computes expressions, including order of evaluation, short-circuit logic, NULL handling, and subexpression execution. Understanding these rules is critical for writing correct queries, especially when dealing with nullable data, preventing division-by-zero errors, or relying on short-circuit behavior for performance or correctness.

PostgreSQL doesn't guarantee left-to-right evaluation of subexpressions. The optimizer may reorder operations for efficiency. This means you cannot reliably use one condition to "protect" another (e.g., x != 0 AND y/x > 5 might still divide by zero). For conditional logic, use CASE expressions which guarantee evaluation order. Understanding NULL propagation - how NULL values flow through expressions - prevents unexpected query results.

Key concepts include: (1) NULL propagates through most operations (NULL + 5 = NULL), (2) Boolean logic uses three-valued logic (TRUE, FALSE, NULL), (3) Short-circuit evaluation exists for AND/OR but isn't guaranteed for other operators, (4) CASE expressions guarantee top-to-bottom evaluation, (5) Aggregate functions ignore NULLs (except COUNT(*)), (6) COALESCE and NULLIF provide NULL-handling utilities.

Expression Evaluation Concepts
-- NULL propagation (most operations)
NULL + 5 = NULL
NULL * 10 = NULL
NULL || 'text' = NULL
'text' || NULL = NULL

-- Three-valued logic (TRUE, FALSE, NULL)
TRUE AND NULL = NULL
FALSE AND NULL = FALSE
TRUE OR NULL = TRUE
FALSE OR NULL = NULL
NOT NULL = NULL

-- Short-circuit evaluation (AND, OR)
FALSE AND (expensive_function()) -- May not call function
TRUE OR (expensive_function())   -- May not call function

-- CASE guarantees evaluation order
CASE
    WHEN condition1 THEN result1  -- If TRUE, stops here
    WHEN condition2 THEN result2  -- Only evaluated if condition1 FALSE
    ELSE result3
END

-- Safe division (prevent divide-by-zero)
CASE WHEN denominator != 0 THEN numerator / denominator ELSE NULL END
-- Or using NULLIF
numerator / NULLIF(denominator, 0)

-- NULL handling functions
COALESCE(value1, value2, value3)  -- First non-NULL
NULLIF(value1, value2)            -- NULL if equal
GREATEST(val1, val2, ...)         -- Largest value (ignores NULL)
LEAST(val1, val2, ...)            -- Smallest value (ignores NULL)

-- Aggregate NULL handling
SUM(column)    -- Ignores NULLs
AVG(column)    -- Ignores NULLs
COUNT(column)  -- Counts non-NULLs
COUNT(*)       -- Counts all rows (includes NULL)

-- NULL-safe comparison
value IS NULL
value IS NOT NULL
value IS DISTINCT FROM other_value  -- NULL-aware inequality
value IS NOT DISTINCT FROM other_value  -- NULL-aware equality

Evaluation Principles

NULL Handling:

  • Propagation: NULL in arithmetic/string operations produces NULL
  • Comparisons: NULL = anything is NULL (unknown), not TRUE or FALSE
  • Boolean logic: Follows three-valued logic rules
  • Special tests: Use IS NULL, IS NOT NULL, not = NULL
  • Aggregates: Most ignore NULLs; use COUNT(*) to count all rows

Evaluation Order:

  • No guarantee: PostgreSQL may reorder for optimization
  • CASE expressions: Only construct with guaranteed order
  • Short-circuit: AND/OR may short-circuit but don't rely on it for safety
  • Subqueries: Evaluated when needed, may be optimized

Best Practices:

  • Explicit NULL handling: Use COALESCE, NULLIF, IS NULL
  • Safe division: Use NULLIF or CASE to avoid divide-by-zero
  • CASE for conditions: When evaluation order matters
  • Document assumptions: If relying on specific evaluation behavior

🎯 Example 1: NULL Propagation in Expressions

-- Demonstrating how NULL propagates through operations
SELECT 
    -- Arithmetic with NULL
    10 + NULL AS addition_null,
    100 * NULL AS multiplication_null,
    50 - NULL AS subtraction_null,
    20 / NULL AS division_null,
    -- String operations with NULL
    'Hello ' || NULL AS concat_null,
    NULL || ' World' AS null_concat,
    -- Comparisons with NULL (result is NULL, not TRUE/FALSE)
    10 > NULL AS comparison_null,
    NULL = NULL AS null_equals_null,
    -- Function calls with NULL
    LENGTH(NULL) AS length_null,
    UPPER(NULL) AS upper_null,
    -- NULL in CASE (shows NULL propagates)
    CASE WHEN NULL THEN 'yes' ELSE 'no' END AS null_in_when;
Result:
 addition_null | multiplication_null | subtraction_null | division_null | concat_null | null_concat | comparison_null | null_equals_null | length_null | upper_null | null_in_when
---------------+---------------------+------------------+---------------+-------------+-------------+-----------------+------------------+-------------+------------+--------------
 NULL          | NULL                | NULL             | NULL          | NULL        | NULL        | NULL            | NULL             | NULL        | NULL       | no
(1 row)
Explanation: NULL propagates through most operations. Any arithmetic with NULL yields NULL. String concatenation with NULL yields NULL. Comparisons with NULL yield NULL (unknown), not FALSE - this is why "column = NULL" doesn't work; use "column IS NULL" instead. NULL = NULL is NULL because each NULL represents "unknown" and we can't know if two unknowns are equal. Functions generally return NULL for NULL input. NULL in WHEN condition evaluates as FALSE (since NULL is not TRUE).

🎯 Example 2: Three-Valued Logic (TRUE, FALSE, NULL)

-- Boolean operations with TRUE, FALSE, and NULL
SELECT 
    -- AND truth table
    TRUE AND TRUE AS t_and_t,
    TRUE AND FALSE AS t_and_f,
    TRUE AND NULL AS t_and_n,
    FALSE AND NULL AS f_and_n,
    NULL AND NULL AS n_and_n,
    -- OR truth table
    TRUE OR FALSE AS t_or_f,
    TRUE OR NULL AS t_or_n,
    FALSE OR NULL AS f_or_n,
    NULL OR NULL AS n_or_n,
    -- NOT operation
    NOT TRUE AS not_t,
    NOT FALSE AS not_f,
    NOT NULL AS not_n,
    -- Practical example with nullable column
    (10 > 5) AND (NULL > 3) AS practical_and,
    (10 > 5) OR (NULL > 3) AS practical_or;
Result:
 t_and_t | t_and_f | t_and_n | f_and_n | n_and_n | t_or_f | t_or_n | f_or_n | n_or_n | not_t | not_f | not_n | practical_and | practical_or
---------+---------+---------+---------+---------+--------+--------+--------+--------+-------+-------+-------+---------------+--------------
 t       | f       | NULL    | f       | NULL    | t      | t      | NULL   | NULL   | f     | t     | NULL  | NULL          | t
(1 row)
Explanation: PostgreSQL uses three-valued logic: TRUE, FALSE, and NULL (unknown). AND returns FALSE if any operand is FALSE; otherwise NULL if any is NULL; otherwise TRUE. OR returns TRUE if any operand is TRUE; otherwise NULL if any is NULL; otherwise FALSE. NOT NULL is NULL. This affects WHERE clauses: only rows where condition is TRUE are returned, not NULL. Example: (10 > 5) AND (NULL > 3) = TRUE AND NULL = NULL, so row is filtered out. Understanding this prevents unexpected missing results.

🎯 Example 3: Safe Division and CASE Evaluation Order

-- Preventing divide-by-zero and demonstrating CASE evaluation order
SELECT 
    order_id,
    total_amount,
    (SELECT COUNT(*) FROM order_items oi WHERE oi.order_id = orders.order_id) AS item_count,
    -- UNSAFE: May divide by zero (no guaranteed evaluation order)
    -- total_amount / item_count AS unsafe_avg,
    
    -- SAFE: Using CASE (guaranteed evaluation order)
    CASE 
        WHEN (SELECT COUNT(*) FROM order_items oi WHERE oi.order_id = orders.order_id) > 0
        THEN total_amount / (SELECT COUNT(*) FROM order_items oi WHERE oi.order_id = orders.order_id)
        ELSE NULL
    END AS safe_avg_case,
    
    -- SAFE: Using NULLIF (returns NULL if denominator is 0)
    total_amount / NULLIF((SELECT COUNT(*) FROM order_items oi WHERE oi.order_id = orders.order_id), 0) AS safe_avg_nullif,
    
    -- SAFE: Using GREATEST to ensure non-zero denominator
    total_amount / GREATEST((SELECT COUNT(*) FROM order_items oi WHERE oi.order_id = orders.order_id), 1) AS safe_avg_greatest,
    
    -- CASE with multiple conditions (evaluates top-to-bottom)
    CASE
        WHEN total_amount = 0 THEN 'Free'
        WHEN total_amount < 100 THEN 'Small'
        WHEN total_amount < 1000 THEN 'Medium'
        ELSE 'Large'
    END AS order_size
FROM orders
WHERE order_id <= 5
ORDER BY order_id;
Result:
 order_id | total_amount | item_count | safe_avg_case | safe_avg_nullif | safe_avg_greatest | order_size
----------+--------------+------------+---------------+-----------------+-------------------+------------
        1 |      1359.97 |          2 |        679.99 |          679.99 |            679.99 | Large
        2 |      1329.98 |          2 |        664.99 |          664.99 |            664.99 | Large
        3 |       899.99 |          1 |        899.99 |          899.99 |            899.99 | Medium
        4 |       149.99 |          1 |        149.99 |          149.99 |            149.99 | Medium
        5 |        42.98 |          1 |         42.98 |           42.98 |             42.98 | Small
(5 rows)
Explanation: CASE expressions guarantee top-to-bottom evaluation, stopping at first TRUE condition. Use CASE to safely check before division. NULLIF(denominator, 0) returns NULL if zero, making division NULL (safe) instead of error. GREATEST(value, 1) ensures minimum of 1, preventing zero. Don't rely on AND short-circuit for safety: "denominator != 0 AND numerator/denominator" may still fail because PostgreSQL can reorder operations. CASE is the only guaranteed sequential evaluation construct. Proper NULL/zero handling prevents runtime errors.

🎯 Example 4: Aggregate Functions and NULL Handling

-- How aggregates handle NULL values
WITH test_data AS (
    SELECT * FROM (VALUES
        (1, 100, 'Active'),
        (2, 200, 'Active'),
        (3, NULL, 'Inactive'),
        (4, 150, NULL),
        (5, NULL, NULL)
    ) AS t(id, amount, status)
)
SELECT 
    -- COUNT(*) counts all rows including NULLs
    COUNT(*) AS count_all_rows,
    -- COUNT(column) counts non-NULL values
    COUNT(amount) AS count_amounts,
    COUNT(status) AS count_statuses,
    -- SUM ignores NULLs
    SUM(amount) AS sum_amounts,
    -- AVG ignores NULLs (sum of non-nulls / count of non-nulls)
    AVG(amount) AS avg_amount,
    -- MIN/MAX ignore NULLs
    MIN(amount) AS min_amount,
    MAX(amount) AS max_amount,
    -- String aggregation ignores NULLs
    STRING_AGG(status, ', ' ORDER BY status) AS statuses_concat,
    -- COUNT DISTINCT ignores NULLs
    COUNT(DISTINCT status) AS unique_statuses
FROM test_data;
Result:
 count_all_rows | count_amounts | count_statuses | sum_amounts | avg_amount | min_amount | max_amount | statuses_concat | unique_statuses
----------------+---------------+----------------+-------------+------------+------------+------------+-----------------+-----------------
              5 |             3 |              3 |         450 |     150.00 |        100 |        200 | Active, Active, | Inactive |               2
(1 row)
Explanation: Aggregate functions handle NULLs specially. COUNT(*) counts all rows (5 total). COUNT(column) counts non-NULL values (3 non-NULL amounts, 3 non-NULL statuses). SUM, AVG, MIN, MAX ignore NULLs - AVG(amount) = 450/3 = 150, not 450/5 = 90. STRING_AGG skips NULL values. COUNT DISTINCT counts unique non-NULL values. This NULL-ignoring behavior means AVG(column) ≠ SUM(column)/COUNT(*) when NULLs exist. Use COALESCE if you want NULLs treated as specific values (e.g., zero).

🎯 Example 5: NULL-Safe Comparisons and COALESCE

-- Proper NULL handling in queries
SELECT 
    customer_id,
    first_name,
    last_name,
    email,
    phone,
    -- Regular = returns NULL (not TRUE) if either side is NULL
    phone = '555-1234' AS regular_equality,
    -- IS DISTINCT FROM: NULL-aware inequality (NULL = NULL is FALSE)
    phone IS DISTINCT FROM '555-1234' AS distinct_comparison,
    -- IS NOT DISTINCT FROM: NULL-aware equality (NULL = NULL is TRUE)
    phone IS NOT DISTINCT FROM NULL AS is_null_safe,
    -- COALESCE: first non-NULL value
    COALESCE(phone, 'No phone') AS phone_with_default,
    -- NULLIF: returns NULL if values equal
    NULLIF(phone, '') AS null_if_empty,
    -- Combined: convert empty to NULL, then provide default
    COALESCE(NULLIF(phone, ''), 'No phone') AS phone_cleaned,
    -- Three-way comparison
    CASE
        WHEN phone IS NULL THEN 'Missing'
        WHEN phone = '555-1234' THEN 'Match'
        ELSE 'No match'
    END AS phone_status
FROM customers
WHERE customer_id <= 5
ORDER BY customer_id;
Result:
 customer_id | first_name | last_name |          email          |   phone   | regular_equality | distinct_comparison | is_null_safe | phone_with_default | null_if_empty | phone_cleaned | phone_status
-------------+------------+-----------+-------------------------+-----------+------------------+---------------------+--------------+--------------------+---------------+---------------+--------------
           1 | John       | Smith     | john.smith@example.com  | 555-1234  | t                | f                   | f            | 555-1234           | 555-1234      | 555-1234      | Match
           2 | Sarah      | Johnson   | sarah.j@example.com     | 555-5678  | f                | t                   | f            | 555-5678           | 555-5678      | 555-5678      | No match
           3 | Michael    | Brown     | michael.b@example.com   | 555-9012  | f                | t                   | f            | 555-9012           | 555-9012      | 555-9012      | No match
           4 | Emily      | Davis     | emily.d@example.com     | 555-3456  | f                | t                   | f            | 555-3456           | 555-3456      | 555-3456      | No match
           5 | James      | Wilson    | james.w@example.com     | 555-7890  | f                | t                   | f            | 555-7890           | 555-7890      | 555-7890      | No match
(5 rows)
Explanation: NULL requires special handling. Regular = returns NULL if either side is NULL, not TRUE or FALSE. IS DISTINCT FROM provides NULL-aware inequality (treats NULL = NULL as FALSE, so they're not distinct). IS NOT DISTINCT FROM is NULL-aware equality (NULL = NULL is TRUE). COALESCE returns first non-NULL argument (use for defaults). NULLIF returns NULL if arguments equal (convert empty strings to NULL). Combine them: COALESCE(NULLIF(value, ''), 'default') converts empty to NULL then provides default. Always use IS NULL/IS NOT NULL, never = NULL.

4.3 Calling Functions

4.3.1 Positional Notation

📖 Full Description

Positional Notation is the traditional method of calling functions where arguments are passed based on their position in the function signature. The first argument value corresponds to the first parameter, the second value to the second parameter, and so on. This is the most common and concise function call syntax, familiar from most programming languages and SQL usage.

With positional notation, argument order matters critically - swapping arguments can cause type errors or logical bugs. You must provide arguments for all required parameters in exact order, though optional parameters with defaults can be omitted from the end. This notation is ideal when function signatures are well-known, parameter count is small, and parameter meaning is obvious from context.

Most built-in PostgreSQL functions use positional notation: SUBSTRING(string, start, length), ROUND(value, decimals), COALESCE(val1, val2, val3). While concise, positional calls can become unclear with many parameters or when some have similar types (multiple integers or strings). For complex functions, named notation (covered in 4.3.2) provides better clarity.

Positional Notation Syntax
-- Basic positional notation
function_name(arg1, arg2, arg3, ...)

-- Common examples
SUBSTRING('Hello World', 1, 5)      -- string, start, length
ROUND(123.456, 2)                   -- value, decimal_places
COALESCE(NULL, NULL, 'default')     -- value1, value2, value3
LPAD('text', 10, '0')               -- string, length, fill

-- All arguments in order
SELECT 
    SUBSTRING(product_name, 1, 10) AS short_name,
    ROUND(price, 2) AS rounded_price,
    LPAD(product_id::TEXT, 5, '0') AS padded_id
FROM products;

-- Optional parameters (from end)
LEFT(string, 5)                     -- Omit optional 3rd parameter
TRIM(string)                        -- Omit optional 2nd and 3rd parameters

-- User-defined function with positional args
CREATE FUNCTION calculate_discount(
    original_price NUMERIC,
    discount_percent NUMERIC
) RETURNS NUMERIC AS $$
BEGIN
    RETURN original_price * (1 - discount_percent / 100);
END;
$$ LANGUAGE plpgsql;

-- Call with positional args
SELECT calculate_discount(100.00, 10);  -- 90.00

Positional Notation Characteristics

Advantages:

  • Concise: Shorter syntax, less typing
  • Familiar: Standard in most programming languages
  • Simple: Easy for functions with few parameters
  • Performance: Slight parsing advantage (negligible)

Disadvantages:

  • Order-dependent: Must remember parameter order
  • Error-prone: Easy to swap arguments of same type
  • Less readable: Unclear what each argument represents
  • Maintenance: Adding parameters breaks existing calls

Best Practices:

  • Use for simple functions: 1-3 well-known parameters
  • Document calls: Comment complex function calls
  • Check signatures: Verify parameter order before calling
  • Consider named: For functions with many/similar parameters

🎯 Example 1: Basic Built-in Functions with Positional Arguments

-- Common PostgreSQL functions using positional notation
SELECT 
    product_name,
    price,
    -- String functions (positional args)
    SUBSTRING(product_name, 1, 10) AS short_name,
    LEFT(product_name, 5) AS first_5_chars,
    LPAD(product_id::TEXT, 5, '0') AS padded_id,
    REPLACE(product_name, ' ', '_') AS name_with_underscores,
    -- Numeric functions (positional args)
    ROUND(price, 2) AS price_rounded,
    ROUND(price * 1.08, 2) AS price_with_tax,
    POWER(2, 3) AS two_cubed,
    -- Multiple arguments in order
    COALESCE(NULL, price, 0) AS price_or_zero,
    NULLIF(stock_quantity, 0) AS non_zero_stock
FROM products
WHERE product_id <= 3
ORDER BY product_id;
Result:
 product_name   |  price  | short_name  | first_5_chars | padded_id | name_with_underscores | price_rounded | price_with_tax | two_cubed | price_or_zero | non_zero_stock
----------------+---------+-------------+---------------+-----------+-----------------------+---------------+----------------+-----------+---------------+----------------
 Laptop Pro 15" | 1299.99 | Laptop Pro | Lapto         | 00001     | Laptop_Pro_15"        |       1299.99 |        1403.99 |         8 |       1299.99 |             50
 Wireless Mouse |   29.99 | Wireless M | Wirel         | 00002     | Wireless_Mouse        |          29.99 |          32.39 |         8 |          29.99 |            200
 Smartphone X   |  899.99 | Smartphone | Smart         | 00003     | Smartphone_X          |         899.99 |         971.99 |         8 |         899.99 |            100
(3 rows)
Explanation: Positional notation passes arguments in order defined by function signature. SUBSTRING(string, start, length) takes three arguments in that exact order. ROUND(value, decimals) takes two. LPAD(string, length, fill) takes three. Swap arguments and you get type errors or wrong results. Most built-in PostgreSQL functions use positional notation. It's concise for familiar functions but requires remembering parameter order. For infrequently-used functions, check documentation to verify argument order.

🎯 Example 2: Date/Time Functions with Multiple Positional Arguments

-- Date/time functions demonstrating positional argument order
SELECT 
    order_id,
    order_date,
    -- EXTRACT(field FROM source) - field is keyword, not string
    EXTRACT(YEAR FROM order_date) AS year,
    EXTRACT(MONTH FROM order_date) AS month,
    EXTRACT(DAY FROM order_date) AS day,
    -- DATE_PART(text, timestamp) - field as string, different order!
    DATE_PART('hour', order_date) AS hour,
    DATE_PART('minute', order_date) AS minute,
    -- TO_CHAR(timestamp, format) - two arguments
    TO_CHAR(order_date, 'YYYY-MM-DD') AS date_formatted,
    TO_CHAR(order_date, 'HH24:MI:SS') AS time_formatted,
    -- AGE(timestamp, timestamp) - two timestamps
    AGE(CURRENT_TIMESTAMP, order_date) AS order_age,
    -- DATE_TRUNC(text, timestamp) - precision then timestamp
    DATE_TRUNC('month', order_date) AS month_start,
    DATE_TRUNC('day', order_date) AS day_start
FROM orders
WHERE order_id <= 3
ORDER BY order_id;
Result:
 order_id |      order_date     | year | month | day | hour | minute | date_formatted | time_formatted |      order_age      |      month_start    |      day_start
----------+---------------------+------+-------+-----+------+--------+----------------+----------------+---------------------+---------------------+---------------------
        1 | 2026-01-10 15:30:00 | 2026 |     1 |  10 |   15 |     30 | 2026-01-10     | 15:30:00       | 15 days [varies]    | 2026-01-01 00:00:00 | 2026-01-10 00:00:00
        2 | 2026-01-12 10:30:00 | 2026 |     1 |  12 |   10 |     30 | 2026-01-12     | 10:30:00       | 13 days [varies]    | 2026-01-01 00:00:00 | 2026-01-12 00:00:00
        3 | 2026-01-15 14:20:00 | 2026 |     1 |  15 |   14 |     20 | 2026-01-15     | 14:20:00       | 10 days [varies]    | 2026-01-01 00:00:00 | 2026-01-15 00:00:00
(3 rows)
Explanation: Date/time functions show importance of argument order. DATE_PART(field, timestamp) takes field name as first argument, timestamp second. DATE_TRUNC(precision, timestamp) also takes precision first. TO_CHAR(timestamp, format) takes timestamp first, format second. AGE(end, start) calculates end - start. These orderings aren't always intuitive - DATE_PART and EXTRACT have different syntax despite similar purposes. Positional notation requires memorizing these orders or frequent documentation checks.

🎯 Example 3: Functions with Optional Parameters

-- Functions with optional parameters (can omit from end)
SELECT 
    product_name,
    -- SUBSTRING with all arguments
    SUBSTRING(product_name FROM 1 FOR 5) AS sub_explicit,
    -- SUBSTRING with optional length omitted (to end of string)
    SUBSTRING(product_name FROM 8) AS sub_from_8,
    -- TRIM with optional characters (defaults to spaces)
    TRIM('  ' || product_name || '  ') AS trimmed_default,
    -- TRIM with explicit characters to remove
    TRIM('_' FROM '___' || product_name || '___') AS trimmed_underscores,
    -- ROUND with decimal places (required)
    ROUND(price, 2) AS rounded_2,
    -- ROUND without decimal places (defaults to 0)
    ROUND(price) AS rounded_0,
    -- TO_TIMESTAMP with format (required)
    TO_TIMESTAMP('2026-01-25', 'YYYY-MM-DD') AS with_format
FROM products
WHERE product_id <= 2
ORDER BY product_id;
Result:
 product_name   | sub_explicit | sub_from_8 | trimmed_default | trimmed_underscores | rounded_2 | rounded_0 | with_format
----------------+--------------+------------+-----------------+---------------------+-----------+-----------+---------------------
 Laptop Pro 15" | Lapto        | Pro 15"    | Laptop Pro 15"  | Laptop Pro 15"      |   1299.99 |      1300 | 2026-01-25 00:00:00
 Wireless Mouse | Wirel        | ess Mouse  | Wireless Mouse  | Wireless Mouse      |     29.99 |        30 | 2026-01-25 00:00:00
(2 rows)
Explanation: Many functions have optional parameters with default values. Optional parameters can be omitted from the end of the argument list. SUBSTRING(str FROM pos FOR len) - omit FOR len to go to end. TRIM(str) uses default (spaces); TRIM(chars FROM str) specifies characters. ROUND(val, decimals) - omit decimals for integer rounding. You can only omit from the end - can't skip middle parameters with positional notation. If you need to skip parameters, use named notation (section 4.3.2) or mixed notation (4.3.3).

🎯 Example 4: User-Defined Functions with Positional Parameters

-- Demonstrating positional calls to custom functions
-- Assume these functions are defined:
-- CREATE FUNCTION calculate_tax(amount NUMERIC, rate NUMERIC) RETURNS NUMERIC
-- CREATE FUNCTION format_price(amount NUMERIC, currency TEXT, decimals INT) RETURNS TEXT

-- Simulating function behavior with expressions
SELECT 
    product_id,
    product_name,
    price,
    -- Positional: calculate_tax(price, 0.08)
    price * 1.08 AS price_with_tax,
    -- Positional with 3 args: format_price(price, 'USD', 2)
    '$' || ROUND(price, 2)::TEXT AS formatted_price_usd,
    '$' || ROUND(price, 0)::TEXT AS formatted_price_no_cents,
    '€' || ROUND(price * 0.85, 2)::TEXT AS formatted_price_eur,
    -- Multiple calculations showing argument order importance
    ROUND(price / 1.08, 2) AS price_before_tax,
    -- Wrong order would give wrong result: ROUND(1.08, price)
    -- This would try to round 1.08 to 'price' decimal places - type error!
    price * stock_quantity AS inventory_value
FROM products
WHERE product_id <= 4
ORDER BY product_id;
Result:
 product_id |  product_name  |  price  | price_with_tax | formatted_price_usd | formatted_price_no_cents | formatted_price_eur | price_before_tax | inventory_value
------------+----------------+---------+----------------+---------------------+--------------------------+---------------------+------------------+-----------------
          1 | Laptop Pro 15" | 1299.99 |        1403.99 | $1299.99            | $1300                    | €1104.99            |          1203.69 |        64999.50
          2 | Wireless Mouse |   29.99 |          32.39 | $29.99              | $30                      | €25.49              |            27.77 |         5998.00
          3 | Smartphone X   |  899.99 |         971.99 | $899.99             | $900                     | €764.99             |           833.32 |        89999.00
          4 | USB-C Cable    |   12.99 |          14.03 | $12.99              | $13                      | €11.04              |            12.03 |         6495.00
(4 rows)
Explanation: User-defined functions follow same positional notation as built-ins. Argument order must match function signature exactly. calculate_tax(amount, rate) requires amount first, rate second. Swapping gives wrong results or type errors. format_price(amount, currency, decimals) needs all three in order. With positional notation, you must provide all required arguments in exact sequence. This is error-prone for functions with many similar-type parameters (multiple NUMERICs or TEXT values). Clear function names and documentation help prevent mistakes.

🎯 Example 5: Common Pitfalls with Positional Notation

-- Demonstrating common mistakes and how to avoid them
SELECT 
    product_name,
    price,
    stock_quantity,
    -- CORRECT: LPAD(string, length, fill)
    LPAD(product_id::TEXT, 5, '0') AS correct_pad,
    -- WRONG ORDER would be: LPAD(5, product_id::TEXT, '0')
    -- This fails - can't convert integer 5 to text parameter type
    
    -- CORRECT: SUBSTRING(string, start, length)
    SUBSTRING(product_name, 1, 10) AS correct_substring,
    -- WRONG would be: SUBSTRING(1, 10, product_name)
    -- Type error - expects string first
    
    -- CORRECT: ROUND(value, decimals)
    ROUND(price, 2) AS correct_round,
    -- WRONG: ROUND(2, price) would round 2 to 'price' decimal places
    
    -- CAREFUL with similar types - easy to swap
    -- POWER(base, exponent) - which is which?
    POWER(2, 3) AS two_cubed,        -- 8
    POWER(3, 2) AS three_squared,    -- 9
    -- Swapping these gives different results!
    
    -- POSITION(substring IN string) has unusual order
    POSITION('Pro' IN product_name) AS position_of_pro,
    -- Many expect (string, substring) but it's reversed
    
    -- Best practice: use comments for clarity
    SUBSTRING(
        product_name,  -- source string
        1,             -- start position
        10             -- length
    ) AS documented_call
FROM products
WHERE product_id <= 3
ORDER BY product_id;
Result:
 product_name   |  price  | stock_quantity | correct_pad | correct_substring | correct_round | two_cubed | three_squared | position_of_pro | documented_call
----------------+---------+----------------+-------------+-------------------+---------------+-----------+---------------+-----------------+-----------------
 Laptop Pro 15" | 1299.99 |             50 | 00001       | Laptop Pro        |       1299.99 |         8 |             9 |               8 | Laptop Pro
 Wireless Mouse |   29.99 |            200 | 00002       | Wireless M        |          29.99 |         8 |             9 |               0 | Wireless M
 Smartphone X   |  899.99 |            100 | 00003       | Smartphone        |         899.99 |         8 |             9 |               0 | Smartphone
(3 rows)
Explanation: Positional notation pitfalls: (1) Swapping similar-type arguments causes wrong results, not errors - POWER(2,3) vs POWER(3,2) both valid but different. (2) Some functions have non-intuitive order - POSITION(substring IN string) reverses typical (string, substring). (3) Type mismatches cause errors but similar types don't. (4) With many arguments, it's easy to forget order. Solutions: Check documentation, use comments for clarity, consider named notation (4.3.2) for complex functions. Named notation prevents these errors by explicitly labeling each argument.

4.3.2 Named Notation

📖 Full Description

Named Notation (also called named parameters or keyword arguments) allows specifying function arguments by parameter name rather than position, using the syntax parameter_name => value. This makes function calls self-documenting, eliminates position-dependency errors, and allows omitting optional parameters from anywhere in the list, not just the end. Named notation significantly improves code clarity for complex functions.

With named notation, you explicitly label each argument: function_name(param1 => val1, param2 => val2). Arguments can appear in any order since names identify them. You can omit optional parameters regardless of position. This is invaluable for functions with many parameters, similar-type parameters, or when calling infrequently-used functions where parameter order isn't memorized.

PostgreSQL supports named notation for user-defined functions (PL/pgSQL, SQL functions) and some built-in functions. The => operator (or older := syntax) binds parameter names to values. Named notation enhances maintainability - if function signature changes (parameters added/reordered), existing named calls often remain valid. It's the preferred style for functions with 4+ parameters or when clarity is prioritized over conciseness.

Named Notation Syntax
-- Basic named notation
function_name(parameter_name => value)

-- Multiple named parameters
function_name(
    param1 => value1,
    param2 => value2,
    param3 => value3
)

-- Named parameters in any order
function_name(
    param3 => value3,
    param1 => value1,
    param2 => value2
)

-- Omitting optional parameters (any position)
function_name(
    param1 => value1,
    -- param2 skipped
    param3 => value3
)

-- User-defined function example
CREATE FUNCTION calculate_discount(
    original_price NUMERIC,
    discount_percent NUMERIC,
    minimum_order NUMERIC DEFAULT 0,
    member_bonus NUMERIC DEFAULT 0
) RETURNS NUMERIC AS $$
BEGIN
    IF original_price >= minimum_order THEN
        RETURN original_price * (1 - (discount_percent + member_bonus) / 100);
    ELSE
        RETURN original_price * (1 - discount_percent / 100);
    END IF;
END;
$$ LANGUAGE plpgsql;

-- Calling with named notation
SELECT calculate_discount(
    original_price => 100.00,
    discount_percent => 10,
    minimum_order => 50.00,
    member_bonus => 5
);

-- Omit optional parameters
SELECT calculate_discount(
    original_price => 100.00,
    discount_percent => 10
);

-- Order doesn't matter with names
SELECT calculate_discount(
    discount_percent => 10,
    original_price => 100.00
);

Named Notation Characteristics

Advantages:

  • Self-documenting: Clear what each argument represents
  • Order-independent: Arguments can be in any order
  • Flexible omission: Skip optional parameters anywhere in list
  • Maintenance-friendly: More resilient to signature changes
  • Error-prevention: Hard to pass wrong value to wrong parameter

Disadvantages:

  • Verbose: More typing than positional
  • Limited support: Not all built-in functions support it
  • Syntax overhead: => operator adds characters

When to Use:

  • Many parameters: 4+ parameters benefit from names
  • Similar types: Multiple strings, numbers, or booleans
  • Optional parameters: When skipping middle parameters
  • Infrequent calls: When parameter order isn't memorized
  • Team projects: Improves code readability for others

🎯 Example 1: Basic Named Notation vs Positional

-- Comparing positional and named notation clarity
-- Simulating a discount calculation function
WITH calculations AS (
    SELECT 
        100.00 AS original_price,
        10 AS discount_percent,
        50.00 AS minimum_order,
        5 AS member_bonus
)
SELECT 
    -- POSITIONAL (harder to understand without documentation)
    original_price * (1 - (discount_percent + member_bonus) / 100) AS positional_result,
    
    -- NAMED (self-documenting, clear intent)
    -- calculate_discount(
    --     original_price => 100.00,
    --     discount_percent => 10,
    --     minimum_order => 50.00,
    --     member_bonus => 5
    -- )
    original_price * (1 - (discount_percent + member_bonus) / 100) AS named_result,
    
    -- Breaking down to show parameter clarity
    original_price AS price,
    discount_percent AS discount,
    member_bonus AS bonus,
    (discount_percent + member_bonus) AS total_discount_pct,
    original_price * (1 - (discount_percent + member_bonus) / 100) AS final_price
FROM calculations;
Result:
 positional_result | named_result | price  | discount | bonus | total_discount_pct | final_price
-------------------+--------------+--------+----------+-------+--------------------+-------------
             85.00 |        85.00 | 100.00 |       10 |     5 |                 15 |       85.00
(1 row)
Explanation: Named notation makes function calls self-documenting. Compare calculate_discount(100, 10, 50, 5) (positional - what do these numbers mean?) versus calculate_discount(original_price => 100, discount_percent => 10, minimum_order => 50, member_bonus => 5) (named - immediately clear). Named notation prevents passing values to wrong parameters. If function has calculate_discount(price, discount, minimum, bonus), positional requires exact order. Named notation: order irrelevant, intent explicit.

🎯 Example 2: Order Independence with Named Parameters

-- Named parameters can be provided in any order
-- Simulating format_address function
WITH addresses AS (
    SELECT 
        '123 Main St' AS street,
        'Springfield' AS city,
        'IL' AS state,
        '62701' AS zip_code,
        'USA' AS country
)
SELECT 
    -- Traditional order
    street || ', ' || city || ', ' || state || ' ' || zip_code || ', ' || country AS traditional_order,
    
    -- Different order (if using named notation, order doesn't matter)
    -- format_address(city => city, state => state, street => street, zip_code => zip_code, country => country)
    street || ', ' || city || ', ' || state || ' ' || zip_code || ', ' || country AS named_any_order,
    
    -- Demonstrating that result is same regardless of argument order
    -- format_address(country => country, zip_code => zip_code, state => state, city => city, street => street)
    street || ', ' || city || ', ' || state || ' ' || zip_code || ', ' || country AS named_reverse_order,
    
    -- All produce same result because names identify parameters
    street AS p_street,
    city AS p_city,
    state AS p_state,
    zip_code AS p_zip,
    country AS p_country
FROM addresses;
Result:
            traditional_order            |          named_any_order             |         named_reverse_order           |  p_street   |    p_city    | p_state | p_zip | p_country
------------------------------------------+--------------------------------------+---------------------------------------+-------------+--------------+---------+-------+-----------
 123 Main St, Springfield, IL 62701, USA | 123 Main St, Springfield, IL 62701..| 123 Main St, Springfield, IL 62701..| 123 Main St | Springfield  | IL      | 62701 | USA
(1 row)
Explanation: Named notation's order independence is powerful. format_address(street => s, city => c, state => st, zip => z, country => co) produces same result as format_address(country => co, zip => z, state => st, city => c, street => s). Parameter names bind values regardless of position. This prevents errors when parameter order isn't memorized or when working with unfamiliar functions. You can organize arguments logically (required first, optional last, or alphabetically) without affecting behavior.

🎯 Example 3: Omitting Optional Parameters Flexibly

-- Named notation allows skipping optional parameters anywhere
-- Simulating send_notification function with many optional parameters
WITH notifications AS (
    SELECT 
        'order123' AS notification_id,
        'New Order' AS subject,
        'user@example.com' AS recipient,
        'Your order has been placed' AS message,
        'email' AS channel,
        'high' AS priority,
        true AS send_copy_to_admin,
        false AS require_confirmation
)
SELECT 
    notification_id,
    subject,
    recipient,
    -- With positional: must provide all parameters up to last one needed
    -- send_notification('order123', 'New Order', 'user@example.com', 'message', 'email', 'high', true, false)
    
    -- With named: can skip any optional parameters
    -- send_notification(
    --     notification_id => 'order123',
    --     subject => 'New Order',
    --     recipient => 'user@example.com',
    --     -- skip message (uses default)
    --     channel => 'email',
    --     priority => 'high'
    --     -- skip send_copy_to_admin and require_confirmation (use defaults)
    -- )
    
    channel,
    priority,
    -- Demonstrating selective inclusion
    CASE WHEN send_copy_to_admin THEN 'Admin CC' ELSE 'No CC' END AS admin_copy,
    CASE WHEN require_confirmation THEN 'Needs Confirm' ELSE 'No Confirm' END AS confirmation
FROM notifications;
Result:
 notification_id |  subject  |     recipient      | channel | priority | admin_copy | confirmation
-----------------+-----------+--------------------+---------+----------+------------+--------------
 order123        | New Order | user@example.com   | email   | high     | Admin CC   | No Confirm
(1 row)
Explanation: Named notation's killer feature: skip optional parameters anywhere. Positional requires: send_notification(id, subject, recipient, message, channel, priority, admin_copy, confirm_needed). To skip 'message' but provide 'channel', you'd need placeholder (NULL or default). Named notation: send_notification(id => 'x', subject => 'y', recipient => 'z', channel => 'email', priority => 'high') - skip message entirely. Perfect for functions with many optional parameters where you only need to set a few. Common in configuration functions or API-style calls.

🎯 Example 4: Complex Function with Many Similar-Type Parameters

-- Functions with many similar-type parameters benefit from named notation
-- Simulating create_report function with multiple TEXT and NUMERIC parameters
WITH report_config AS (
    SELECT 
        'Monthly Sales Report' AS report_title,
        'Sales Analysis' AS report_subtitle,
        'John Doe' AS author_name,
        'Sales' AS department_name,
        '2026-01' AS period_start,
        '2026-01' AS period_end,
        'landscape' AS page_orientation,
        'detailed' AS detail_level,
        'summary_first' AS section_order,
        true AS include_charts,
        true AS include_raw_data,
        10 AS top_n_products,
        5 AS top_n_customers
)
SELECT 
    -- POSITIONAL (error-prone with many TEXT parameters)
    -- create_report('Monthly Sales Report', 'Sales Analysis', 'John Doe', 'Sales', 
    --               '2026-01', '2026-01', 'landscape', 'detailed', 'summary_first',
    --               true, true, 10, 5)
    -- Which string is which? Easy to mix up!
    
    -- NAMED (clear and self-documenting)
    -- create_report(
    --     report_title => 'Monthly Sales Report',
    --     report_subtitle => 'Sales Analysis',
    --     author_name => 'John Doe',
    --     department_name => 'Sales',
    --     period_start => '2026-01',
    --     period_end => '2026-01',
    --     page_orientation => 'landscape',
    --     detail_level => 'detailed',
    --     section_order => 'summary_first',
    --     include_charts => true,
    --     include_raw_data => true,
    --     top_n_products => 10,
    --     top_n_customers => 5
    -- )
    
    report_title,
    author_name,
    department_name,
    CONCAT(period_start, ' to ', period_end) AS period,
    page_orientation AS orientation,
    top_n_products AS top_products,
    top_n_customers AS top_customers
FROM report_config;
Result:
     report_title      | author_name | department_name |       period        | orientation | top_products | top_customers
-----------------------+-------------+-----------------+---------------------+-------------+--------------+---------------
 Monthly Sales Report  | John Doe    | Sales           | 2026-01 to 2026-01  | landscape   |           10 |             5
(1 row)
Explanation: Functions with many similar-type parameters are nightmares with positional notation. create_report with 9 TEXT parameters, 2 BOOLEAN, 2 INTEGER - which TEXT goes where? Positional: create_report('A', 'B', 'C', 'D', 'E', 'F', 'G', 'H', 'I', ...) - meaningless without documentation. Named: report_title => 'A', author_name => 'B', etc. - instantly clear. Named notation prevents "passing department_name where author_name expected" errors. For APIs, config functions, or complex business logic, named notation is essential for maintainability and correctness.

🎯 Example 5: Maintenance and Refactoring Benefits

-- Named notation makes code more resilient to changes
-- Original function: send_email(recipient, subject, body)
-- Updated function: send_email(recipient, subject, body, cc, bcc, priority)

-- POSITIONAL calls break when signature changes:
-- OLD: send_email('user@example.com', 'Subject', 'Body')
-- NEW: send_email('user@example.com', 'Subject', 'Body', NULL, NULL, 'normal')
-- Must update ALL calls, even if not using new parameters

-- NAMED calls often remain valid:
-- OLD: send_email(recipient => 'user@example.com', subject => 'Subject', body => 'Body')
-- NEW: Same call still works! New parameters have defaults.

-- Demonstrating resilience
WITH email_data AS (
    SELECT 
        'customer@example.com' AS recipient,
        'Order Confirmation' AS subject,
        'Your order #123 is confirmed' AS body,
        NULL AS cc,
        NULL AS bcc,
        'high' AS priority
)
SELECT 
    recipient,
    subject,
    body,
    -- Original call (3 parameters)
    CONCAT('TO: ', recipient, ' | SUBJECT: ', subject) AS original_call,
    
    -- Extended call (6 parameters)
    CONCAT('TO: ', recipient, 
           ' | SUBJECT: ', subject,
           ' | PRIORITY: ', COALESCE(priority, 'normal')) AS extended_call,
    
    -- Named notation version would work with both signatures:
    -- send_email(recipient => recipient, subject => subject, body => body)
    -- send_email(recipient => recipient, subject => subject, body => body, priority => priority)
    
    priority,
    CASE WHEN cc IS NULL THEN 'No CC' ELSE cc END AS cc_status
FROM email_data;
Result:
       recipient        |      subject       |               body               |              original_call               |                  extended_call                     | priority | cc_status
------------------------+--------------------+----------------------------------+------------------------------------------+----------------------------------------------------+----------+-----------
 customer@example.com   | Order Confirmation | Your order #123 is confirmed     | TO: customer@example.com | SUBJECT: O...| TO: customer@example.com | SUBJECT: Order...|high     |  high     | No CC
(1 row)
Explanation: Named notation shines during maintenance. Function signature changes from send_email(recipient, subject, body) to send_email(recipient, subject, body, cc, bcc, priority, attachment). Positional calls break or need updating: send_email('a', 'b', 'c') might now need send_email('a', 'b', 'c', NULL, NULL, 'normal', NULL). Named calls: send_email(recipient => 'a', subject => 'b', body => 'c') still work if new params have defaults. Adding parameters at end doesn't break named calls. Even reordering parameters doesn't break them (though not recommended). Named notation = less maintenance burden, fewer bugs after refactoring.

4.3.3 Mixed Notation

📖 Full Description

Mixed Notation combines positional and named parameters in a single function call, providing a balance between conciseness and clarity. The syntax requires all positional arguments to appear first, followed by any named arguments: function_name(positional_arg1, positional_arg2, named_param => value). Once you use a named argument, all subsequent arguments must also be named. This rule prevents ambiguity in parameter binding.

Mixed notation is ideal when a function has a few obvious required parameters followed by many optional ones. You can use positional notation for the common, well-known parameters (saving keystrokes) and switch to named notation for optional or less-obvious parameters (adding clarity). For example, send_email('user@example.com', 'Subject', 'Body', priority => 'high', cc => 'admin@example.com') uses positional for the clear recipient/subject/body and named for optional priority/cc.

The transition from positional to named must be clean - you cannot return to positional after using named notation. PostgreSQL enforces this to avoid confusion. Mixed notation offers flexibility: use it when positional alone is too error-prone (many parameters) but named alone feels unnecessarily verbose (obvious first parameters). It's common in database APIs, ORMs, and functions with "required positional + optional named" patterns.

Mixed Notation Syntax
-- Mixed notation: positional first, then named
function_name(
    positional_arg1,
    positional_arg2,
    named_param3 => value3,
    named_param4 => value4
)

-- VALID: Positional arguments before named
SELECT function(
    'arg1',           -- positional
    'arg2',           -- positional
    param3 => 'val3', -- named (transition point)
    param4 => 'val4'  -- named (all subsequent must be named)
);

-- INVALID: Cannot use positional after named
SELECT function(
    'arg1',           -- positional
    param2 => 'val2', -- named (transition)
    'arg3'            -- ERROR: positional after named not allowed
);

-- Practical example: required positional + optional named
CREATE FUNCTION format_currency(
    amount NUMERIC,                    -- required, obvious (positional)
    currency_code TEXT DEFAULT 'USD',  -- optional (named)
    decimal_places INTEGER DEFAULT 2,  -- optional (named)
    use_symbol BOOLEAN DEFAULT true,   -- optional (named)
    thousands_sep TEXT DEFAULT ','     -- optional (named)
) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
BEGIN
    -- Function implementation
    RETURN CONCAT(
        CASE WHEN use_symbol THEN 
            CASE currency_code
                WHEN 'USD' THEN '$'
                WHEN 'EUR' THEN '€'
                WHEN 'GBP' THEN '£'
                ELSE currency_code || ' '
            END
        ELSE currency_code || ' ' END,
        TO_CHAR(amount, 'FM999' || thousands_sep || '999' || 
                REPEAT('0', decimal_places))
    );
END;
$$ LANGUAGE plpgsql;

-- Mixed notation: positional for amount, named for options
SELECT format_currency(
    1234.567,                  -- positional (required, obvious)
    decimal_places => 3,       -- named (optional, clarity)
    thousands_sep => '_'       -- named (optional, clarity)
);

-- Pure positional (less clear)
SELECT format_currency(1234.567, 'USD', 3, true, '_');

-- Pure named (more verbose)
SELECT format_currency(
    amount => 1234.567,
    currency_code => 'USD',
    decimal_places => 3,
    use_symbol => true,
    thousands_sep => '_'
);

Mixed Notation Rules and Best Practices

Syntax Rules:

  • Positional first: All positional arguments must precede named arguments
  • No returning: Once named notation starts, cannot use positional again
  • Explicit transition: First named parameter marks the transition point
  • Order after transition: Named parameters can be in any order after transition

When to Use Mixed Notation:

  • Obvious required + optional: Common parameters positional, rare ones named
  • API-style functions: Primary data positional, configuration named
  • Partial clarity needed: First N parameters memorized, rest need labels
  • Variable optional usage: Sometimes skip optionals, sometimes set specific ones

Best Practices:

  • Keep positional count low: 1-3 positional, rest named
  • Positional for required: Required parameters without defaults work well positionally
  • Named for optional: Parameters with defaults benefit from named notation
  • Consistency in codebase: Team should agree on positional/named boundary
  • Document transition point: Comment where positional ends, named begins

🎯 Example 1: Basic Mixed Notation - Required Positional + Optional Named

-- Function with clear required parameters and various optional ones
-- Simulating create_user function
WITH user_data AS (
    SELECT 
        'john.doe@example.com' AS email,
        'password123' AS password_hash,
        'John' AS first_name,
        'Doe' AS last_name,
        'admin' AS role,
        true AS email_verified,
        false AS send_welcome_email,
        'UTC' AS timezone
)
SELECT 
    email,
    first_name,
    last_name,
    -- POSITIONAL ONLY (less clear for optional params)
    -- create_user('john.doe@example.com', 'hash', 'John', 'Doe', 'admin', true, false, 'UTC')
    -- What are true/false? What is 'admin'?
    
    -- MIXED NOTATION (best balance)
    -- create_user(
    --     'john.doe@example.com',  -- positional (required, obvious)
    --     'hash',                   -- positional (required, obvious)
    --     role => 'admin',          -- named (clarity for optional)
    --     email_verified => true,   -- named (clarity)
    --     timezone => 'UTC'         -- named (clarity)
    -- )
    
    role,
    CASE WHEN email_verified THEN 'Verified' ELSE 'Not Verified' END AS verification_status,
    CASE WHEN send_welcome_email THEN 'Will Send Welcome' ELSE 'No Welcome' END AS welcome_status,
    timezone,
    CONCAT(first_name, ' ', last_name, ' (', role, ')') AS full_profile
FROM user_data;
Result:
         email          | first_name | last_name | role  | verification_status | welcome_status   | timezone |    full_profile
------------------------+------------+-----------+-------+---------------------+------------------+----------+---------------------
 john.doe@example.com   | John       | Doe       | admin | Verified            | No Welcome       | UTC      | John Doe (admin)
(1 row)
Explanation: Mixed notation balances brevity and clarity. create_user has 8 parameters: email and password (required, always used) work well positionally. Optional parameters (role, email_verified, send_welcome_email, timezone) benefit from named notation for clarity. Mixed call: create_user(email, password, role => 'admin', verified => true) - clear required data, explicit optional config. Pure positional create_user(email, pass, 'J', 'D', 'admin', true, false, 'UTC') - what's what? Pure named is overly verbose for email/password. Mixed is the sweet spot.

🎯 Example 2: Transition Point Rules

-- Demonstrating positional-to-named transition rules
-- Simulating calculate_shipping function
WITH shipping_calc AS (
    SELECT 
        10.50 AS weight_kg,
        'CA' AS destination_state,
        'standard' AS shipping_method,
        false AS is_expedited,
        true AS is_insured,
        100.00 AS insurance_value,
        'residential' AS address_type
)
SELECT 
    weight_kg,
    destination_state,
    shipping_method,
    -- VALID: Positional, positional, then all named
    -- calculate_shipping(
    --     10.50,                        -- positional: weight
    --     'CA',                         -- positional: destination
    --     shipping_method => 'standard', -- TRANSITION: first named parameter
    --     is_expedited => false,        -- named (can be any order)
    --     address_type => 'residential', -- named
    --     is_insured => true            -- named (different order from definition)
    -- )
    
    -- INVALID example (would cause error):
    -- calculate_shipping(
    --     10.50,                        -- positional
    --     shipping_method => 'standard', -- named (transition)
    --     'CA'                          -- ERROR: positional after named
    -- )
    
    CASE WHEN is_expedited THEN 'Expedited' ELSE 'Standard' END AS speed,
    CASE WHEN is_insured THEN CONCAT('Insured ($', insurance_value, ')') ELSE 'Not Insured' END AS insurance,
    address_type,
    -- Calculated shipping cost simulation
    weight_kg * 2.5 + 
        CASE WHEN is_expedited THEN 10 ELSE 0 END +
        CASE WHEN is_insured THEN 5 ELSE 0 END AS estimated_cost
FROM shipping_calc;
Result:
 weight_kg | destination_state | shipping_method |   speed   |     insurance      | address_type | estimated_cost
-----------+-------------------+-----------------+-----------+--------------------+--------------+----------------
     10.50 | CA                | standard        | Standard  | Insured ($100)     | residential  |          31.25
(1 row)
Explanation: Transition point rules prevent ambiguity. calculate_shipping(10.5, 'CA', method => 'standard', expedited => false) is VALID: first two positional, then all named. calculate_shipping(10.5, method => 'standard', 'CA') is INVALID: can't use positional 'CA' after named method. Why? PostgreSQL wouldn't know if 'CA' is destination (position 2) or another named parameter. Once you use name => value, all subsequent must be name => value. Named parameters after transition can be in any order: method => X, insured => Y, expedited => Z equals expedited => Z, method => X, insured => Y.

🎯 Example 3: Optimal Use Case - API-Style Functions

-- API-style function: data positional, config named
-- Simulating send_notification function
WITH notifications AS (
    SELECT 
        'order_placed' AS event_type,
        '{"order_id": "12345", "amount": 299.99}' AS event_data,
        'high' AS priority,
        false AS send_sms,
        true AS send_email,
        false AS send_push,
        3 AS retry_attempts,
        300 AS retry_delay_seconds
)
SELECT 
    event_type,
    event_data,
    -- MIXED NOTATION (ideal for API functions)
    -- send_notification(
    --     'order_placed',                 -- positional: event (required, primary data)
    --     '{"order_id": "12345", ...}',   -- positional: data (required, primary data)
    --     priority => 'high',             -- named: config (optional, clarity)
    --     send_email => true,             -- named: config
    --     retry_attempts => 3,            -- named: config
    --     retry_delay_seconds => 300      -- named: config
    -- )
    -- Core data positional, configuration named
    
    priority,
    CONCAT(
        CASE WHEN send_email THEN 'Email ' ELSE '' END,
        CASE WHEN send_sms THEN 'SMS ' ELSE '' END,
        CASE WHEN send_push THEN 'Push' ELSE '' END
    ) AS channels,
    retry_attempts,
    CONCAT(retry_delay_seconds, 's between retries') AS retry_policy,
    -- Simulated notification result
    CONCAT('Sending ', event_type, ' notification with priority ', priority) AS result
FROM notifications;
Result:
  event_type   |                event_data                  | priority | channels | retry_attempts |    retry_policy      |                     result
---------------+--------------------------------------------+----------+----------+----------------+----------------------+------------------------------------------------
 order_placed  | {"order_id": "12345", "amount": 299.99}    | high     | Email    |              3 | 300s between retries | Sending order_placed notification with priority high
(1 row)
Explanation: API-style functions perfect for mixed notation. send_notification(event, data, options...) - event and data are core payload (positional makes sense), while priority, channels, retry policy are configuration (named for clarity). Mixed: send_notification('event', '{data}', priority => 'high', retries => 3) - data up front, config labeled. This pattern common in: logging functions (message positional, level/tags named), API clients (endpoint/body positional, headers/timeout named), notification systems (recipient/content positional, delivery options named). Clear separation: "what to send" (positional) vs "how to send it" (named).

🎯 Example 4: Skipping Optional Parameters with Mixed Notation

-- Mixed notation allows flexible optional parameter handling
-- Simulating generate_report function with many optionals
WITH report_settings AS (
    SELECT 
        'sales_report' AS report_type,
        '2026-01-01' AS start_date,
        '2026-01-31' AS end_date,
        'pdf' AS output_format,
        true AS include_summary,
        true AS include_details,
        false AS include_charts,
        false AS include_raw_data,
        'monthly' AS aggregation_level,
        'department' AS group_by_column
)
SELECT 
    report_type,
    CONCAT(start_date, ' to ', end_date) AS period,
    -- POSITIONAL ONLY (must provide all up to last used)
    -- generate_report('sales_report', '2026-01-01', '2026-01-31', 'pdf', true, true, false, false, 'monthly', 'department')
    -- Hard to read, must provide even unused parameters
    
    -- MIXED NOTATION (skip unwanted optionals easily)
    -- generate_report(
    --     'sales_report',           -- positional: report type (required)
    --     '2026-01-01',             -- positional: start (required)
    --     '2026-01-31',             -- positional: end (required)
    --     output_format => 'pdf',   -- named: only set what's needed
    --     include_summary => true,  -- named: clear intent
    --     aggregation_level => 'monthly' -- named: skip charts, raw_data, details
    -- )
    
    output_format,
    CONCAT(
        CASE WHEN include_summary THEN 'Summary ' ELSE '' END,
        CASE WHEN include_details THEN 'Details ' ELSE '' END,
        CASE WHEN include_charts THEN 'Charts ' ELSE '' END,
        CASE WHEN include_raw_data THEN 'Raw Data' ELSE '' END
    ) AS sections,
    aggregation_level,
    group_by_column,
    CONCAT('Generating ', report_type, ' for ', aggregation_level, ' view') AS status
FROM report_settings;
Result:
  report_type  |            period            | output_format |          sections           | aggregation_level | group_by_column |                    status
---------------+------------------------------+---------------+-----------------------------+-------------------+-----------------+-----------------------------------------------
 sales_report  | 2026-01-01 to 2026-01-31     | pdf           | Summary Details             | monthly           | department      | Generating sales_report for monthly view
(1 row)
Explanation: Mixed notation excels at selective optional parameters. generate_report has 10 parameters: 3 required (type, start, end), 7 optional. Positional: must provide all 10 even if only using some: generate_report('type', 'start', 'end', 'pdf', true, false, false, false, NULL, NULL, 'monthly') - clunky. Mixed: generate_report('type', 'start', 'end', format => 'pdf', summary => true, aggregation => 'monthly') - skip unused optionals entirely. Only provide what's needed, in any order after positional. Perfect for functions where you rarely use all optionals but often use specific combinations.

🎯 Example 5: Real-World Pattern - Balancing Conciseness and Clarity

-- Practical mixed notation in production code
-- Simulating log_event function (common in applications)
WITH events AS (
    SELECT 
        'user_login' AS event_name,
        'INFO' AS severity,
        '{"user_id": 1001, "ip": "192.168.1.100"}' AS event_payload,
        'authentication' AS category,
        'user_service' AS source_component,
        'prod' AS environment,
        true AS persist_to_database,
        false AS send_to_external_service,
        '2026-01-15 10:30:00' AS event_timestamp
)
SELECT 
    event_name,
    severity,
    -- COMPARISON OF STYLES:
    
    -- 1. Pure Positional (shortest but unclear)
    -- log_event('user_login', 'INFO', '{...}', 'auth', 'service', 'prod', true, false, '2026-01-15 10:30:00')
    -- What's 'auth'? What's true/false?
    
    -- 2. Pure Named (clearest but verbose)
    -- log_event(
    --     event_name => 'user_login',
    --     severity => 'INFO',
    --     event_payload => '{...}',
    --     category => 'authentication',
    --     source_component => 'user_service',
    --     environment => 'prod',
    --     persist_to_database => true,
    --     send_to_external_service => false,
    --     event_timestamp => '2026-01-15 10:30:00'
    -- )
    -- Very clear but lots of typing for obvious parameters
    
    -- 3. Mixed (OPTIMAL: concise + clear)
    -- log_event(
    --     'user_login',                    -- positional: obvious (event name always first)
    --     'INFO',                          -- positional: obvious (severity always second)
    --     '{...}',                         -- positional: obvious (payload always third)
    --     category => 'authentication',    -- named: adds context
    --     environment => 'prod',           -- named: important but not always obvious
    --     persist_to_database => true      -- named: configuration detail
    -- )
    
    category,
    source_component,
    environment,
    CASE WHEN persist_to_database THEN 'Persisted' ELSE 'Transient' END AS storage,
    CASE WHEN send_to_external_service THEN 'External' ELSE 'Internal Only' END AS distribution,
    CONCAT('[', severity, '] ', event_name, ' in ', environment) AS log_entry
FROM events;
Result:
  event_name  | severity |                event_payload                  |    category     | source_component | environment |  storage   |  distribution  |          log_entry
--------------+----------+-----------------------------------------------+-----------------+------------------+-------------+------------+----------------+------------------------------
 user_login   | INFO     | {"user_id": 1001, "ip": "192.168.1.100"}      | authentication  | user_service     | prod        | Persisted  | Internal Only  | [INFO] user_login in prod
(1 row)
Explanation: Real-world best practice: use mixed notation for "core + config" pattern. Logging example: event name, severity, payload are core (positional - always provided, order memorized). Category, environment, persistence are config (named - varies by call, benefits from labels). Mixed notation hits sweet spot: log_event('login', 'INFO', data, category => 'auth', env => 'prod') - compact for essentials, explicit for details. Guidelines: positional for ≤3 most common/required parameters, named for everything else. Team consistency matters: agree which parameters go positional vs named. Document the boundary in code/standards.

Chapter 5. Data Definition

📖 Chapter Overview

Data Definition Language (DDL) encompasses SQL commands for creating, modifying, and managing database structures. This chapter covers table creation, column definitions, constraints, schemas, partitioning, and the full lifecycle of database objects. Understanding DDL is fundamental to designing robust, scalable PostgreSQL databases.

PostgreSQL provides comprehensive DDL capabilities including advanced features like identity columns, generated columns, table inheritance, declarative partitioning, row-level security, and foreign data wrappers. This chapter explores both basic table operations and sophisticated data organization strategies.

Topics covered: table basics, default values, identity and generated columns, constraint types (CHECK, NOT NULL, UNIQUE, PRIMARY KEY, FOREIGN KEY, EXCLUSION), system columns, table modifications (ALTER TABLE), privileges (GRANT/REVOKE), row security policies, schemas and namespaces, table inheritance, partitioning strategies, foreign data, and dependency tracking.

5.1 Table Basics

📖 Full Description

Tables are the fundamental data storage structures in relational databases, organizing data into rows (records) and columns (fields). Each table has a unique name within its schema and consists of typed columns that define what data can be stored. PostgreSQL tables support various column types including numeric, text, date/time, boolean, arrays, JSON, and custom types.

Creating a table requires specifying the table name, column names, and data types using the CREATE TABLE command. Tables can include constraints (rules that validate data), default values, and optional features like identity columns or generated columns. Proper table design involves choosing appropriate data types, naming conventions, and normalization strategies to ensure data integrity and query performance.

PostgreSQL tables are schema-qualified objects, meaning they belong to a specific schema (namespace). Tables can be temporary (session-scoped) or permanent, unlogged (faster but not crash-safe), or regular. Understanding table basics is essential for all database operations since tables hold the actual data your applications manipulate.

Table Creation Syntax
-- Basic table creation
CREATE TABLE table_name (
    column1_name data_type,
    column2_name data_type,
    column3_name data_type
);

-- Table with constraints
CREATE TABLE table_name (
    column1_name data_type PRIMARY KEY,
    column2_name data_type NOT NULL,
    column3_name data_type UNIQUE,
    column4_name data_type CHECK (condition)
);

-- Temporary table (session-scoped)
CREATE TEMP TABLE temp_table_name (
    column1_name data_type,
    column2_name data_type
);

-- Unlogged table (faster writes, not crash-safe)
CREATE UNLOGGED TABLE cache_table (
    key TEXT PRIMARY KEY,
    value TEXT,
    expires_at TIMESTAMP
);

-- Table IF NOT EXISTS (avoid errors if exists)
CREATE TABLE IF NOT EXISTS table_name (
    id SERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    name TEXT NOT NULL
);

-- Complete example with multiple features
CREATE TABLE products (
    product_id SERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    product_name VARCHAR(100) NOT NULL,
    category_id INTEGER,
    price NUMERIC(10,2) CHECK (price >= 0),
    stock_quantity INTEGER DEFAULT 0,
    is_active BOOLEAN DEFAULT true,
    created_at TIMESTAMP DEFAULT CURRENT_TIMESTAMP
);

Table Creation Parameters

Table Name:

  • Naming rules: Must start with letter or underscore, max 63 bytes, case-insensitive unless quoted
  • Schema qualification: Can specify schema: schema_name.table_name
  • Conventions: Use lowercase with underscores (snake_case), plural nouns recommended

Column Definitions:

  • column_name: Unique within table, follows same naming rules as tables
  • data_type: Defines what kind of data the column stores (INTEGER, TEXT, TIMESTAMP, etc.)
  • Constraints: Optional rules like NOT NULL, UNIQUE, CHECK, PRIMARY KEY, FOREIGN KEY
  • Defaults: Optional DEFAULT clause provides value when none specified

Table Types:

  • Permanent: Regular tables, data survives session end and server restarts
  • Temporary: TEMP or TEMPORARY keyword, exists only for session duration
  • Unlogged: UNLOGGED keyword, faster writes but lost on crash (use for caches)

Common Data Types:

  • Numeric: INTEGER, BIGINT, NUMERIC(precision, scale), REAL, DOUBLE PRECISION
  • Text: TEXT, VARCHAR(n), CHAR(n)
  • Date/Time: DATE, TIME, TIMESTAMP, TIMESTAMPTZ, INTERVAL
  • Boolean: BOOLEAN (true/false/null)
  • Special: SERIAL, BIGSERIAL (auto-incrementing), UUID, JSON, JSONB, ARRAY

🎯 Example 1: Basic Table Creation

-- Create a simple customers table
CREATE TABLE customers (
    customer_id SERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    first_name VARCHAR(50) NOT NULL,
    last_name VARCHAR(50) NOT NULL,
    email VARCHAR(100) UNIQUE NOT NULL,
    phone VARCHAR(20),
    created_at TIMESTAMP DEFAULT CURRENT_TIMESTAMP
);

-- Verify table was created
SELECT 
    table_schema,
    table_name,
    table_type
FROM information_schema.tables
WHERE table_name = 'customers';
Result:
 table_schema | table_name | table_type
--------------+------------+------------
 public       | customers  | BASE TABLE
(1 row)
Explanation: Basic table creation with SERIAL primary key (auto-incrementing integer), VARCHAR text columns with length limits, NOT NULL constraints ensuring required fields, UNIQUE constraint on email preventing duplicates, optional phone column (can be NULL), and DEFAULT for automatic timestamp. The information_schema query confirms table creation in the public schema. SERIAL creates a sequence for auto-generating IDs.

🎯 Example 2: Table with Multiple Constraints

-- Create orders table with various constraints
CREATE TABLE orders (
    order_id SERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    customer_id INTEGER NOT NULL,
    order_date DATE NOT NULL DEFAULT CURRENT_DATE,
    total_amount NUMERIC(10,2) CHECK (total_amount >= 0),
    status VARCHAR(20) CHECK (status IN ('pending', 'processing', 'shipped', 'delivered', 'cancelled')),
    shipping_address TEXT NOT NULL,
    created_at TIMESTAMP DEFAULT CURRENT_TIMESTAMP,
    updated_at TIMESTAMP DEFAULT CURRENT_TIMESTAMP
);

-- Insert sample data
INSERT INTO orders (customer_id, total_amount, status, shipping_address) 
VALUES 
    (1, 299.99, 'pending', '123 Main St, Springfield, IL 62701'),
    (2, 149.50, 'shipped', '456 Oak Ave, Chicago, IL 60601');

-- Query created orders
SELECT order_id, customer_id, total_amount, status 
FROM orders 
ORDER BY order_id;
Result:
 order_id | customer_id | total_amount |  status
----------+-------------+--------------+---------
        1 |           1 |       299.99 | pending
        2 |           2 |       149.50 | shipped
(2 rows)
Explanation: Table demonstrates multiple constraint types: CHECK (total_amount >= 0) prevents negative amounts, CHECK with IN enforces valid status values only, NOT NULL requires customer_id and shipping_address, DEFAULT CURRENT_DATE auto-fills order_date, DEFAULT CURRENT_TIMESTAMP tracks creation/update times. Constraints validate data at INSERT/UPDATE time, rejecting invalid data before storage. Try INSERT INTO orders (customer_id, total_amount, status, shipping_address) VALUES (1, -50, 'pending', 'address') would fail due to CHECK constraint.

🎯 Example 3: Temporary Tables

-- Create temporary table for session calculations
CREATE TEMP TABLE session_cart (
    cart_id SERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    product_id INTEGER NOT NULL,
    quantity INTEGER CHECK (quantity > 0),
    added_at TIMESTAMP DEFAULT CURRENT_TIMESTAMP
);

-- Insert items
INSERT INTO session_cart (product_id, quantity) 
VALUES (101, 2), (102, 1), (103, 5);

-- Query session cart
SELECT cart_id, product_id, quantity, 
       TO_CHAR(added_at, 'HH24:MI:SS') AS time_added
FROM session_cart;

-- Check if temp table exists in pg_tables
SELECT schemaname, tablename, temporary 
FROM pg_tables 
WHERE tablename = 'session_cart';
Result:
-- Session cart contents:
 cart_id | product_id | quantity | time_added
---------+------------+----------+------------
       1 |        101 |        2 | 14:32:15
       2 |        102 |        1 | 14:32:15
       3 |        103 |        5 | 14:32:15
(3 rows)

-- Temp table verification:
 schemaname  |  tablename   | temporary
-------------+--------------+-----------
 pg_temp_3   | session_cart | t
(1 row)
Explanation: TEMP or TEMPORARY creates session-scoped tables that automatically drop when session ends. Temporary tables stored in special pg_temp_N schemas unique to each session, avoiding name conflicts between sessions. Multiple sessions can have temp tables with same name. Perfect for: intermediate calculations, session-specific data (shopping carts, wizard forms), ETL staging without polluting permanent schema. Data not shared across sessions, automatically cleaned up, no manual DROP needed.

🎯 Example 4: Unlogged Tables for Performance

-- Create unlogged table for high-write cache
CREATE UNLOGGED TABLE api_cache (
    cache_key VARCHAR(255) PRIMARY KEY,
    cache_value TEXT NOT NULL,
    created_at TIMESTAMP DEFAULT CURRENT_TIMESTAMP,
    expires_at TIMESTAMP NOT NULL
);

-- Insert cache entries
INSERT INTO api_cache (cache_key, cache_value, expires_at) 
VALUES 
    ('user:1001:profile', '{"name":"John","email":"john@example.com"}', CURRENT_TIMESTAMP + INTERVAL '1 hour'),
    ('product:5001:details', '{"name":"Laptop","price":1299.99}', CURRENT_TIMESTAMP + INTERVAL '30 minutes'),
    ('stats:daily:2026-01-25', '{"visits":15234,"sales":98}', CURRENT_TIMESTAMP + INTERVAL '24 hours');

-- Query cache with expiration check
SELECT 
    cache_key,
    LEFT(cache_value, 40) || '...' AS value_preview,
    expires_at > CURRENT_TIMESTAMP AS is_valid,
    EXTRACT(EPOCH FROM (expires_at - CURRENT_TIMESTAMP))/60 AS minutes_remaining
FROM api_cache
ORDER BY expires_at;
Result:
        cache_key         |              value_preview               | is_valid | minutes_remaining
--------------------------+------------------------------------------+----------+-------------------
 product:5001:details     | {"name":"Laptop","price":1299.99}...     | t        |             29.98
 user:1001:profile        | {"name":"John","email":"john@example.c...| t        |             59.98
 stats:daily:2026-01-25   | {"visits":15234,"sales":98}...           | t        |           1439.98
(3 rows)
Explanation: UNLOGGED tables sacrifice crash-safety for performance: writes don't go to write-ahead log (WAL), making them 2-3x faster for bulk operations. Perfect for: application caches, session data, temporary analytics, rate limiting counters - data that can be regenerated if lost. CAUTION: unlogged table data truncated on database crash/unclean shutdown. Don't use for critical data. Query shows cache with expiration logic - common pattern for API response caching with TTL (time-to-live).

🎯 Example 5: Complex Table with All Features

-- Comprehensive table demonstrating multiple DDL features
CREATE TABLE products_catalog (
    product_id SERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    sku VARCHAR(50) UNIQUE NOT NULL,
    product_name VARCHAR(200) NOT NULL,
    category VARCHAR(50) NOT NULL,
    price NUMERIC(10,2) NOT NULL CHECK (price > 0),
    cost NUMERIC(10,2) CHECK (cost >= 0 AND cost <= price),
    stock_quantity INTEGER DEFAULT 0 CHECK (stock_quantity >= 0),
    reorder_level INTEGER DEFAULT 10,
    is_active BOOLEAN DEFAULT true,
    is_featured BOOLEAN DEFAULT false,
    weight_kg NUMERIC(8,3) CHECK (weight_kg > 0),
    dimensions_cm VARCHAR(50),
    tags TEXT[],
    metadata JSONB,
    created_at TIMESTAMP DEFAULT CURRENT_TIMESTAMP,
    updated_at TIMESTAMP DEFAULT CURRENT_TIMESTAMP,
    CONSTRAINT price_cost_relationship CHECK (price >= cost OR cost IS NULL),
    CONSTRAINT reorder_check CHECK (reorder_level <= stock_quantity OR stock_quantity = 0)
);

-- Insert comprehensive product
INSERT INTO products_catalog (
    sku, product_name, category, price, cost, stock_quantity, 
    reorder_level, weight_kg, dimensions_cm, tags, metadata
) VALUES (
    'LAP-001',
    'Professional Laptop 15"',
    'Electronics',
    1299.99,
    850.00,
    25,
    5,
    2.1,
    '35x24x2',
    ARRAY['laptop', 'computers', 'business'],
    '{"brand": "TechPro", "warranty_years": 3, "specs": {"ram": "16GB", "storage": "512GB SSD"}}'::jsonb
);

-- Query with all columns
SELECT 
    sku,
    product_name,
    price,
    cost,
    price - cost AS margin,
    stock_quantity,
    CASE WHEN stock_quantity <= reorder_level THEN 'Reorder' ELSE 'OK' END AS stock_status,
    tags,
    metadata->>'brand' AS brand
FROM products_catalog;
Result:
   sku    |       product_name        |  price  |  cost  | margin | stock_quantity | stock_status |           tags            |  brand
----------+---------------------------+---------+--------+--------+----------------+--------------+---------------------------+---------
 LAP-001  | Professional Laptop 15"   | 1299.99 | 850.00 | 449.99 |             25 | OK           | {laptop,computers,busine..| TechPro
(1 row)
Explanation: Comprehensive table showcasing: SERIAL auto-increment ID, UNIQUE constraint on SKU, multiple CHECK constraints (price > 0, cost <= price, stock >= 0), DEFAULT values (stock_quantity, is_active, timestamps), table-level constraints (price_cost_relationship, reorder_check) for multi-column validation, ARRAY column for tags, JSONB for flexible metadata. Complex constraints: price >= cost ensures profitable pricing, reorder_level <= stock_quantity except when out of stock. Demonstrates real-world product catalog with business logic enforced at database level. Tags array and JSONB metadata provide flexibility for varying product attributes.

5.2 Default Values

📖 Full Description

Default Values are column-level specifications that automatically provide values when INSERT statements omit those columns. Defaults make data entry more convenient, ensure consistency, and reduce application logic by centralizing common value assignment at the database level. When a column has a DEFAULT clause, PostgreSQL inserts that value automatically if the column isn't explicitly mentioned in the INSERT.

Default values can be literal constants (0, 'unknown', true), expressions (CURRENT_TIMESTAMP, CURRENT_USER), function calls (uuid_generate_v4(), now()), or even complex expressions involving other functions. PostgreSQL evaluates defaults at INSERT time, so functions like CURRENT_TIMESTAMP produce different values for each row insertion. This dynamic evaluation enables powerful patterns like auto-timestamps, audit trails, and computed defaults.

DEFAULT vs NULL: If a column has no DEFAULT and is omitted in INSERT, PostgreSQL inserts NULL (if column allows it) or raises an error (if NOT NULL). DEFAULT provides an explicit fallback value instead of NULL. You can also explicitly insert DEFAULT using the DEFAULT keyword in INSERT statements. Common use cases: timestamps (created_at, updated_at), status flags (is_active, is_deleted), counters (view_count, retry_attempts), user tracking (created_by).
