Default Values Syntax
-- Default with literal value
CREATE TABLE table_name (
    column_name data_type DEFAULT literal_value
);

-- Default with expression/function
CREATE TABLE table_name (
    column_name data_type DEFAULT expression
);

-- Common default patterns
CREATE TABLE example_defaults (
    -- Literal defaults
    status VARCHAR(20) DEFAULT 'pending',
    priority INTEGER DEFAULT 0,
    is_active BOOLEAN DEFAULT true,
    
    -- Timestamp defaults
    created_at TIMESTAMP DEFAULT CURRENT_TIMESTAMP,
    updated_at TIMESTAMP DEFAULT now(),
    created_date DATE DEFAULT CURRENT_DATE,
    
    -- User/session defaults
    created_by TEXT DEFAULT CURRENT_USER,
    session_id TEXT DEFAULT gen_random_uuid()::TEXT,
    
    -- Computed defaults
    expires_at TIMESTAMP DEFAULT (CURRENT_TIMESTAMP + INTERVAL '30 days'),
    full_name TEXT DEFAULT 'Unknown User'
);

-- Explicitly insert DEFAULT value
INSERT INTO table_name (col1, col2) 
VALUES ('value1', DEFAULT);

-- Adding default to existing column
ALTER TABLE table_name 
ALTER COLUMN column_name SET DEFAULT expression;

-- Removing default from column
ALTER TABLE table_name 
ALTER COLUMN column_name DROP DEFAULT;

Default Value Characteristics

Types of Defaults:

  • Literal constants: Numbers (0, 100), strings ('active'), booleans (true, false)
  • SQL functions: CURRENT_TIMESTAMP, CURRENT_USER, CURRENT_DATE, now(), uuid_generate_v4()
  • Expressions: Calculations (amount * 1.1), concatenations ('prefix_' || value)
  • Type conversions: gen_random_uuid()::TEXT, '2026-01-01'::DATE

Evaluation Timing:

  • INSERT time: Defaults evaluated when row inserted, not when table created
  • Dynamic values: Functions like CURRENT_TIMESTAMP return different values per INSERT
  • Static values: Literals always produce same value
  • Per-row evaluation: Each row in multi-row INSERT can have different default values

Behavior Rules:

  • Omitted columns: Default used when column not in INSERT column list
  • Explicit DEFAULT: Can use DEFAULT keyword in VALUES clause
  • NULL vs DEFAULT: INSERT...VALUES (NULL) inserts NULL, not default
  • NOT NULL + DEFAULT: Column never NULL if always omitted/DEFAULT used

Common Patterns:

  • Audit timestamps: created_at, updated_at with CURRENT_TIMESTAMP
  • Status tracking: status DEFAULT 'pending', is_active DEFAULT true
  • Counters: view_count DEFAULT 0, retry_count DEFAULT 0
  • User attribution: created_by DEFAULT CURRENT_USER
  • UUIDs: id DEFAULT gen_random_uuid() for distributed systems

🎯 Example 1: Basic Default Values

-- Table with various literal defaults
CREATE TABLE user_accounts (
    account_id SERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    username VARCHAR(50) NOT NULL,
    account_type VARCHAR(20) DEFAULT 'standard',
    is_active BOOLEAN DEFAULT true,
    is_verified BOOLEAN DEFAULT false,
    login_count INTEGER DEFAULT 0,
    created_at TIMESTAMP DEFAULT CURRENT_TIMESTAMP
);

-- Insert with some defaults used
INSERT INTO user_accounts (username, account_type) 
VALUES ('john_doe', 'premium');

-- Insert using all defaults (except username)
INSERT INTO user_accounts (username) 
VALUES ('jane_smith');

-- Query results
SELECT 
    account_id,
    username,
    account_type,
    is_active,
    login_count,
    TO_CHAR(created_at, 'YYYY-MM-DD HH24:MI:SS') AS created
FROM user_accounts
ORDER BY account_id;
Result:
 account_id |  username  | account_type | is_active | login_count |       created
------------+------------+--------------+-----------+-------------+---------------------
          1 | john_doe   | premium      | t         |           0 | 2026-01-25 14:45:30
          2 | jane_smith | standard     | t         |           0 | 2026-01-25 14:45:31
(2 rows)
Explanation: First INSERT specifies account_type='premium', overriding 'standard' default. Second INSERT omits account_type, receiving 'standard' default. Both rows get defaults for is_active (true), is_verified (false), login_count (0), and created_at (current timestamp). Notice created_at timestamps differ slightly - DEFAULT evaluated separately per INSERT. login_count DEFAULT 0 perfect for counters that increment later. is_active DEFAULT true means new accounts enabled unless explicitly disabled.

🎯 Example 2: Timestamp Defaults for Audit Tracking

-- Audit tracking with automatic timestamps
CREATE TABLE orders_audit (
    order_id SERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    customer_id INTEGER NOT NULL,
    order_total NUMERIC(10,2) NOT NULL,
    status VARCHAR(20) DEFAULT 'pending',
    created_at TIMESTAMP DEFAULT CURRENT_TIMESTAMP,
    updated_at TIMESTAMP DEFAULT CURRENT_TIMESTAMP,
    created_date DATE DEFAULT CURRENT_DATE,
    created_by TEXT DEFAULT CURRENT_USER
);

-- Insert orders (timestamps auto-populated)
INSERT INTO orders_audit (customer_id, order_total) 
VALUES (101, 299.99), (102, 149.50), (103, 599.00);

-- Wait a moment, then update one order
UPDATE orders_audit 
SET status = 'processing', updated_at = CURRENT_TIMESTAMP 
WHERE order_id = 1;

-- Query with formatted timestamps
SELECT 
    order_id,
    customer_id,
    order_total,
    status,
    TO_CHAR(created_at, 'YYYY-MM-DD HH24:MI:SS') AS created_time,
    TO_CHAR(updated_at, 'YYYY-MM-DD HH24:MI:SS') AS updated_time,
    created_by
FROM orders_audit
ORDER BY order_id;
Result:
 order_id | customer_id | order_total |   status    |    created_time     |    updated_time     | created_by
----------+-------------+-------------+-------------+---------------------+---------------------+------------
        1 |         101 |      299.99 | processing  | 2026-01-25 14:50:15 | 2026-01-25 14:51:20 | postgres
        2 |         102 |      149.50 | pending     | 2026-01-25 14:50:15 | 2026-01-25 14:50:15 | postgres
        3 |         103 |      599.00 | pending     | 2026-01-25 14:50:15 | 2026-01-25 14:50:15 | postgres
(3 rows)
Explanation: Audit tracking pattern: created_at DEFAULT CURRENT_TIMESTAMP records insertion time, updated_at starts same as created_at but manually updated on changes (order_id=1 updated to 'processing'). created_by DEFAULT CURRENT_USER tracks which database user created record. Notice all rows inserted in same statement share created_at timestamp, but can be updated independently. For auto-updating updated_at, use triggers (Chapter 37) or application logic. DEFAULT handles initial values; triggers handle automatic updates.

🎯 Example 3: Computed Default Values

-- Defaults using expressions and calculations
CREATE TABLE subscriptions (
    subscription_id SERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    user_id INTEGER NOT NULL,
    plan_type VARCHAR(20) NOT NULL,
    start_date DATE DEFAULT CURRENT_DATE,
    end_date DATE DEFAULT (CURRENT_DATE + INTERVAL '30 days')::DATE,
    trial_days INTEGER DEFAULT 14,
    trial_end_date DATE DEFAULT (CURRENT_DATE + INTERVAL '14 days')::DATE,
    auto_renew BOOLEAN DEFAULT true,
    created_at TIMESTAMP DEFAULT CURRENT_TIMESTAMP
);

-- Insert subscriptions with computed defaults
INSERT INTO subscriptions (user_id, plan_type) 
VALUES (1001, 'basic'), (1002, 'premium'), (1003, 'enterprise');

-- Query showing computed defaults
SELECT 
    subscription_id,
    user_id,
    plan_type,
    start_date,
    end_date,
    end_date - start_date AS subscription_days,
    trial_end_date,
    trial_end_date - start_date AS trial_length,
    auto_renew
FROM subscriptions
ORDER BY subscription_id;
Result:
 subscription_id | user_id | plan_type  | start_date |  end_date  | subscription_days | trial_end_date | trial_length | auto_renew
-----------------+---------+------------+------------+------------+-------------------+----------------+--------------+------------
               1 |    1001 | basic      | 2026-01-25 | 2026-02-24 |                30 | 2026-02-08     |           14 | t
               2 |    1002 | premium    | 2026-01-25 | 2026-02-24 |                30 | 2026-02-08     |           14 | t
               3 |    1003 | enterprise | 2026-01-25 | 2026-02-24 |                30 | 2026-02-08     |           14 | t
(3 rows)
Explanation: Computed defaults using expressions: end_date DEFAULT (CURRENT_DATE + INTERVAL '30 days')::DATE calculates 30-day subscription period, trial_end_date adds 14 days for trial period. Expressions evaluated at INSERT time, so all rows get consistent dates based on insertion date. Type casting ::DATE converts TIMESTAMP result to DATE. Perfect for: expiration dates, calculated periods, derived values. Alternative: use GENERATED ALWAYS AS columns (5.4) for values that recalculate on updates.

🎯 Example 4: Explicit DEFAULT Keyword Usage

-- Table with various defaults
CREATE TABLE products_inventory (
    product_id SERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    sku VARCHAR(50) NOT NULL,
    quantity INTEGER DEFAULT 0,
    reorder_level INTEGER DEFAULT 10,
    status VARCHAR(20) DEFAULT 'active',
    last_stocked TIMESTAMP DEFAULT CURRENT_TIMESTAMP
);

-- Insert explicitly using DEFAULT keyword
INSERT INTO products_inventory (sku, quantity, reorder_level, status, last_stocked) 
VALUES 
    ('PROD-001', 100, 20, 'active', CURRENT_TIMESTAMP),
    ('PROD-002', DEFAULT, DEFAULT, DEFAULT, DEFAULT),
    ('PROD-003', 50, DEFAULT, 'backordered', DEFAULT),
    ('PROD-004', 0, 5, DEFAULT, CURRENT_TIMESTAMP - INTERVAL '7 days');

-- Query results
SELECT 
    product_id,
    sku,
    quantity,
    reorder_level,
    status,
    TO_CHAR(last_stocked, 'YYYY-MM-DD') AS stocked_date
FROM products_inventory
ORDER BY product_id;
Result:
 product_id |    sku    | quantity | reorder_level |   status    | stocked_date
------------+-----------+----------+---------------+-------------+--------------
          1 | PROD-001  |      100 |            20 | active      | 2026-01-25
          2 | PROD-002  |        0 |            10 | active      | 2026-01-25
          3 | PROD-003  |       50 |            10 | backordered | 2026-01-25
          4 | PROD-004  |        0 |             5 | active      | 2026-01-18
(4 rows)
Explanation: DEFAULT keyword explicitly requests default value in VALUES clause. PROD-002 uses DEFAULT for all columns (except sku) - receives quantity=0, reorder_level=10, status='active', last_stocked=now(). PROD-003 mixes explicit values (quantity=50, status='backordered') with DEFAULT (reorder_level=10, last_stocked=now()). PROD-004 shows you can override defaults even for timestamp columns. DEFAULT keyword useful when: inserting subset of columns but want defaults for specific ones, making code self-documenting, maintaining column order in INSERT statement.

🎯 Example 5: Modifying Defaults on Existing Tables

-- Create table with initial defaults
CREATE TABLE customer_settings (
    setting_id SERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    customer_id INTEGER NOT NULL,
    theme VARCHAR(20) DEFAULT 'light',
    language VARCHAR(10) DEFAULT 'en',
    notifications_enabled BOOLEAN DEFAULT true,
    email_frequency VARCHAR(20) DEFAULT 'daily'
);

-- Insert with initial defaults
INSERT INTO customer_settings (customer_id) 
VALUES (1001);

-- Change default values using ALTER TABLE
ALTER TABLE customer_settings 
ALTER COLUMN theme SET DEFAULT 'dark';

ALTER TABLE customer_settings 
ALTER COLUMN email_frequency SET DEFAULT 'weekly';

ALTER TABLE customer_settings 
ALTER COLUMN notifications_enabled SET DEFAULT false;

-- Insert after changing defaults
INSERT INTO customer_settings (customer_id) 
VALUES (1002);

-- Remove a default (column becomes NULL if omitted)
ALTER TABLE customer_settings 
ALTER COLUMN language DROP DEFAULT;

-- Insert after removing default
INSERT INTO customer_settings (customer_id, theme) 
VALUES (1003, 'light');

-- Query all rows showing effect of default changes
SELECT 
    setting_id,
    customer_id,
    theme,
    language,
    notifications_enabled,
    email_frequency
FROM customer_settings
ORDER BY setting_id;
Result:
 setting_id | customer_id | theme | language | notifications_enabled | email_frequency
------------+-------------+-------+----------+-----------------------+-----------------
          1 |        1001 | light | en       | t                     | daily
          2 |        1002 | dark  | en       | f                     | weekly
          3 |        1003 | light | NULL     | f                     | weekly
(3 rows)
Explanation: ALTER COLUMN SET DEFAULT changes default for future INSERTs without affecting existing rows: setting_id=1 has old defaults (light, daily, true), setting_id=2 has new defaults (dark, weekly, false). ALTER COLUMN DROP DEFAULT removes default - language becomes NULL for setting_id=3 since omitted and no default. Important: changing defaults affects only future inserts, not existing data. To update existing rows, use UPDATE. Common pattern: evolve defaults as business requirements change (light→dark theme preference shift, daily→weekly emails to reduce volume). Use ALTER for schema evolution without data migration.

5.3 Identity Columns

📖 Full Description

Identity Columns are SQL-standard auto-incrementing columns that automatically generate unique sequential values for each new row. PostgreSQL identity columns, introduced in version 10, provide a more standard alternative to the traditional SERIAL pseudo-type. Identity columns use sequences under the hood but offer better control through GENERATED ALWAYS or GENERATED BY DEFAULT semantics and conform to SQL:2003 standard.

Two identity generation strategies exist: GENERATED ALWAYS prohibits manual value insertion (database always generates), ensuring sequence integrity; GENERATED BY DEFAULT allows manual override when explicitly provided but auto-generates when omitted. Identity columns support sequence options like INCREMENT, START WITH, MINVALUE, MAXVALUE, and CYCLE to control value generation behavior.

Identity columns vs SERIAL: SERIAL is PostgreSQL-specific shorthand creating INTEGER + DEFAULT nextval() + owned sequence. Identity columns are SQL-standard, provide stronger guarantees (GENERATED ALWAYS), support OVERRIDING clauses in INSERT, and offer ALTER COLUMN operations to change generation strategy. Best practice: use identity columns for new applications (standard-compliant), SERIAL for legacy compatibility. Identity columns better express intent and integrate with SQL standard features.

Identity Columns Syntax
-- GENERATED ALWAYS AS IDENTITY (strict auto-generation)
CREATE TABLE table_name (
    id INTEGER GENERATED ALWAYS AS IDENTITY PRIMARY KEY,
    other_columns...
);

-- GENERATED BY DEFAULT AS IDENTITY (allows manual override)
CREATE TABLE table_name (
    id INTEGER GENERATED BY DEFAULT AS IDENTITY PRIMARY KEY,
    other_columns...
);

-- Identity with sequence options
CREATE TABLE table_name (
    id INTEGER GENERATED ALWAYS AS IDENTITY (
        START WITH 1000
        INCREMENT BY 10
        MINVALUE 1000
        MAXVALUE 999999
        CACHE 20
    ) PRIMARY KEY,
    data TEXT
);

-- Inserting into identity column tables
-- GENERATED BY DEFAULT: can omit or use DEFAULT
INSERT INTO table_name (other_column) VALUES ('data');
INSERT INTO table_name (id, other_column) VALUES (DEFAULT, 'data');

-- GENERATED ALWAYS: must use OVERRIDING SYSTEM VALUE to insert manually
INSERT INTO table_name (id, other_column) 
OVERRIDING SYSTEM VALUE 
VALUES (12345, 'data');

-- Altering identity column
-- Add identity to existing column
ALTER TABLE table_name 
ALTER COLUMN id ADD GENERATED BY DEFAULT AS IDENTITY;

-- Change generation strategy
ALTER TABLE table_name 
ALTER COLUMN id SET GENERATED ALWAYS;

-- Change sequence options
ALTER TABLE table_name 
ALTER COLUMN id SET INCREMENT BY 5;

-- Remove identity
ALTER TABLE table_name 
ALTER COLUMN id DROP IDENTITY;

Identity Column Characteristics

Generation Strategies:

  • GENERATED ALWAYS: Database always generates value, manual insert rejected unless OVERRIDING SYSTEM VALUE
  • GENERATED BY DEFAULT: Database generates if omitted, accepts explicit values
  • Use ALWAYS: When strict sequence control needed, prevents gaps from manual inserts
  • Use BY DEFAULT: When manual IDs needed occasionally (data migration, testing, replication)

Sequence Options:

  • START WITH: Initial value (default 1)
  • INCREMENT BY: Step size (default 1, can be negative for countdown)
  • MINVALUE/MAXVALUE: Range limits (defaults based on column type)
  • CYCLE: Restart from MINVALUE when MAXVALUE reached (default NO CYCLE)
  • CACHE: Pre-allocate sequence numbers for performance (default 1)

Identity vs SERIAL Comparison:

  • Standard compliance: Identity is SQL:2003 standard, SERIAL is PostgreSQL-specific
  • Syntax: Identity uses GENERATED AS IDENTITY, SERIAL is type alias for INTEGER + DEFAULT
  • Control: Identity offers ALWAYS/BY DEFAULT choice, SERIAL only BY DEFAULT behavior
  • Modification: Identity has ALTER COLUMN operations, SERIAL requires manual sequence management
  • Introspection: Identity columns identifiable in information_schema.columns.is_identity

Operations:

  • OVERRIDING SYSTEM VALUE: Force insert into GENERATED ALWAYS column
  • OVERRIDING USER VALUE: Force auto-generation even when value provided
  • setval(): Manually adjust sequence current value
  • currval(): Get last generated value in current session

🎯 Example 1: GENERATED ALWAYS vs GENERATED BY DEFAULT

-- Table with GENERATED ALWAYS (strict)
CREATE TABLE orders_always (
    order_id INTEGER GENERATED ALWAYS AS IDENTITY PRIMARY KEY,
    customer_id INTEGER NOT NULL,
    order_date DATE DEFAULT CURRENT_DATE
);

-- Table with GENERATED BY DEFAULT (flexible)
CREATE TABLE orders_default (
    order_id INTEGER GENERATED BY DEFAULT AS IDENTITY PRIMARY KEY,
    customer_id INTEGER NOT NULL,
    order_date DATE DEFAULT CURRENT_DATE
);

-- Insert into BY DEFAULT (works fine, auto-generates ID)
INSERT INTO orders_default (customer_id) VALUES (101), (102);

-- Insert into BY DEFAULT with explicit ID (allowed)
INSERT INTO orders_default (order_id, customer_id) VALUES (100, 103);

-- Insert into ALWAYS without ID (works fine, auto-generates)
INSERT INTO orders_always (customer_id) VALUES (201), (202);

-- Try inserting explicit ID into ALWAYS (will fail)
-- INSERT INTO orders_always (order_id, customer_id) VALUES (999, 203);
-- ERROR: cannot insert into column "order_id"

-- Insert into ALWAYS with OVERRIDING SYSTEM VALUE (allowed)
INSERT INTO orders_always (order_id, customer_id) 
OVERRIDING SYSTEM VALUE 
VALUES (999, 203);

-- Query results
SELECT 'BY DEFAULT' AS table_type, order_id, customer_id FROM orders_default
UNION ALL
SELECT 'ALWAYS' AS table_type, order_id, customer_id FROM orders_always
ORDER BY table_type, order_id;
Result:
 table_type | order_id | customer_id
------------+----------+-------------
 ALWAYS     |        1 |         201
 ALWAYS     |        2 |         202
 ALWAYS     |      999 |         203
 BY DEFAULT |        1 |         101
 BY DEFAULT |        2 |         102
 BY DEFAULT |      100 |         103
(6 rows)
Explanation: GENERATED ALWAYS enforces strict sequence control: cannot insert explicit order_id without OVERRIDING SYSTEM VALUE clause. GENERATED BY DEFAULT flexible: accepts explicit values (order_id=100 inserted directly) or auto-generates when omitted (IDs 1,2). OVERRIDING SYSTEM VALUE allows manual insertion into ALWAYS columns for special cases (data migration, testing). Choose ALWAYS for strict autonumbering preventing manual interference, BY DEFAULT when occasional manual IDs needed. Note: manual insert (100) doesn't affect sequence - next auto-generated value still follows sequence.

🎯 Example 2: Identity Columns with Sequence Options

-- Identity column with custom sequence settings
CREATE TABLE invoices (
    invoice_id INTEGER GENERATED ALWAYS AS IDENTITY (
        START WITH 10000
        INCREMENT BY 1
        MINVALUE 10000
        MAXVALUE 99999
        NO CYCLE
        CACHE 10
    ) PRIMARY KEY,
    customer_id INTEGER NOT NULL,
    invoice_date DATE DEFAULT CURRENT_DATE,
    total_amount NUMERIC(10,2)
);

-- Insert invoices (IDs start at 10000)
INSERT INTO invoices (customer_id, total_amount) 
VALUES (101, 299.99), (102, 149.50), (103, 599.00);

-- Query invoices
SELECT invoice_id, customer_id, total_amount 
FROM invoices 
ORDER BY invoice_id;

-- Check sequence details
SELECT 
    sequence_name,
    start_value,
    increment_by,
    min_value,
    max_value,
    cache_size,
    cycle
FROM information_schema.sequences
WHERE sequence_name LIKE 'invoices%';
Result:
-- Invoices:
 invoice_id | customer_id | total_amount
------------+-------------+--------------
      10000 |         101 |       299.99
      10001 |         102 |       149.50
      10002 |         103 |       599.00
(3 rows)

-- Sequence details:
     sequence_name      | start_value | increment_by | min_value | max_value | cache_size | cycle
------------------------+-------------+--------------+-----------+-----------+------------+-------
 invoices_invoice_id_seq|       10000 |            1 |     10000 |     99999 |         10 | NO
(1 row)
Explanation: Custom sequence options: START WITH 10000 begins invoice numbers at 10000 (professional-looking), MINVALUE/MAXVALUE define valid range (10000-99999 for 5-digit invoices), CACHE 10 pre-allocates 10 IDs improving performance under high insert load, NO CYCLE prevents sequence wraparound when max reached. PostgreSQL creates named sequence (invoices_invoice_id_seq) managing identity column. Sequence options useful for: business-friendly ID formats (INV-10000), preventing ID collisions in distributed systems (different START WITH per shard), performance tuning (CACHE).

🎯 Example 3: Migrating SERIAL to Identity Columns

-- Old style: SERIAL (legacy approach)
CREATE TABLE products_serial (
    product_id SERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    product_name VARCHAR(100) NOT NULL,
    price NUMERIC(10,2)
);

-- New style: Identity column (recommended)
CREATE TABLE products_identity (
    product_id INTEGER GENERATED BY DEFAULT AS IDENTITY PRIMARY KEY,
    product_name VARCHAR(100) NOT NULL,
    price NUMERIC(10,2)
);

-- Insert data into both
INSERT INTO products_serial (product_name, price) 
VALUES ('Laptop', 1299.99), ('Mouse', 29.99);

INSERT INTO products_identity (product_name, price) 
VALUES ('Keyboard', 79.99), ('Monitor', 399.99);

-- Check system catalogs for differences
SELECT 
    'SERIAL' AS type,
    a.attname AS column_name,
    pg_get_expr(d.adbin, d.adrelid) AS default_expression,
    NULL::text AS identity_generation
FROM pg_attribute a
JOIN pg_class c ON a.attrelid = c.oid
LEFT JOIN pg_attrdef d ON a.attrelid = d.adrelid AND a.attnum = d.adnum
WHERE c.relname = 'products_serial' AND a.attname = 'product_id'

UNION ALL

SELECT 
    'IDENTITY' AS type,
    a.attname AS column_name,
    NULL::text AS default_expression,
    CASE a.attidentity
        WHEN 'a' THEN 'ALWAYS'
        WHEN 'd' THEN 'BY DEFAULT'
        ELSE NULL
    END AS identity_generation
FROM pg_attribute a
JOIN pg_class c ON a.attrelid = c.oid
WHERE c.relname = 'products_identity' AND a.attname = 'product_id';
Result:
   type   | column_name |           default_expression            | identity_generation
----------+-------------+-----------------------------------------+---------------------
 SERIAL   | product_id  | nextval('products_serial_product_id_...'| NULL
 IDENTITY | product_id  | NULL                                    | BY DEFAULT
(2 rows)
Explanation: SERIAL creates column with DEFAULT nextval(sequence), visible in pg_attrdef. Identity columns use attidentity system column ('a'=ALWAYS, 'd'=BY DEFAULT) with no DEFAULT expression. Functionally similar but identity columns SQL-standard and offer better ALTER operations. Migration strategy: new tables use identity, existing SERIAL tables work fine (don't force migration). For new projects: prefer identity columns. For existing: SERIAL acceptable, or ALTER TABLE...ALTER COLUMN ADD GENERATED BY DEFAULT AS IDENTITY to convert (requires dropping DEFAULT first).

🎯 Example 4: Working with Identity Column Values

-- Create table with identity column
CREATE TABLE shipments (
    shipment_id BIGINT GENERATED BY DEFAULT AS IDENTITY PRIMARY KEY,
    order_id INTEGER NOT NULL,
    tracking_number VARCHAR(50),
    shipped_date DATE DEFAULT CURRENT_DATE
);

-- Insert shipments and retrieve generated IDs
INSERT INTO shipments (order_id, tracking_number) 
VALUES (1001, 'TRACK-001'), (1002, 'TRACK-002'), (1003, 'TRACK-003')
RETURNING shipment_id, tracking_number;

-- Get current sequence value (last generated)
SELECT currval(pg_get_serial_sequence('shipments', 'shipment_id')) AS current_id;

-- Manually set sequence value (useful after bulk imports)
SELECT setval(pg_get_serial_sequence('shipments', 'shipment_id'), 1000);

-- Insert after setval (next ID will be 1001)
INSERT INTO shipments (order_id, tracking_number) 
VALUES (1004, 'TRACK-004')
RETURNING shipment_id;

-- Query all shipments
SELECT shipment_id, order_id, tracking_number 
FROM shipments 
ORDER BY shipment_id;
Result:
-- RETURNING from first insert:
 shipment_id | tracking_number
-------------+-----------------
           1 | TRACK-001
           2 | TRACK-002
           3 | TRACK-003
(3 rows)

-- Current sequence value:
 current_id
------------
          3
(1 row)

-- After setval, insert RETURNING:
 shipment_id
-------------
        1001
(1 row)

-- All shipments:
 shipment_id | order_id | tracking_number
-------------+----------+-----------------
           1 |     1001 | TRACK-001
           2 |     1002 | TRACK-002
           3 |     1003 | TRACK-003
        1001 |     1004 | TRACK-004
(4 rows)
Explanation: RETURNING clause retrieves generated identity values immediately after INSERT (useful for getting auto-generated IDs). currval() returns last value generated in current session (safe for concurrent use). setval() manually adjusts sequence - here jumping from 3 to 1000, next insert gets 1001. Common setval() uses: after bulk import with explicit IDs (prevent conflicts), resetting development databases, syncing sequences after restore. pg_get_serial_sequence() finds sequence name for identity/serial columns. Note gap in IDs (3→1001) - identity columns allow gaps, not guaranteed consecutive.

🎯 Example 5: Advanced Identity Column Management

-- Create table without identity initially
CREATE TABLE customer_feedback (
    feedback_id INTEGER PRIMARY KEY,
    customer_id INTEGER NOT NULL,
    rating INTEGER CHECK (rating BETWEEN 1 AND 5),
    comments TEXT,
    created_at TIMESTAMP DEFAULT CURRENT_TIMESTAMP
);

-- Add identity to existing column
ALTER TABLE customer_feedback 
ALTER COLUMN feedback_id ADD GENERATED BY DEFAULT AS IDENTITY (START WITH 1);

-- Insert with auto-generation
INSERT INTO customer_feedback (customer_id, rating, comments) 
VALUES (101, 5, 'Excellent service!'), (102, 4, 'Very good');

-- Change to GENERATED ALWAYS for stricter control
ALTER TABLE customer_feedback 
ALTER COLUMN feedback_id SET GENERATED ALWAYS;

-- Try insert with explicit ID (will fail now)
-- INSERT INTO customer_feedback (feedback_id, customer_id, rating) 
-- VALUES (999, 103, 3);
-- ERROR: cannot insert into column "feedback_id"

-- Insert with OVERRIDING (required for ALWAYS)
INSERT INTO customer_feedback (feedback_id, customer_id, rating, comments) 
OVERRIDING SYSTEM VALUE 
VALUES (999, 103, 3, 'Good product');

-- Change sequence options
ALTER TABLE customer_feedback 
ALTER COLUMN feedback_id SET INCREMENT BY 10;

-- Insert with new increment
INSERT INTO customer_feedback (customer_id, rating) 
VALUES (104, 5);

-- Query results
SELECT feedback_id, customer_id, rating, LEFT(comments, 20) AS comment_preview 
FROM customer_feedback 
ORDER BY feedback_id;

-- Drop identity (convert back to regular column)
ALTER TABLE customer_feedback 
ALTER COLUMN feedback_id DROP IDENTITY IF EXISTS;

-- Verify identity removed
SELECT 
    column_name,
    is_identity,
    identity_generation
FROM information_schema.columns
WHERE table_name = 'customer_feedback' AND column_name = 'feedback_id';
Result:
-- Feedback records:
 feedback_id | customer_id | rating | comment_preview
-------------+-------------+--------+-------------------
           1 |         101 |      5 | Excellent service!
           2 |         102 |      4 | Very good
          12 |         104 |      5 | NULL
         999 |         103 |      3 | Good product
(4 rows)

-- After DROP IDENTITY:
 column_name | is_identity | identity_generation
-------------+-------------+---------------------
 feedback_id | NO          | NULL
(1 row)
Explanation: Identity column lifecycle: ADD GENERATED...AS IDENTITY converts regular column to identity, SET GENERATED ALWAYS/BY DEFAULT changes generation strategy, SET INCREMENT BY/START WITH adjusts sequence parameters, DROP IDENTITY removes auto-generation. Notice feedback_id=12 (not 3) after SET INCREMENT BY 10 - sequence jumped by 10. feedback_id=999 inserted with OVERRIDING SYSTEM VALUE while column was GENERATED ALWAYS. Flexible management allows evolving requirements: start with BY DEFAULT (migration flexibility), switch to ALWAYS (strict control), adjust increment (business needs), remove identity (manual control). ALTER operations don't affect existing values, only future generation.

5.4 Generated Columns

📖 Full Description

Generated Columns are columns whose values are automatically computed from other columns using an expression, eliminating data redundancy and ensuring consistency. PostgreSQL generated columns (introduced in version 12) are defined with GENERATED ALWAYS AS (expression) STORED, where the expression determines the column value based on other columns in the same row. Values are automatically maintained - you cannot insert or update generated columns directly.

Generated columns always use STORED strategy in PostgreSQL, meaning computed values are physically stored on disk and updated whenever dependent columns change. This differs from computed/calculated columns in other databases that may compute values on-the-fly (VIRTUAL). STORED generated columns can be indexed, used in constraints, and queried efficiently without runtime computation overhead, at the cost of additional storage space.

Common use cases: full_name from first_name + last_name, price_with_tax from price * tax_rate, search_text from concatenated searchable fields, computed metrics (total_amount from quantity * unit_price), denormalized data for performance, derived status fields. Generated columns eliminate application-level calculation duplication, prevent data inconsistency bugs, and ensure database-level integrity. Unlike triggers or application logic, generated columns are declarative and automatically maintained by PostgreSQL.

Generated Columns Syntax
-- Basic generated column
CREATE TABLE table_name (
    column1 data_type,
    column2 data_type,
    generated_column data_type GENERATED ALWAYS AS (expression) STORED
);

-- Generated column with expression
CREATE TABLE products (
    price NUMERIC(10,2),
    tax_rate NUMERIC(4,3),
    price_with_tax NUMERIC(10,2) GENERATED ALWAYS AS (price * (1 + tax_rate)) STORED
);

-- Text concatenation
CREATE TABLE customers (
    first_name VARCHAR(50),
    last_name VARCHAR(50),
    full_name VARCHAR(101) GENERATED ALWAYS AS (first_name || ' ' || last_name) STORED
);

-- Complex expression with functions
CREATE TABLE orders (
    order_date DATE,
    total_amount NUMERIC(10,2),
    year INTEGER GENERATED ALWAYS AS (EXTRACT(YEAR FROM order_date)) STORED,
    quarter INTEGER GENERATED ALWAYS AS (EXTRACT(QUARTER FROM order_date)) STORED
);

-- Conditional generated column
CREATE TABLE invoices (
    total_amount NUMERIC(10,2),
    paid_amount NUMERIC(10,2),
    status VARCHAR(20) GENERATED ALWAYS AS (
        CASE 
            WHEN paid_amount >= total_amount THEN 'paid'
            WHEN paid_amount > 0 THEN 'partial'
            ELSE 'unpaid'
        END
    ) STORED
);

-- Adding generated column to existing table
ALTER TABLE table_name 
ADD COLUMN generated_col data_type GENERATED ALWAYS AS (expression) STORED;

-- Dropping generated column
ALTER TABLE table_name 
DROP COLUMN generated_col;

Generated Column Characteristics

Key Features:

  • Automatic calculation: Values computed from expression, no manual INSERT/UPDATE allowed
  • STORED only: PostgreSQL requires STORED (physical storage), no VIRTUAL support currently
  • Always synchronized: Values update automatically when dependent columns change
  • Can be indexed: STORED columns support indexes for query optimization
  • Constraints allowed: Can have UNIQUE, CHECK, NOT NULL constraints

Expression Rules:

  • Deterministic: Must use IMMUTABLE functions only (no CURRENT_TIMESTAMP, random(), etc.)
  • Same-row only: Can reference columns in same row, not other rows or tables
  • No subqueries: Cannot use SELECT statements in expression
  • No aggregates: Cannot use SUM, COUNT, AVG, etc. directly
  • Type compatibility: Expression result must match column data type

Advantages:

  • Consistency: Database enforces calculation logic, prevents application bugs
  • Simplicity: No triggers or application code needed for derived values
  • Query performance: Pre-computed values, no runtime calculation overhead
  • Indexability: Can create indexes on generated columns for fast searches
  • Declarative: Schema documents derivation logic explicitly

Limitations:

  • Storage overhead: STORED columns consume disk space
  • Write performance: Values recomputed on every UPDATE of dependent columns
  • No VIRTUAL: PostgreSQL doesn't support on-the-fly computation (compute at query time)
  • Expression restrictions: Only IMMUTABLE functions, no cross-row references

🎯 Example 1: Basic Generated Columns - Text Concatenation

-- Table with generated full name
CREATE TABLE employees_names (
    employee_id SERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    first_name VARCHAR(50) NOT NULL,
    last_name VARCHAR(50) NOT NULL,
    full_name VARCHAR(101) GENERATED ALWAYS AS (first_name || ' ' || last_name) STORED,
    email_prefix VARCHAR(100) GENERATED ALWAYS AS (LOWER(first_name || '.' || last_name)) STORED
);

-- Insert data (generated columns auto-calculated)
INSERT INTO employees_names (first_name, last_name) 
VALUES 
    ('John', 'Doe'),
    ('Jane', 'Smith'),
    ('Robert', 'Johnson');

-- Try to insert into generated column (will fail)
-- INSERT INTO employees_names (first_name, last_name, full_name) 
-- VALUES ('Alice', 'Williams', 'Alice Williams');
-- ERROR: cannot insert into column "full_name"

-- Update source columns (generated columns auto-update)
UPDATE employees_names 
SET first_name = 'Jonathan' 
WHERE employee_id = 1;

-- Query results
SELECT 
    employee_id,
    first_name,
    last_name,
    full_name,
    email_prefix
FROM employees_names
ORDER BY employee_id;
Result:
 employee_id | first_name | last_name |    full_name     |   email_prefix
-------------+------------+-----------+------------------+-------------------
           1 | Jonathan   | Doe       | Jonathan Doe     | jonathan.doe
           2 | Jane       | Smith     | Jane Smith       | jane.smith
           3 | Robert     | Johnson   | Robert Johnson   | robert.johnson
(3 rows)
Explanation: Generated columns automatically computed: full_name concatenates first_name + space + last_name, email_prefix creates lowercase version with dot separator. INSERT only specifies source columns (first_name, last_name) - generated columns calculated automatically. UPDATE first_name='Jonathan' for employee_id=1 automatically updates full_name='Jonathan Doe' and email_prefix='jonathan.doe'. Cannot INSERT/UPDATE generated columns directly - database maintains them. Perfect for: display names, search fields, email generation, computed identifiers. Eliminates inconsistency where application updates first_name but forgets to update full_name.

🎯 Example 2: Numeric Calculations

-- Product pricing with generated columns
CREATE TABLE products_pricing (
    product_id SERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    product_name VARCHAR(100) NOT NULL,
    base_price NUMERIC(10,2) NOT NULL,
    tax_rate NUMERIC(5,4) DEFAULT 0.0825,
    discount_percentage NUMERIC(5,2) DEFAULT 0,
    
    -- Generated columns for pricing
    discount_amount NUMERIC(10,2) GENERATED ALWAYS AS (
        base_price * (discount_percentage / 100)
    ) STORED,
    
    price_after_discount NUMERIC(10,2) GENERATED ALWAYS AS (
        base_price - (base_price * (discount_percentage / 100))
    ) STORED,
    
    tax_amount NUMERIC(10,2) GENERATED ALWAYS AS (
        (base_price - (base_price * (discount_percentage / 100))) * tax_rate
    ) STORED,
    
    final_price NUMERIC(10,2) GENERATED ALWAYS AS (
        (base_price - (base_price * (discount_percentage / 100))) * (1 + tax_rate)
    ) STORED
);

-- Insert products
INSERT INTO products_pricing (product_name, base_price, discount_percentage) 
VALUES 
    ('Laptop', 1299.99, 10),
    ('Mouse', 29.99, 0),
    ('Keyboard', 79.99, 15);

-- Update discount
UPDATE products_pricing 
SET discount_percentage = 20 
WHERE product_name = 'Laptop';

-- Query with all calculated fields
SELECT 
    product_name,
    base_price,
    discount_percentage || '%' AS discount,
    discount_amount,
    price_after_discount,
    tax_amount,
    final_price
FROM products_pricing
ORDER BY product_id;
Result:
 product_name | base_price | discount | discount_amount | price_after_discount | tax_amount | final_price
--------------+------------+----------+-----------------+----------------------+------------+-------------
 Laptop       |    1299.99 | 20%      |          260.00 |              1039.99 |      85.80 |     1125.79
 Mouse        |      29.99 | 0%       |            0.00 |                29.99 |       2.47 |       32.46
 Keyboard     |      79.99 | 15%      |           12.00 |                67.99 |       5.61 |       73.60
(3 rows)
Explanation: Complex pricing calculation chain: discount_amount computed from base_price and discount_percentage, price_after_discount subtracts discount, tax_amount calculated on discounted price, final_price adds tax. All computed automatically and consistently. UPDATE discount_percentage=20 for Laptop automatically recalculates all dependent fields (discount_amount=260.00, final_price=1125.79). Generated columns ensure pricing consistency - impossible to have mismatched discount_amount and discount_percentage. Alternative approach (application calculates prices) risks inconsistency if logic changes or calculations differ across codebase. Database-level generation centralizes business logic.

🎯 Example 3: Date/Time Extraction and Partitioning

-- Orders with date-based generated columns for analytics
CREATE TABLE orders_analytics (
    order_id SERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    customer_id INTEGER NOT NULL,
    order_date TIMESTAMP NOT NULL DEFAULT CURRENT_TIMESTAMP,
    total_amount NUMERIC(10,2) NOT NULL,
    
    -- Generated date components for analytics/partitioning
    order_year INTEGER GENERATED ALWAYS AS (EXTRACT(YEAR FROM order_date)::INTEGER) STORED,
    order_quarter INTEGER GENERATED ALWAYS AS (EXTRACT(QUARTER FROM order_date)::INTEGER) STORED,
    order_month INTEGER GENERATED ALWAYS AS (EXTRACT(MONTH FROM order_date)::INTEGER) STORED,
    order_day_of_week INTEGER GENERATED ALWAYS AS (EXTRACT(DOW FROM order_date)::INTEGER) STORED,
    order_date_only DATE GENERATED ALWAYS AS (order_date::DATE) STORED,
    
    -- Quarter name for reporting
    quarter_name VARCHAR(10) GENERATED ALWAYS AS (
        'Q' || EXTRACT(QUARTER FROM order_date)::TEXT || ' ' || EXTRACT(YEAR FROM order_date)::TEXT
    ) STORED
);

-- Insert orders with specific dates
INSERT INTO orders_analytics (customer_id, order_date, total_amount) 
VALUES 
    (101, '2026-01-15 10:30:00', 299.99),
    (102, '2026-03-20 14:45:00', 149.50),
    (103, '2026-04-10 09:15:00', 599.00),
    (104, '2026-07-25 16:20:00', 899.99);

-- Query with extracted date components
SELECT 
    order_id,
    TO_CHAR(order_date, 'YYYY-MM-DD') AS order_date,
    total_amount,
    order_year,
    order_quarter,
    order_month,
    CASE order_day_of_week 
        WHEN 0 THEN 'Sunday'
        WHEN 1 THEN 'Monday'
        WHEN 2 THEN 'Tuesday'
        WHEN 3 THEN 'Wednesday'
        WHEN 4 THEN 'Thursday'
        WHEN 5 THEN 'Friday'
        WHEN 6 THEN 'Saturday'
    END AS day_name,
    quarter_name
FROM orders_analytics
ORDER BY order_id;

-- Create index on generated column for fast queries
CREATE INDEX idx_orders_year_month ON orders_analytics(order_year, order_month);

-- Fast query using generated column index
SELECT order_year, order_month, COUNT(*), SUM(total_amount) AS monthly_total
FROM orders_analytics
WHERE order_year = 2026 AND order_month >= 4
GROUP BY order_year, order_month;
Result:
-- All orders:
 order_id | order_date | total_amount | order_year | order_quarter | order_month | day_name  | quarter_name
----------+------------+--------------+------------+---------------+-------------+-----------+--------------
        1 | 2026-01-15 |       299.99 |       2026 |             1 |           1 | Thursday  | Q1 2026
        2 | 2026-03-20 |       149.50 |       2026 |             1 |           3 | Friday    | Q1 2026
        3 | 2026-04-10 |       599.00 |       2026 |             2 |           4 | Friday    | Q2 2026
        4 | 2026-07-25 |       899.99 |       2026 |             3 |           7 | Saturday  | Q3 2026
(4 rows)

-- Aggregation query result:
 order_year | order_month | count | monthly_total
------------+-------------+-------+---------------
       2026 |           4 |     1 |        599.00
       2026 |           7 |     1 |        899.99
(2 rows)
Explanation: Date extraction via generated columns enables efficient analytics: order_year/quarter/month extracted once at INSERT, stored for fast queries. Generated columns can be indexed (idx_orders_year_month) making time-based queries fast without runtime EXTRACT() calls. quarter_name computed for reporting ('Q1 2026', 'Q2 2026'). Perfect for: data warehousing, time-series partitioning, dashboard queries, reporting. Alternative (extract in queries): SELECT EXTRACT(YEAR FROM order_date) is slower, not indexable. Generated columns move computation from query time to write time - faster for read-heavy workloads.

🎯 Example 4: Conditional Logic and Status Fields

-- Inventory with generated status fields
CREATE TABLE inventory_status (
    product_id SERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    product_name VARCHAR(100) NOT NULL,
    quantity_in_stock INTEGER NOT NULL DEFAULT 0,
    reorder_level INTEGER NOT NULL DEFAULT 10,
    quantity_reserved INTEGER NOT NULL DEFAULT 0,
    
    -- Generated availability fields
    quantity_available INTEGER GENERATED ALWAYS AS (
        quantity_in_stock - quantity_reserved
    ) STORED,
    
    needs_reorder BOOLEAN GENERATED ALWAYS AS (
        quantity_in_stock <= reorder_level
    ) STORED,
    
    stock_status VARCHAR(20) GENERATED ALWAYS AS (
        CASE 
            WHEN quantity_in_stock - quantity_reserved <= 0 THEN 'out_of_stock'
            WHEN quantity_in_stock <= reorder_level THEN 'low_stock'
            WHEN quantity_in_stock > reorder_level * 2 THEN 'overstock'
            ELSE 'normal'
        END
    ) STORED,
    
    status_priority INTEGER GENERATED ALWAYS AS (
        CASE 
            WHEN quantity_in_stock - quantity_reserved <= 0 THEN 1
            WHEN quantity_in_stock <= reorder_level THEN 2
            WHEN quantity_in_stock > reorder_level * 2 THEN 4
            ELSE 3
        END
    ) STORED
);

-- Insert products with varying stock levels
INSERT INTO inventory_status (product_name, quantity_in_stock, reorder_level, quantity_reserved) 
VALUES 
    ('Laptop', 5, 10, 2),
    ('Mouse', 50, 10, 5),
    ('Keyboard', 0, 10, 0),
    ('Monitor', 100, 20, 10);

-- Update stock (generated fields auto-update)
UPDATE inventory_status 
SET quantity_reserved = 8 
WHERE product_name = 'Laptop';

-- Query with generated status fields
SELECT 
    product_name,
    quantity_in_stock,
    quantity_reserved,
    quantity_available,
    needs_reorder,
    stock_status,
    status_priority
FROM inventory_status
ORDER BY status_priority, product_name;
Result:
 product_name | quantity_in_stock | quantity_reserved | quantity_available | needs_reorder | stock_status  | status_priority
--------------+-------------------+-------------------+--------------------+---------------+---------------+-----------------
 Keyboard     |                 0 |                 0 |                  0 | t             | out_of_stock  |               1
 Laptop       |                 5 |                 8 |                 -3 | t             | out_of_stock  |               1
 Mouse        |                50 |                 5 |                 45 | f             | normal        |               3
 Monitor      |               100 |                10 |                 90 | f             | overstock     |               4
(4 rows)
Explanation: Business logic encoded in generated columns: quantity_available = in_stock - reserved (actual sellable quantity), needs_reorder boolean flag for procurement alerts, stock_status categorizes inventory health, status_priority enables sorting by urgency. UPDATE quantity_reserved=8 for Laptop automatically recalculates quantity_available=-3 (oversold!), needs_reorder=true, stock_status='out_of_stock', status_priority=1 (urgent). Generated columns ensure consistent status across application - no risk of code bugs showing "normal" status when actually out of stock. Can add CHECK constraint to prevent negative availability or triggers for automatic alerts. Centralizing business rules in database schema improves reliability.

🎯 Example 5: Search Optimization with Generated Columns

-- Products with generated search column and index
CREATE TABLE products_search (
    product_id SERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    sku VARCHAR(50) NOT NULL,
    product_name VARCHAR(200) NOT NULL,
    category VARCHAR(50),
    brand VARCHAR(50),
    description TEXT,
    
    -- Generated searchable text column
    search_text TEXT GENERATED ALWAYS AS (
        LOWER(
            COALESCE(sku, '') || ' ' ||
            COALESCE(product_name, '') || ' ' ||
            COALESCE(category, '') || ' ' ||
            COALESCE(brand, '') || ' ' ||
            COALESCE(description, '')
        )
    ) STORED
);

-- Create GIN index on generated search column for fast text search
CREATE INDEX idx_products_search_text ON products_search USING GIN(to_tsvector('english', search_text));

-- Insert products
INSERT INTO products_search (sku, product_name, category, brand, description) 
VALUES 
    ('LAP-001', 'Professional Laptop 15 inch', 'Electronics', 'TechPro', 'High-performance laptop for business professionals'),
    ('MOU-001', 'Wireless Gaming Mouse', 'Accessories', 'GameGear', 'Ergonomic mouse with RGB lighting for gamers'),
    ('KEY-001', 'Mechanical Keyboard', 'Accessories', 'TypeMaster', 'Professional mechanical keyboard with Cherry MX switches');

-- Fast full-text search using generated column
SELECT 
    product_id,
    sku,
    product_name,
    category,
    brand
FROM products_search
WHERE to_tsvector('english', search_text) @@ to_tsquery('english', 'professional | gaming')
ORDER BY product_id;

-- Simple LIKE search also benefits from generated column
SELECT 
    product_id,
    product_name,
    brand
FROM products_search
WHERE search_text LIKE '%mechanical%';
Result:
-- Full-text search result:
 product_id |   sku   |        product_name         |   category   |   brand
------------+---------+-----------------------------+--------------+------------
          1 | LAP-001 | Professional Laptop 15 inch | Electronics  | TechPro
          2 | MOU-001 | Wireless Gaming Mouse       | Accessories  | GameGear
          3 | KEY-001 | Mechanical Keyboard         | Accessories  | TypeMaster
(3 rows)

-- LIKE search result:
 product_id |     product_name     |   brand
------------+----------------------+------------
          3 | Mechanical Keyboard  | TypeMaster
(1 row)
Explanation: Generated search_text column concatenates all searchable fields (sku, name, category, brand, description) into single lowercase text field for efficient searching. GIN index on to_tsvector(search_text) enables fast full-text search across all product attributes with single index. Without generated column, would need: separate indexes on each field (more storage, slower), complex WHERE clauses (sku LIKE X OR name LIKE X OR...), or runtime concatenation (slower, not indexable). Generated column + GIN index = single efficient search mechanism. Update any source field (product_name, description, etc.) automatically updates search_text and index. Perfect for: product catalogs, user directories, document management, any multi-field search scenario. Reduces application complexity - database handles search text maintenance.

5.5 Constraints

📖 Section Overview

Constraints are rules enforced by the database to ensure data integrity, validity, and consistency. PostgreSQL supports six constraint types: CHECK (custom validation logic), NOT NULL (required fields), UNIQUE (no duplicates), PRIMARY KEY (unique identifier), FOREIGN KEY (referential integrity), and EXCLUSION (advanced overlap prevention). Constraints prevent invalid data from entering the database, eliminating entire classes of application bugs.

Constraints can be defined at column level (single column) or table level (multiple columns). PostgreSQL validates constraints at INSERT and UPDATE time, rejecting transactions that violate rules. Constraints are named for easy management and provide clear error messages when violations occur. This section covers all constraint types with practical examples of their usage and best practices.

5.5.1 Check Constraints

📖 Full Description

Check Constraints enforce custom validation rules using boolean expressions that must evaluate to TRUE or NULL for each row. CHECK constraints can validate single columns (price > 0) or multiple columns (end_date > start_date), enabling complex business logic at the database level. Any boolean expression is valid, including comparisons, pattern matching, range checks, and function calls with IMMUTABLE functions.

CHECK constraints defined with CHECK (condition) syntax either inline with column definition (column-level) or as separate table constraint (table-level for multi-column rules). PostgreSQL evaluates CHECK constraints during INSERT and UPDATE operations, raising errors when expressions return FALSE. Expressions returning NULL are considered satisfied (NULL represents "unknown" not "false"), which is important for nullable columns.

Common use cases: numeric ranges (age BETWEEN 0 AND 120), enumeration values (status IN ('active', 'inactive', 'pending')), business rules (discount_price <= regular_price), format validation (email matching pattern), logical relationships between columns, preventing impossible states. CHECK constraints are declarative - database automatically enforces rules without application code, preventing invalid data regardless of which application or tool inserts data.

Check Constraints Syntax
-- Column-level CHECK constraint
CREATE TABLE table_name (
    column_name data_type CHECK (condition)
);

-- Named column-level CHECK
CREATE TABLE table_name (
    column_name data_type CONSTRAINT constraint_name CHECK (condition)
);

-- Table-level CHECK (for multi-column constraints)
CREATE TABLE table_name (
    column1 data_type,
    column2 data_type,
    CHECK (condition)
);

-- Named table-level CHECK
CREATE TABLE table_name (
    column1 data_type,
    column2 data_type,
    CONSTRAINT constraint_name CHECK (condition)
);

-- Common CHECK patterns
CREATE TABLE products (
    price NUMERIC(10,2) CHECK (price > 0),
    discount NUMERIC(5,2) CHECK (discount >= 0 AND discount <= 100),
    status VARCHAR(20) CHECK (status IN ('active', 'inactive', 'discontinued')),
    
    -- Multi-column CHECK
    regular_price NUMERIC(10,2),
    sale_price NUMERIC(10,2),
    CONSTRAINT sale_price_check CHECK (sale_price IS NULL OR sale_price <= regular_price)
);

-- Complex CHECK with expression
CREATE TABLE employees (
    birth_date DATE,
    hire_date DATE,
    salary NUMERIC(10,2),
    CONSTRAINT age_check CHECK (hire_date >= birth_date + INTERVAL '16 years'),
    CONSTRAINT salary_check CHECK (salary > 0 AND salary <= 1000000)
);

-- Adding CHECK to existing table
ALTER TABLE table_name 
ADD CONSTRAINT constraint_name CHECK (condition);

-- Dropping CHECK constraint
ALTER TABLE table_name 
DROP CONSTRAINT constraint_name;

Check Constraint Characteristics

Validation Rules:

  • Boolean expression: Must return TRUE, FALSE, or NULL
  • TRUE or NULL passes: Only FALSE values rejected (NULL considered unknown/satisfied)
  • IMMUTABLE functions only: Cannot use functions that depend on external state (CURRENT_DATE okay if marked IMMUTABLE)
  • No subqueries: Cannot reference other tables (use triggers or foreign keys instead)
  • Same-row only: Can only reference columns in current row being validated

Common Patterns:

  • Numeric ranges: CHECK (age >= 0 AND age <= 120), CHECK (price > 0)
  • Enumeration values: CHECK (status IN ('pending', 'approved', 'rejected'))
  • String patterns: CHECK (email ~* '^[A-Za-z0-9._%+-]+@[A-Za-z0-9.-]+\.[A-Za-z]{2,}$')
  • String length: CHECK (LENGTH(password) >= 8)
  • Logical relationships: CHECK (end_date >= start_date)
  • Conditional validation: CHECK (status = 'shipped' OR tracking_number IS NULL)

Multi-Column Checks:

  • Date ranges: CHECK (checkout_date >= checkin_date)
  • Price relationships: CHECK (sale_price <= regular_price)
  • Conditional requirements: CHECK (has_discount = false OR discount_amount > 0)
  • Mutual exclusivity: CHECK ((method_a IS NULL) != (method_b IS NULL))

Best Practices:

  • Name constraints: Use descriptive names for clear error messages
  • Simple expressions: Complex logic better in triggers or application
  • Consider NULL: Remember NULL in expression makes entire expression NULL (passes check)
  • Performance: CHECK evaluated on every INSERT/UPDATE, keep expressions fast
  • Document intent: Use comments to explain business rules behind checks

🎯 Example 1: Basic Check Constraints - Numeric Ranges

-- Product table with numeric CHECK constraints
CREATE TABLE products_validated (
    product_id SERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    product_name VARCHAR(100) NOT NULL,
    price NUMERIC(10,2) CHECK (price > 0),
    discount_percentage NUMERIC(5,2) CHECK (discount_percentage >= 0 AND discount_percentage <= 100),
    stock_quantity INTEGER CHECK (stock_quantity >= 0),
    weight_kg NUMERIC(8,3) CHECK (weight_kg > 0),
    rating NUMERIC(3,2) CONSTRAINT rating_range CHECK (rating >= 1.0 AND rating <= 5.0)
);

-- Valid inserts
INSERT INTO products_validated (product_name, price, discount_percentage, stock_quantity, weight_kg, rating) 
VALUES 
    ('Laptop', 1299.99, 15, 50, 2.5, 4.5),
    ('Mouse', 29.99, 0, 100, 0.15, 4.8);

-- Try invalid price (will fail)
-- INSERT INTO products_validated (product_name, price, stock_quantity, weight_kg) 
-- VALUES ('Invalid Product', -100, 10, 1.0);
-- ERROR: new row violates check constraint "products_validated_price_check"

-- Try invalid discount (will fail)
-- INSERT INTO products_validated (product_name, price, discount_percentage, stock_quantity, weight_kg) 
-- VALUES ('Invalid Discount', 100, 150, 10, 1.0);
-- ERROR: new row violates check constraint "products_validated_discount_percentage_check"

-- Query valid products
SELECT product_name, price, discount_percentage, stock_quantity, rating 
FROM products_validated;
Result:
 product_name |  price  | discount_percentage | stock_quantity | rating
--------------+---------+---------------------+----------------+--------
 Laptop       | 1299.99 |               15.00 |             50 |   4.50
 Mouse        |   29.99 |                0.00 |            100 |   4.80
(2 rows)
Explanation: CHECK constraints validate data at insert time: price > 0 prevents negative prices, discount_percentage BETWEEN 0 AND 100 ensures valid percentages, stock_quantity >= 0 prevents negative inventory, rating BETWEEN 1.0 AND 5.0 enforces valid rating range. Named constraint (CONSTRAINT rating_range) provides meaningful error message. Attempting INSERT with price=-100 fails with clear error identifying which constraint violated. CHECK constraints are first line of defense against invalid data - even if application has bugs, database rejects bad data.

🎯 Example 2: Enumeration Values and String Patterns

-- Orders with status enumeration and email validation
CREATE TABLE orders_constrained (
    order_id SERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    customer_email VARCHAR(100) CHECK (customer_email ~* '^[A-Za-z0-9._%+-]+@[A-Za-z0-9.-]+\.[A-Za-z]{2,}$'),
    status VARCHAR(20) CONSTRAINT status_values CHECK (status IN ('pending', 'processing', 'shipped', 'delivered', 'cancelled')),
    payment_method VARCHAR(20) CHECK (payment_method IN ('credit_card', 'debit_card', 'paypal', 'bank_transfer')),
    total_amount NUMERIC(10,2) CHECK (total_amount > 0),
    created_at TIMESTAMP DEFAULT CURRENT_TIMESTAMP
);

-- Valid inserts
INSERT INTO orders_constrained (customer_email, status, payment_method, total_amount) 
VALUES 
    ('john@example.com', 'pending', 'credit_card', 299.99),
    ('jane@company.org', 'shipped', 'paypal', 149.50);

-- Try invalid email (will fail)
-- INSERT INTO orders_constrained (customer_email, status, payment_method, total_amount) 
-- VALUES ('invalid-email', 'pending', 'credit_card', 100);
-- ERROR: new row violates check constraint "orders_constrained_customer_email_check"

-- Try invalid status (will fail)
-- INSERT INTO orders_constrained (customer_email, status, payment_method, total_amount) 
-- VALUES ('test@example.com', 'invalid_status', 'credit_card', 100);
-- ERROR: new row violates check constraint "status_values"

-- Query orders
SELECT order_id, customer_email, status, payment_method, total_amount 
FROM orders_constrained;
Result:
 order_id |   customer_email   |  status  | payment_method | total_amount
----------+--------------------+----------+----------------+--------------
        1 | john@example.com   | pending  | credit_card    |       299.99
        2 | jane@company.org   | shipped  | paypal         |       149.50
(2 rows)
Explanation: CHECK constraints for data quality: email regex validation (~* operator for case-insensitive pattern matching) ensures valid email format, status IN (...) limits values to predefined set (prevents typos like 'shiped' or 'pendng'), payment_method IN ensures only supported payment types accepted. Enumeration constraints via CHECK provide type-safety without creating custom ENUM types. Regex patterns validate format: email, phone numbers, postal codes, SKUs. Status enumeration prevents invalid states that would break application logic expecting specific values.

🎯 Example 3: Multi-Column Check Constraints

-- Events table with date range validation
CREATE TABLE events (
    event_id SERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    event_name VARCHAR(100) NOT NULL,
    start_date TIMESTAMP NOT NULL,
    end_date TIMESTAMP NOT NULL,
    regular_price NUMERIC(10,2) NOT NULL,
    early_bird_price NUMERIC(10,2),
    max_capacity INTEGER,
    registered_count INTEGER DEFAULT 0,
    
    -- Multi-column CHECK constraints
    CONSTRAINT date_range_check CHECK (end_date > start_date),
    CONSTRAINT pricing_check CHECK (early_bird_price IS NULL OR early_bird_price < regular_price),
    CONSTRAINT capacity_check CHECK (registered_count <= max_capacity),
    CONSTRAINT positive_prices CHECK (regular_price > 0 AND (early_bird_price IS NULL OR early_bird_price > 0))
);

-- Valid inserts
INSERT INTO events (event_name, start_date, end_date, regular_price, early_bird_price, max_capacity, registered_count) 
VALUES 
    ('Tech Conference 2026', '2026-06-01 09:00', '2026-06-03 17:00', 499.99, 399.99, 500, 150),
    ('Workshop: SQL Basics', '2026-07-15 10:00', '2026-07-15 16:00', 99.99, NULL, 30, 20);

-- Try invalid date range (will fail)
-- INSERT INTO events (event_name, start_date, end_date, regular_price, max_capacity) 
-- VALUES ('Invalid Event', '2026-06-10', '2026-06-01', 100, 50);
-- ERROR: new row violates check constraint "date_range_check"

-- Try invalid pricing (early_bird > regular)
-- INSERT INTO events (event_name, start_date, end_date, regular_price, early_bird_price, max_capacity) 
-- VALUES ('Bad Pricing', '2026-06-01', '2026-06-02', 100, 150, 50);
-- ERROR: new row violates check constraint "pricing_check"

-- Update that would violate capacity
-- UPDATE events SET registered_count = 600 WHERE event_id = 1;
-- ERROR: new row violates check constraint "capacity_check"

-- Query events
SELECT 
    event_name,
    TO_CHAR(start_date, 'YYYY-MM-DD') AS start,
    TO_CHAR(end_date, 'YYYY-MM-DD') AS end,
    regular_price,
    early_bird_price,
    registered_count || '/' || max_capacity AS registration
FROM events;
Result:
      event_name       |   start    |    end     | regular_price | early_bird_price | registration
-----------------------+------------+------------+---------------+------------------+--------------
 Tech Conference 2026  | 2026-06-01 | 2026-06-03 |        499.99 |           399.99 | 150/500
 Workshop: SQL Basics  | 2026-07-15 | 2026-07-15 |         99.99 |             NULL | 20/30
(2 rows)
Explanation: Multi-column CHECK constraints enforce relationships: date_range_check ensures end_date > start_date (prevents backwards events), pricing_check validates early_bird_price < regular_price when provided (NULL early_bird allowed), capacity_check prevents overbooking (registered_count <= max_capacity). Note pricing_check handles NULL: early_bird_price IS NULL OR early_bird_price < regular_price - allows NULL or valid price. Without NULL handling, constraint would reject NULL early_bird prices. Multi-column checks ideal for: date ranges, price relationships, capacity limits, conditional requirements, data consistency rules.

🎯 Example 4: Complex Business Rules with CHECK

-- Shipping table with complex validation rules
CREATE TABLE shipment_rates (
    rate_id SERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    origin_country VARCHAR(2) NOT NULL,
    destination_country VARCHAR(2) NOT NULL,
    weight_min_kg NUMERIC(8,2) NOT NULL,
    weight_max_kg NUMERIC(8,2) NOT NULL,
    base_rate NUMERIC(10,2) NOT NULL,
    fuel_surcharge_pct NUMERIC(5,2) DEFAULT 0,
    remote_area_fee NUMERIC(10,2) DEFAULT 0,
    
    -- Complex business rule checks
    CONSTRAINT weight_range_check CHECK (weight_max_kg > weight_min_kg),
    CONSTRAINT weight_positive CHECK (weight_min_kg >= 0),
    CONSTRAINT rate_positive CHECK (base_rate > 0),
    CONSTRAINT surcharge_range CHECK (fuel_surcharge_pct >= 0 AND fuel_surcharge_pct <= 100),
    CONSTRAINT different_countries CHECK (origin_country != destination_country OR origin_country = destination_country),
    CONSTRAINT country_code_format CHECK (
        LENGTH(origin_country) = 2 AND 
        LENGTH(destination_country) = 2 AND
        origin_country = UPPER(origin_country) AND 
        destination_country = UPPER(destination_country)
    )
);

-- Valid inserts
INSERT INTO shipment_rates (origin_country, destination_country, weight_min_kg, weight_max_kg, base_rate, fuel_surcharge_pct, remote_area_fee) 
VALUES 
    ('US', 'CA', 0, 5, 15.99, 8.5, 0),
    ('US', 'GB', 5, 20, 45.99, 12.0, 10.00),
    ('US', 'AU', 0, 10, 65.99, 15.0, 25.00);

-- Try invalid weight range
-- INSERT INTO shipment_rates (origin_country, destination_country, weight_min_kg, weight_max_kg, base_rate) 
-- VALUES ('US', 'CA', 10, 5, 20);
-- ERROR: new row violates check constraint "weight_range_check"

-- Try invalid country code (lowercase)
-- INSERT INTO shipment_rates (origin_country, destination_country, weight_min_kg, weight_max_kg, base_rate) 
-- VALUES ('us', 'ca', 0, 5, 15);
-- ERROR: new row violates check constraint "country_code_format"

-- Query rates with calculated totals
SELECT 
    origin_country || '→' || destination_country AS route,
    weight_min_kg || '-' || weight_max_kg || 'kg' AS weight_range,
    base_rate,
    fuel_surcharge_pct || '%' AS fuel_surcharge,
    base_rate * (1 + fuel_surcharge_pct/100) + remote_area_fee AS total_estimate
FROM shipment_rates
ORDER BY rate_id;
Result:
 route  | weight_range | base_rate | fuel_surcharge | total_estimate
--------+--------------+-----------+----------------+----------------
 US→CA  | 0-5kg        |     15.99 | 8.5%           |          17.35
 US→GB  | 5-20kg       |     45.99 | 12.0%          |          61.51
 US→AU  | 0-10kg       |     65.99 | 15.0%          |         100.89
(3 rows)
Explanation: Complex validation with multiple CHECK constraints: weight_range_check ensures max > min (prevents invalid ranges like 10-5kg), country_code_format validates 2-character uppercase codes (prevents 'usa' or 'Us'), surcharge_range ensures percentage between 0-100, rate_positive prevents negative prices. Multiple simple CHECK constraints often clearer than single complex one. Constraints compose: must satisfy ALL checks. Invalid country code 'us' fails even if all other fields valid. Business rules encoded at schema level - any application inserting data must follow rules, preventing invalid rate configurations.

🎯 Example 5: Conditional and NULL-Aware Checks

-- Products with conditional validation
CREATE TABLE products_conditional (
    product_id SERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    product_name VARCHAR(100) NOT NULL,
    has_discount BOOLEAN NOT NULL DEFAULT false,
    regular_price NUMERIC(10,2) NOT NULL CHECK (regular_price > 0),
    discount_price NUMERIC(10,2),
    is_physical BOOLEAN NOT NULL DEFAULT true,
    weight_kg NUMERIC(8,3),
    requires_shipping BOOLEAN NOT NULL DEFAULT true,
    shipping_fee NUMERIC(10,2),
    
    -- Conditional CHECK constraints
    CONSTRAINT discount_price_check CHECK (
        (has_discount = false AND discount_price IS NULL) OR
        (has_discount = true AND discount_price IS NOT NULL AND discount_price < regular_price)
    ),
    
    CONSTRAINT weight_check CHECK (
        (is_physical = false) OR 
        (is_physical = true AND weight_kg IS NOT NULL AND weight_kg > 0)
    ),
    
    CONSTRAINT shipping_fee_check CHECK (
        (requires_shipping = false AND shipping_fee IS NULL) OR
        (requires_shipping = true AND shipping_fee >= 0)
    )
);

-- Valid inserts
INSERT INTO products_conditional (product_name, has_discount, regular_price, discount_price, is_physical, weight_kg, requires_shipping, shipping_fee) 
VALUES 
    ('Laptop', true, 1299.99, 999.99, true, 2.5, true, 15.00),
    ('Software License', false, 99.99, NULL, false, NULL, false, NULL),
    ('E-Book', false, 19.99, NULL, false, NULL, false, NULL);

-- Try discount without price (will fail)
-- INSERT INTO products_conditional (product_name, has_discount, regular_price, is_physical, weight_kg, requires_shipping) 
-- VALUES ('Invalid', true, 100, true, 1, false);
-- ERROR: new row violates check constraint "discount_price_check"

-- Try physical product without weight (will fail)
-- INSERT INTO products_conditional (product_name, has_discount, regular_price, is_physical, requires_shipping) 
-- VALUES ('Invalid Physical', false, 100, true, false);
-- ERROR: new row violates check constraint "weight_check"

-- Query products
SELECT 
    product_name,
    has_discount,
    regular_price,
    discount_price,
    is_physical,
    weight_kg,
    requires_shipping,
    shipping_fee
FROM products_conditional
ORDER BY product_id;
Result:
   product_name    | has_discount | regular_price | discount_price | is_physical | weight_kg | requires_shipping | shipping_fee
-------------------+--------------+---------------+----------------+-------------+-----------+-------------------+--------------
 Laptop            | t            |       1299.99 |         999.99 | t           |      2.50 | t                 |        15.00
 Software License  | f            |         99.99 |           NULL | f           |      NULL | f                 |         NULL
 E-Book            | f            |         19.99 |           NULL | f           |      NULL | f                 |         NULL
(3 rows)
Explanation: Conditional CHECK constraints enforce "if-then" business rules: discount_price_check says "if has_discount=true, then discount_price must be NOT NULL and less than regular_price", weight_check says "if is_physical=true, then weight_kg must be NOT NULL and positive", shipping_fee_check says "if requires_shipping=true, then shipping_fee must be provided". Conditional logic using (condition_A) OR (condition_B AND requirements) pattern. Physical products must have weight, digital products (is_physical=false) can have NULL weight. Discounted products must provide discount_price. Enforces data consistency based on product type - prevents impossible states like physical product with no weight or discounted product without discount price.
